
==================== SCENE 1 ====================
The warning siren on the breakwater hadn’t even finished its second sweep when Lena hit the first iron grate of the causeway and felt it tremble under her boots. Wind shoved the air flat and salty against her teeth. On the marsh to her right, the sedges lay down in one synchronized bow, and out beyond the rock teeth, the squall line marched in a blue-black wall. It would swallow the horizon in a minute. The tide had turned and was already fingering the hollows in the road.

“Two minutes, thirty,” she said into the recorder clipped at her collarbone. “Surface slick, crosswind from the southeast, first shelf flooding at the dip before the knuckle bend. Moving.”

She kept the duffel tight to her ribs and ran. The causeway kinked around an anchoring boulder they called the knuckle; beyond it, the island rose with its crown of black firs and a collar of stone walls. She watched her feet, because the ancient concrete was sloped and pocked, and seaweed had already drawn a green thread along the edges. Spray blew up and stung one eye. The wind ate sound; even her breath seemed to go sideways.

Boot soles slapped: nine, ten, eleven, the count she ran when she needed to forget the distance and stay present, and then the wind dropped for a heartbeat as she cut into the lee of the knuckle. In that bubble, she could hear water whispering under the road like a throat clearing. The next gust tried to pluck the cap from her head. She reached the last set of grates, the ones the gulls used as a stage in calmer weather, and hopped the widening spread of a puddle that was already flexing like a muscle under pressure.

“On island. Three minutes fifty-two,” she said, the words tasting of copper and salt.

The gate on the island side was open—someone had left it tied back with frayed yacht line—and the gravel beyond it had been raked recently. Lena felt the small recognition of clean edges settle in her spine: someone on this rock understood what order did for a place. She jogged up the slope, the duffel thumping her hip. The wind made a noise like a flag tearing around the chimney tops of the main house, a long, low clap. Fir boughs sawed at each other.

She took the path that skinned close along the outbuildings. The generator shed sat behind the potting house, a corrugated thing with a new padlock and a patch of blackened ground beneath the exhaust. She stopped at its door, breathing hard, then fumbled the key ring out of her pocket. The tag on the brass key was stamped GENSHD in an unlovely font, but it turned cleanly in the lock. Inside, the air was metallic and unfinished, bright with the smell of fuel and cut plywood.

She set the duffel down on the workbench and snapped on the bare bulb. “Generator shed. Unit: Cummins back-up, 35 kilowatts, brand new belt. Fuel: diesel, 250-gallon tank, sight glass at three-quarters. Control panel shows AUTO engaged. Alarms normal.”

She ran her hand along the conduit. The conduit ran out of the panel into a tidy ladder to the ceiling, through a weatherproof junction into the skin of the shed, and out toward the main house. She traced the bundle with her eyes to another branch out to a small secondary box that someone had labeled MED in black marker.

“Secondary branch to med wing subpanel. Surge protector installed. Good.”

She opened the control box. The auto-start schedule was a simple wheel on a digital display. She tapped the menu button and read off the intervals.

“Auto-test Tuesday at 0900. Auto-start on mains drop after 8 seconds. Cool down five minutes. Transfer switch time 12 seconds. Restart attempts: three. Ambient sensor on. Manual start operational. Okay.”

Her finger hovered over the buttons and she did a quick check of the fuel lines—a squeeze and a look, no cracks—and then shut it all up again. She photographed each screen with her phone, slid her notebook across the bench and made block letters, her hand steady because this was the part of the work she trusted more than any other: naming things, pinning them down.

Outside, the gusts had turned from sheets to fists. Rain came in a sudden pellet scatter against the shed door and then paused, the way a cat tests a door. Lena pulled her hood up and stepped back into the wild gray. She took the path along the retaining wall that led toward the back of the house where the service entry sat: a double door with all paint scoured thin near the handle from years of pushing. To the right, the funicular house clung to the slope like a mussel shell, its rails dropping down to the boathouse in a clean steel chute.

She went for the funicular first. The tower was hinged shut with a hasp the size of her palm. The key turned stubbornly, as if offended to be used, and then loosened. Inside, the lift car was parked at the top, a little wooden platform with a low gate, and the drum with cable over-slung like the white back of a whale. The control panel had two buttons—UP and DOWN—and a keyed lockout. Lena put her ear to the drum case and listened: the faint tick and thermal creak of a system that had sat in the sun all afternoon.

“Funicular house,” she said into the recorder, the wind setting a low harmonica note at the door crack. “Rails: galvanized, recent. Car: one-ton capacity plate. Cable: braided steel, 5/8-inch. Drum brake: electric with mechanical fail-safe. Locks: padlock on tower, keyed lockout at control. Bottom lock—unknown, to be checked at boathouse.”

She squatted to photograph the plate and ran her finger over the keyway. No grit, no brine creep. Lower down, the rail bed had a set of expansion joints with new bolts. She liked that the nuts gleamed and the washers sat even.

She pressed the DOWN button just long enough to hear the brake release—an honest, heavy clack—then released it, relocked the control with the key and pocketed it. The car stayed put, as it should. On the wall, a laminated sheet listed the usage policy and the emergency stop procedure. She snapped a picture, read the fine print.

“Auto-shutoff on overrun, emergency stop at both landings,” she said. “No remote. Good.”

The rain came again, this time committed. It rattled the tin roof like thrown rice. She shut the tower door and jogged for the service entry, the gravel jumping under her boots. The door had a stubborn bottom seam that caught, then gave; she shouldered it open and stepped into the smells: the old domestic heart, warm dust, a thread of bleach, something sweet under it like canned fruit.

The service corridor was a narrow artery with half a dozen doors on either side and a runner that had been beaten flat by a century of feet. On the left, the bell board stood on the wall like a commandment: a field of little windows with the room names typed on typewriter slips beneath them. Above the windows, a grid of enamel numbers. Beside it, a rack of levers with brass handles the color of tea spilled on linen. A woolly rope hung down from the ceiling bell, the sort of antique affectation that here was not a joke.

“Bell board,” she said. “Glass windows intact. From top left: Library, Morning Room, Blue Bedroom, Green Bedroom, Nursery, Kitchen, Pantry, Servants’ Hall, East Porch, West Porch. Secondary line for boathouse exists—tag says ‘Pier.’”

She set the duffel on the runner and took out a small LED headlamp, a strung-together set of jumper clips, her multimeter, and a spool of red string. Wind rattled the windowpanes like teeth. She pressed the string to the wall with painter’s tape, laying lines from the board to the corners, making the logic visible. She eased the board’s face open, careful not to stress the old hinges, and found a marvel of brass spring arms and cotton-insulated wires braided in neat pairs. The colors had paled to tea and bone, but the organization was clean: every room circuit bound in twine, labeled with varnished cards.

“Voltage is low. These are battery bells,” she said, testing across the feed. She followed the main wires down the wall to a wooden box mounted at knee height. It opened to reveal a rack of three big dry cells in series and a small, luminous ball-bearing switch that acted as a mercury tilt.

“Dry cell pack reads five-point-one volts. Good. Battery box labeled April. Replacement schedule quarterly.”

She jotted the color code and leaned in to blow a spider out of the way of her view. Then she traced the wire for the nursery down to a small brass screw terminal and gave it a tiny, satisfying tug. Solid.

“Nursery line is direct to the own circuit, no piggy. Bells rearranged sometime in the last decade. Label tape is new compared to others.”

She set the meter to continuity and clipped to the nursery line, then walked with the other lead down the corridor, following the wire with her eyes up into the ceiling, across the house frame—she could picture it even without a diagram. A doormat squeaked under her heel; she shifted so it would stop and then, because old habits were the discipline, took her phone and photographed each junction, each fray, each little solder pea where an old hand had done the job simply.

She tested the Kitchen line, the Green Bedroom, the Pier. Each gave a clean tone through the meter. When she tapped the brass arm for the Morning Room with her knuckle, the little window flipped to show a red slash, then reset with a click when she pulled the lever. She wrote: “Indicators responsive. Cancel levers stiff but operational. Lubricate with graphite.”

Rain pounded. The house creaked and answered like a ship. Lena pushed her hood back and listened to how the wind turned the corners of the building. The tide would be at the causeway now, but she’d made it. She could feel the grin she didn’t bother to show, a tucked thing.

On the far end of the corridor, a door went down five steps to what had once been the scullery and now served as the receiving room for deliveries. To the right of that, under a framed sheet of fire exits, a low iron door crouched halfway up the wall with a hinged lid and a chipped enamel frame. Coal had once poured through that mouth and into a stone bin that now held buckets and a rusted lawn roller. Ash ghosts still smudged the hinges. The chute itself, based on the angles, ran from the outside wall straight into the foundation and up at a shallow throat to the old furnace.

She crouched and opened the iron lid. The throat hadn’t been used in decades, but a recent coat of black paint kept it from looking like a relic. The hinge wanted oil; it rasped.

“Coal chute access point: service corridor scullery wall. Inner fall: eight feet to bin. Exterior hatch likely under east porch stairs. Hinges: iron, paint new. Lock tab present but no padlock installed. Draft: none.”

She held her hand into the chute. No pull, no damp breath. It had been sealed well down in the old furnace room when the boiler was converted. She shut it gently and took another picture. On the opposite wall, smaller hatches showed plumbing clean-outs; she traced them on her map with quick, square lines.

She went back to the bell board, ran the string for the Pier and the East Porch down the corridor so she could remember how they left the house, then lifted the runner and looked at the wire trunk in the baseboard: it was wood, with a ledger board that a good carpenter had planed smooth. No rodents, no chew marks.

“Main trunk sound. Humidity in corridor: forty-six percent,” she said after reading the little sensor clipped to her jacket. “This is the right place to keep the board.”

Her ears were attuned to any machine noise, and she heard it now: the first far-off ping and thrum of the generator doing its automatic run-up check—the Tuesday schedule was not today; she’d looked. This was wind noise making its way into the shed and rattling something, or something else entirely, but she didn’t dwell. She marked the schedule in her travel journal with a box: TUES 0900 TEST, AUTO ON OUTAGE +8s, TRANSFER +12s, COOLDOWN 5m.

She stepped into the pantry to check the pier line from the other end. A little brass push button sat on a square of Bakelite near the back door, under the hat hooks, stamped PIER in careful capitals. She pressed it with her knuckle and walked back. The bell board had a neat red slash in the Pier window.

“Good,” she said aloud. “Wiring’s honest.”

The rain backed off and then surged again. It was a rain that had opinions. Lena left the bell board open while she followed the corridor back to the service yard. The east porch steps were slick and green; she took them sideways. Beneath, where the stone footings kept a low space like a mouth under a lip, lay the exterior coal hatch: an iron panel flush with the gravel, with a loop handle on top. She jammed her fingers under and it came up grudgingly. The underside smelled of oil paint and old carbon.

“Coal chute exterior: under east porch, three feet inside the post line. Hatch weight moderate. No lock present. Seal in good shape.”

She shut it and wiped her hand on her jeans. The porch windchimes were tangled and trying to become knots. On the far side, the funicular rails gleamed in the spit of rain, and below, the boathouse roof wore a seaweed fringe where scraps had blown up and stuck.

She trotted along the gravel to the boathouse landing. The path tipped down through wind-wet ferns, the ground underfoot sprung with water. At the bottom, the funicular’s lower station had its own small lean-to with a sliding bolt. The bolt moved with a scrape that said it had been used enough. Inside, the bottom gate had a padlock that matched the upper: two keys, one for each. She tried the key; it turned.

“Funicular lower lock present and keyed. Safety interlock intact. Gate spring catches—and catches hard.”

She put her hand on the rail and felt the cold of it go into her palm. The cable under the car was crisp, no fur of rust, no kink. She checked the emergency pull—there was a red cord and a small plaque. She recorded the text, because that was what you did with systems: you made their language yours.

Back up the hill, the house framed the sky, and the squall had pushed closer, so close now that the world over the marsh had gone to a sheet of slate. She retraced her steps at a jog, tasting rain again, and ducked back into the service corridor, glad of its stable air. The bulb over the bell board hummed softly. She wrenched open the battery box again and wrote the date on a strip of tape and slapped it on: TODAY—CHECKED.

The corridor had a quiet that wasn’t empty; it was packed with memory and utility, the two things that made places like this run. She rolled her shoulders, letting all the small muscles settle. She had the bones of it now—the generator’s schedule, the tension of the bell wires, the ways up and down—and that was what she’d come for: enough to stand on and name.

She began making a clean map, elbows in, pen tip fine. She drew the causeway and the arrow of the wind, the shape of the house with its internal arteries, the string paths she’d laid, the small squares of access like the coal chute and the hatched rectangles of the funicular gates. She marked the generator shed with a block and wrote the schedule in the margin. She drew tiny bells on the board standard and labeled the circuits with their typewriter names. When she looked up, the light had gone a deeper shade and the rain was angering itself into thick ropes, and the boards around the old dumbwaiter shivered with it.

She clasped the recorder in her hand, a habit more than a need, and said, “Baseline: set. Map: generated. Systems responsive. Proceed to rooms.”

Then she shut the bell board with a gentle click and taped the strings in place for later reference. The wind pushed at the door at the far end of the corridor in a petulant way, and the runner rippled slightly at its insistence. She watched it, just long enough to understand its small threat, then turned and moved deeper into the service hall, into the next job.


==================== SCENE 2 ====================
The landing outside the nursery was a bright, bare throat of hallway open to the west. The first gust-front rain needled the long window at the end, fanned, and thudded as if the glass were a drumhead. The light in that weather went to medical—flat and poreless—so the old varnish on the wainscot glowed without warmth. Lena stood, a little damp at the collar, and looked at the door.

The nursery door had been sealed in the old way: linen tape lapped across the jamb in an X, pressed into two buttons of vermilion wax like a private coin. There was a hairline of powdered plaster where the painter had cut in the trim years ago and never wiped it clean. On the wax: an impression neat as stamped cartilage—E.V.—the V pinched sharp and honest. She bent, slow and close, and let her eyes find the boring comforts: no crazing in the wax, no tug on the tape, the dust on the hinge-knuckles continuous, the slotted screws unflared. The keyhole had a cotton plug in it—somebody with a conscience had done that, a sense of what moves through keyways.

She touched the recorder’s clip on her pocket to make sure the mic was clear. “Position: nursery landing, west hall. Weather: gust-front precip, wind WSW, fifteen plus. Seal intact: tape and wax unbroken; keyway plugged with cotton. Visual on hinge hardware: no disturbance.”

The bell rang from inside the door.

Not the showy brass in the hall—this was a wire strike: a small, intent ting, like a pin tapped off a rim. The sound had that particular hollowness that came through lath and plaster; it started an answering tremor further down the wall and then settled.

Lena’s head inclined before she told it to. She got one hand flat to the paint, fingers spread in the cold registry of a sealed thing, and said into the recorder, “Event: nursery bell single strike. Mark time: eighteen-oh-six and forty-two—corroborate against board log.”

She swallowed. “Hello?” she added, not to the recorder now, to the door itself, which made no sense, but the word came out shaped and practical. “Hello, the nursery?”

The door held its varnish smell and the oily ghost of old toys, or maybe that was something she was supplying from other houses. The rain freighted on the window hard enough to shim something inside the wall, a minute tick tick tick.

Something pale appeared flush to the threshold line.

It pushed out slowly, as if with the heel of a hand, a corner first, then a whole, stooping rectangle. A note: cheap folded stock, the kind with a deckle that isn’t real. It halted with its short edge just lipping the gap under the door, a whisker of paper exposed to the hall.

Protocol stood up in her like a person. She did not touch the door. She did not touch the wax. The white of the note looked hungry for fingerprints. The draft coming up the stair bent one corner and slapped it back.

“Item apparent,” she said, the voice a little lower to make the words calm. “Folded paper emerging under threshold. Legible: front address line reads ‘Oliver.’ Ink: graphite or soft-lead; test later.”

She set her bag down on the runner, kneeling, the bones in her knees doing their proper knock. The bag snapped open in a learned sequence: nitrile gloves in a zip, paper evidence sleeves, glassine, a thin stainless rule, a polymer shim the size of a bookmark she kept for under-door resurrections. She pulled gloves with her teeth and the lip of her wrist, not touching gloving to glove.

“You’re okay,” she told the landing. “Nobody’s going to breathe on you.”

She slid the polymer under the exposed corner with an angler’s patience. No push against the door. She let the plastic take the note’s belly and then lifted so the paper arched and carried itself along the floor to her side. The graphite scrawl flashed in the light when the angle caught its tiny sheen. The corner had a fleck of lint on it, and she felt an ordinary joy: lint was time and place. The second the paper cleared the gap, the draft wagged the linen tape a hair and the wax dapples sent back their dull cherry grin, unchanged.

“Contact minimized,” she said. “No contact with seal or threshold. Paper retrieved by shim.”

She laid the note on a clean card, photographed the front—OLIVER in a lazy confident hand that was not a child’s—then rotated the card with the forceps, breathing through her nose so she wouldn’t talk moisture onto it. No seal on the fold, just a crush. She photographed the edges, the fold tension, the tiny oils where somebody’s fingers had made a darker crescent. The back had a faint smear like a pencil’s heel had grazed it, the kind of graphite transfer that got caught in the tooth.

“Potential latent and graphite trace present,” she said. “Will bag to preserve.”

She didn’t unfold it; she didn’t need the words inside yet. She slid it into a glassine sleeve, then into a paper evidence envelope with her printed label—Case: Breakwater House—Item: Note ‘OLIVER’—Loc: Nursery threshold, west hall—Date/Time: 18:07 approximate—Handler: L.A.—Method: Shim retrieval, gloves. She sealed the gummed flap and bridged the seams with evidence tape, her initials crossed, a small grid of redundancy.

“Oliver,” she tried, once more to the door, as if speaking the name could press into the wood and find a person elegant enough to write it like that. “If you’re in there, I’m logging the time,” she said, and to the recorder, “Audible time-signal per app: eighteen-oh-seven, second mark.”

The bell didn’t ring again. Air composition on a landing doesn’t change because of a piece of paper, but she could feel the corridor had a slightly different density now, as if it had been asked to carry more information than before.

She palmed the envelope and went to the window at the end of the hall, because angles mattered. The rain made sheets that had edges; between those edges the salt marsh came up like hammered metal. She took a photograph outward for weather record, then two back toward the door to fix position. At the skirting by the jamb there was a millimeter of graphite dust she only saw once she’d looked through the lens; it might have come from inside, or off the heel of the pencil that wrote the note. She capped the camera and taped off the skirting with a little square of blue painter’s tape from her pocket, writing: DON’T CLEAN—TRACE.

From the bag: a small tent marker with the printed figure 1. She set it by the spot where the note had lain, photographed again.

She knelt to inspect the underside of the door. There was no light through the gap except for what the hall gave it. The paint line down there was unbroken. On the threshold plate, a faint rubric of shoe edge from years ago; none of it smudged, none new. She would have liked to swab the brass for skin oils, but that wasn’t the job yet; don’t get greedy, her brain said, the good, clipped version of her own voice.

She looked once, briefly, down the stairs toward the service corridor as if the bell board were a person she could see by looking. “Board saw that hit,” she said to the recorder. “Circuit tag should be standard—nursery line—check for wobble mark at eighteen-oh-six to eighteen-oh-eight window. Log entry to be made within same.”

She pulled the small index-card log she kept in the pocket behind the recorder, wrote her line anyway as if writing it close to the event mattered to time: 18:06:42—Nursery bell single strike—note passed under door—item secured—L.A. She photographed the card next to the envelope, the door in background, linking them in one frame. It was not beautiful, but it would be admissible where it needed to be.

The landing had refurbished itself to silence. Rain went to a lower register, a thrum. The note, stamped into her palm by the stiff paper, was a reality as plain as the taped wax buttons. She slid it into the rigid mailer in the bag and tucked the bag back into itself. The door still smelled like old glaze and cloth.

“Protocol intact,” she said, almost under her breath, then to the recorder so it would be official: “No tamper with sealed room. Evidence preserved in paper/glassine to maintain latent and graphite. Time established; will reconcile with bell board log on return.”

She stood and felt the house take that small weight adjustment through the floor beams. She put one gloved knuckle to the door—not touching the tape or the wax—and knocked in the soft way you use in a hospital you don’t know. “I’m leaving this landing now,” she said, absurdly, kindly, because language was a system and she would make it hers. “Thank you.”

Then she stepped back, retook the window photographs because she liked a neat pair, and started for the stair. When she turned the corner, the tape X on the nursery door made its clean shadow like two bones aligned on an X-ray. Downstairs, the corridor would have the bell’s small swing in it, the physical proof she liked best, but for this landing, and this door, she had the white rectangle on a label inside a narrow envelope, and the time of the strike sitting hard in her pocket, ready to be matched.


==================== SCENE 3 ====================
The study had the old kind of quiet that meant paper, not screens: ranges of labeled spines, a clock that ticked instead of chimed, desks that had cords routed neatly through brass grommets. Rain sat in the window glass like static. The estate phone—a tan box with a row of ivory buttons—sat square in the center of the blotter with a hand-lettered line map taped to the underside of the clear. Lines: House, Shore, Garage, Kitchen, Breakwater Clinic, Counsel/Kersey.

She slid the recorder from her coat, set it on the blotter with the mic angled toward the phone, and pressed the red dot. “Study,” she said for the audio tag, because she liked things to begin in a way that could be found. “Day one, eighteen forty-one. Estate phone outgoing. Calling Kersey direct.” She checked the note she’d written in the car that morning when the first squall hadn’t started yet: Kersey’s number, two alternates, extension for the intake coordinator if he routed her.

She pressed the button for the outside line. The dial tone was a robust, domestic hum. She dialed and watched the numbered holes spin back.

One ring. Two. Three. The click when someone picks up in a quiet office is identifiable anywhere; the line goes personal, less abstract.

“Kersey.”

“Lena Ansel,” she said, same clipped tone, a match. “Central client—Eleanor—spoke this morning. I’m on the island, on-site. I need a time certain for the competency evaluation. End of week gives me what I need to verify observations and make sure everyone’s in the room.”

He didn’t greet. That was fine; he was good at his job and, like her, it was presently evening. Paper-crunching could be heard on his end; the slight rasp of a stapled packet being shifted. “We’re not end-of-week,” he said. “The court likes Wednesday. Our clinician is free on Wednesday, maybe Thursday. We move pretty fast when it’s about decision-making and medical proxies.”

“Wednesday conflicts with access windows out here,” she said. No apology, just the fact. “We need the causeway to be open and the household settled. You require a baseline environment. Friday.”

“I require,” he said, but there was humor in it, or just a practiced elasticity, “a date that doesn’t have the judge calling me on Saturday to ask why I didn’t push this through. I can put you on for Thursday at thirteen-hundred. That’s me compromising.”

Lena looked down at the blotter. Someone had faintly indented a chessboard into the leather in one quadrant; rook squares and numbered ranks you couldn’t see unless the lamp fell just right. “Out here thirteen-hundred Thursday is good for tide but bad for power,” she said. “Generator maintenance mid-day this week. They cut it over and back to test. Also, household members are scattered Thursday. Friday gives maximum compliance.” She scanned the folder tabs nearest at hand without reading the contents: Eleanor / durable power—executed, Caleb—Work Orders—Lower Grounds, Estate Maintenance—Generator Svc. She could feel each label as an edge she could use if she had to.

“Lena,” he said, sounding like he’d said her first name without looking to see if he’d said it before, “if I put Friday on for this you are guaranteeing me there will be no surprises.”

“Nothing in my control will be a surprise,” she said. “I’ll run pre-interviews and collect signatures on attendance. You’ll have your witness list in twenty-four hours, your packets back forty-eight hours before the evaluation, and Eleanor will have eaten and rested. Friday gives us a full window to make sure she isn’t dragged in from a storm. You want clean conditions. So do I.”

The desk phone had small, polite lights over each line. The one for Shore hummed to itself and then stilled. She angled the planner that lay open beneath the lamp; someone had filled out a few squares with neat, narrow writing. There was a check mark on Friday noon: Bread. The simple domesticity of it pleased her: agendas that had to meet in the middle.

He paged through something. The sound came with the minute whine of a cheap plastic ring binder under strain. “Dr. Vale can do Friday morning.”

“Morning tolerances here are skewed by the light. Make her afternoon if you can. Thirteen-hundred or fourteen-hundred is ideal.”

“Fix me parameters, Ms. Ansel. I need numbers.”

“Friday thirteen-hundred evaluation start, ninety-minute window slotted. I’ll have them gathered half an hour prior. You’ll be on video conference, yes?”

He made a short noise like a nod. “We can do video if clinic bandwidth holds. Mobile hotspot backup.”

“Great. Put her in with redundancy. You’ll push the notices tonight?”

“If I schedule it, yes.” His voice had a way of placing each piece on the board where it belonged and then pausing. “I want acknowledgment from each household participant. No later than tomorrow noon.”

“You’ll have them.” She turned a file over and slid out a blank memo page from the back of the folder with lined watermarks: House/Study—Communications. “Send me the standard notice with the rights advisement and the attending physician’s statement to accompany. Email and fax.”

“Fax?” There was mild pity in it.

“We’ve got a line,” she said, looking at the phone’s base where a second jack had a coiled cord leading to a dust-covered machine under the side table, flat and beige with a skewed lid. “And email. Success redundancy.”

“Okay.” He ticked a pen. On his end she could hear the light pat of his tie tapping buttons as he leaned across a desk. “Friday. Thirteen-hundred start. Dr. Marilyn Vale, competency evaluation, on-site with you. I’ll put notice to all parties with a list of required participants: primary subject, household members with vested interests and current proxies—if any—plus a neutral from our office. I’ll be on, as counsel; also a court liaison if necessary.”

“Make attendance mandatory,” Lena said. “Not recommended. Require signatures for any excused absences. Standard language on stabilization of status quo until then, including no asset transfers, no changes to medical directives and no removal of fixtures pertinent to care. I’m printing your notice and posting it on the study door and at the kitchen board.”

Silence made a small white space on the line. He understood what she meant by fixtures; he respected domestic words, too. “You’re not enforcement,” he said mildly.

“I am the person who will call you if anyone tries to hand a pen to an exhausted elder at nine at night,” she said, no heat, just policy. “Friday takes the pressure off everybody. It becomes the day things get discussed in daylight.”

“You’re about to quote me chapter and verse, aren’t you.”

“Not if you agree.” She was already writing it anyway on the memo paper: Friday 1300—Eval—Status Quo hold—No transfers—No directive changes—No removals. She boxed the words and found the pushpin tin.

Kersey exhaled close to the receiver and let what sounded like a chuckle escape. “I’ll put the hold language—limited—until Friday thirteen-hundred, subject to extension upon initial report. Your wording on the notices stays neutral.”

“Neutral is fine. I’ll do the enforcing tone,” she said. “One thing more: I need to record you saying the date and time. Standard. I like the clean edge.”

“That your recorder I’m hearing?” he asked, suddenly curious in a way that had nothing to do with who she was and everything to do with his interest in process.

“It is,” she said.

“All right. This is Kersey. Friday, one p.m., competency evaluation for Eleanor Latham at the residence, Dr. Vale attending, all parties notified; hold on transfers and directives in place through start time. Notices going out tonight. That satisfy your clean edge?”

“It does,” she said. “Thank you.” She slid her index card log out from behind the recorder and wrote the line just as he had said it, with his name first, the cadence as in a formal transcript. She photographed the card flat on the blotter with the phone in frame, just for the file. “Do you want me to queue anything else?”

“Brief me in the morning on any situational changes. I don’t need color; I need bullet points.” He shifted—she could hear a chair’s coasters roll over a carpet seam. “Household is tense, yes?”

“It’s a household handling a lot of information at once,” she said, which was careful and accurate.

“Right.” Paper to paper as he clipped the file. “Your email, again.”

She read it out slowly. Rain made a little hiss on the eaves, steady. The clock ticked. In the hall beyond, someone’s step went past and back again: not listening, just moving because houses do that.

“You’ll have the packet in twenty.” He paused. “Anything else you want to ask me while you’ve got me? I’m not surprised you want Friday. I don’t mind Friday. We like schedules; we can put the court on to the cadence.”

“I want you to speak to Ivy and get her acknowledgment on the hold,” Lena said, eyes on the bookshelf where three rings of brass labeled Estates / Correspondence / Ages stood in a row. “She’s going to say she’s the one who needs things moved along.”

“Ivy often needs things moved along,” he said with a distant tone that didn’t judge, just placed. “I’ll call her after we hang up. You reach her too.”

“I will,” Lena said. She could feel the shape of the next calls in her hand; she was already stacking them like file tabs. “You’ll send me Dr. Vale’s prior eval list and the questionnaire outline?”

“I’ll push you our standard, yes.” He tapped keys again. “And Lena—”

“Yes?”

“Don’t let them turn this into a referendum on feelings. Keep it factual. The judge hates mess. He likes his lines straight.”

“I do lines fine,” she said.

He laughed, quick and administrative, and it clicked into the tone of the night. “Friday it is. You’ll hear the ping when the packet lands.”

The line went to the small sound a phone makes when a body leaves it: not empty, but absent. She pressed Stop on the recorder and held it a second longer, letting the air in the study put its own period at the end.

She turned immediately to the fax under the side table, knelt to read the curled instruction label, pressed the Power, and listened to the way old electronics crisp into life. She pulled fresh paper from the cabinet—ream end crisp and smelled faintly like paste. She checked the email on the computer in the corner—the screen took a moment to wake, found the network, and opened to a browser default that had never changed. Within six minutes, the ping came; a neat attachment, the subject in Kersey’s exact language: Notice: Friday 1300—Competency Evaluation—Latham.

She printed two, then three more for the doors. The notice was four paragraphs of balanced legalese: the statement of purpose, the rights advisement, the list of participants, the hold on transfers and directives, the punctuality requirement. It had dates in the margin like a calendar format. She blue-penned her own initials on the bottom right of the first and then stamped the time in block numerals from the little black-dyed roller on the corner of the desk: 18:57.

She clipped one to the study door, pinning the upper right corner with a brass tack so it sat square. The lamp above the hall bench threw a gold wash that made the text look briefly like an announcement of a recital. She posted one on the kitchen board under Bread and Milk and changed—neatly—the notation for Friday noon to Bread delivery—hold until after eval, adding an arrow so it read clean. She sent a photo of each posting to Kersey’s email and then, one by one, message requests to the household group thread with the photo and the line: Notice posted per counsel—Friday 1300—attendance required—hold in effect.

Her phone vibrated; Ivy’s reply went in clipped with a dot of exasperation: Noted. We’ll be ready. Then the three dots, then: Is Dr. Vale neutral?

“Neutral as any clinician,” Lena texted back. “Fair and by the book.”

Caleb didn’t respond. Mara responded with a thumbs-up and a short, Thank you.

She walked back to the study and sat, the smell of old cigarettes ghosted into the desk’s drawers rising for a second when she slid one open to find tape. She taped down the corners of the memo she’d written for the household to see if they came in here: Friday 1300. Mandatory. Hold. No exceptions. Her handwriting was even; she could look at it later and know she had been steady.

Then she dialed Kersey again and left the crisp voicemail he liked: “Notice posted, time stamped, photo sent. Text acknowledgments received from two of four household members so far. I’ll follow with calls to secure the rest. I’ll be in the study for the next half hour for any incoming.”

She set the phone back and started a new index card: 18:59—Kersey sched. Friday 1300—Dr. Vale—Hold—Notices posted. She slipped the card in the pocket behind the recorder. There was a pleasure in those small boxes checked, the minor administrations that made people feel they were standing on a floor that wouldn’t tilt.

On the shelves sat the house ledger books, bound in cloth. She pulled the one labeled Correspondence and opened it to the top, where an inserted lined page had slots for date and subject. She wrote: Friday Eval—Notice and Hold—Posted—Kersey confirmed. Under it she left a wide margin for the acknowledgments she would collect tonight: initials in a row with times.

The kitchen’s old intercom popped, a soft tongue-click of ancient wiring. Somebody pressed the button and let it go. The study’s panel crackled. Then nothing. Houses make noises like that; they test their own systems when the weather changes.

The rain steadied. The clock clicked over to the next minute. She put a second pen in her breast pocket, because pens were what worked when other things didn’t, and because carrying two felt like having contingency plans that fit in your hand.

Her phone lit again. Kersey had forwarded the judge’s clerk on the email chain and added three words at the top like a stamp: Set for Friday.

She sent back the one thing that needed saying: Understood.

Understood meant the board was drawn. It also meant tight lines, measured moves. The space between now and thirteen-hundred on Friday was hers to use with everyone’s eyes on the clock and her notice pinned straight on two doors. She liked that: the shape of a task bounded by a day and an hour, the way time could be made to behave if you named it and then wrote it down.


==================== SCENE 4 ====================
Mara’s office had a window that was more a porthole than a view, a circle of glass thick enough to green the light. The panes showed a section of gravel path scabbed with lichen and the stripe of the kitchen garden wall. Steam blew across it now and then from a kettle somewhere, or else it was just the morning breathing of a damp house.

The key press sat opposite the desk, a wooden cabinet with a shallow slatted front like a hymn book case. Brass hooks ran in columns, each hook hung with a leather fob and a stamped tin tag, some tags new and bright, some sunk to a dark buttery color. Chalk scribbles crowded the frame: pantry outer, blue shed padlock, north bulkhead, generator cage, greenhouse door, bell cabinet, nursery corridor—X’d. The X was sharp and recent, white grit still clinging to the wood.

Mara stood at the desk with a ledger open at an angle. Her thumb was pressed on a ribbon marker as if it could shut the whole book if she just held the ribbon tight enough. She wore her cardigan like armor—a gray one with a pearl button missing at the wrist—and had a pen behind one ear that matched the dark streak at her hairline. A crocheted holder pinned to the wall held envelopes and a paper clip chain like a silvery vine. On the desk, a ceramic cylinder—tourist gift, lighthouse painted on it—held hard pencils and a stub of wax crayon. It smelled faintly of coal polish and floor soap.

“You’re early,” she said without looking up.

“It’s nine,” Lena said. “You said after breakfast.”

“I said after breakfast,” Mara repeated, and glanced to the office doorway where a tray with an emptied coffee cup and a filigree of crumbs sat. “Now it’s after breakfast.”

Lena set her own notebook down. She had worn the same jacket as yesterday but had put a scarf on over it, more out of habit than weather; the office wasn’t cold, just cool in a way that never warmed. She placed her hand near the edge of the ledger so Mara could see it and then didn’t touch anything.

“I need the keys,” Lena said. “Exterior sheds, generator cage, bell cabinet, boathouse, funicular lock, nursery corridor. And I’ll need the log books: key issue record, arrival/departure, the repair log for the generator and the bell pulls. And you have a housekeeping register for the upper floor; I’ll need that too.”

Mara lifted one eyebrow in a way that might have been taught by someone older, an expression that implied continuity, a line of women who had not been stampeded by an urgent tone. “That’s a long list.”

“Comprehensive,” Lena said.

“That too.” Mara closed the ledger, took her thumb off the ribbon, set both palms on it. “You have a paper from Mr. Kersey?”

“He’s the one that set the competency evaluation for Friday. He’s acting on the court’s direction. He has engaged me to assess the security of the premises until then. I’ve posted the notice.”

Mara turned her head slightly. The office intercom—a little bakelite mouthpiece mounted on the wall—had a slip of paper stuck in the frame that said: speak slow, it’s old. She looked at that, not at Lena, and then she reached back and slid a small tin box out from the bookcase. When she set it on the desk, it clicked with the sound of keys in metal.

“Family instruction is that access remains as it is,” she said. “No expansion.”

“Family instruction?” Lena repeated.

Mara nodded once.

“Whose instruction?” Lena asked. “Ivy?”

“I said family,” Mara said.

“The family employs you,” Lena said. “Kersey represents the estate and the court. You know where that puts me.”

“I know where it puts me,” Mara said. “And that’s here, at this desk, to keep the house running and the things in it from wandering.”

“The boathouse floor is tracked with kelp,” Lena said, even though she hadn’t meant to show her hand that way this early. “The funicular lock’s been scored. I need to audit access.”

“Then audit,” Mara said. “Within the family’s instruction.”

Lena studied the key press. The fobs were numbered and named. One hook was empty and had been weighted with a spare bit of chain to make it hang like the others and not announce its emptiness. A label under it said: nursery corridor—key retained. Another had two keys, the lower one with a blue ribbon of tape on it that said duplicate in a thin hand, probably Mara’s.

“What are you willing to issue,” Lena said.

Mara took a breath and went to the cabinet. She carried the tin box with her and set it on the wire shelf under the key press. She reached up, paused, reached again, and decided. The pause wasn’t hesitation; it was a little ritual of choosing that felt old, as if she had learned to announce every movement to her own sense of order.

“Blue shed and generator cage,” she said, plucking the fobs. “Greenhouse. Bell cabinet—front only.”

“What do you mean front only,” Lena said.

“The rear panel takes another key,” Mara said. “It’s for rewiring and such. We don’t issue that. We call Mr. Pearce.”

“Mr. Pearce the electrician,” Lena said.

“That’s his trade,” Mara said.

“And the funicular lock,” Lena said.

Mara didn’t reach for it. “Funicular is under service. Mr. Pearce again. No issue.”

“It’s a padlock,” Lena said.

“It’s on a system,” Mara said.

“The nursery corridor,” Lena said.

“Retained,” Mara said.

“I’ll need the log books then,” Lena said. “The key issue record, housekeeping register, maintenance logs.”

Mara took the ledger, flipped it open to a page with ruled columns. The handwriting was partly hers, partly an older looped script that had been crabbed at the end as if the hand had failed and someone else had taken over. “Key issue recorded here. I note to whom and when and what for.” She tapped the page with one nail.

“Let me have it,” Lena said.

“No,” Mara said. “You can ask. I’ll read.”

“Housekeeping register,” Lena said.

“Mrs. Linton’s,” Mara said. “Not for circulation.”

“I’m not circulating. I am auditing.”

“Same difference,” Mara said.

Lena’s jaw worked once. “Mara, walk me through this. You’re going to keep the nursery corridor key, the funicular key, and the rear panel for the bell cabinet. You’re going to issue me greenhouse, generator cage, blue shed, and the front of the bell cabinet. And you’ll read me entries from the key issue book, but I don’t get to take it.”

“That’s right,” Mara said.

“I need the arrival/departure book,” Lena said.

Mara didn’t move.

“Then I’ll take photographs here,” Lena said evenly. “Of the pages you read. Of the keys you issue. Of the cabinet as it stands.”

Mara’s mouth thinned. “Photographs don’t go out. You keep them to yourself until Friday.”

“Noted,” Lena said, though she had not agreed.

Mara reached for the tin box and opened it. She took out a stamped token and knocked it once on the tin to bring out a hollow pew sound. “Sign these out,” she said. She slid a small board with a sheet clipped to it across the desk. The heading was precise: Key Issue — Date — Name — Purpose — Return Signature — Time Returned. “And I’ll make my own note.”

Lena took the pen from her breast pocket, didn't touch the one behind Mara's ear, and wrote: generator cage, blue shed, greenhouse, bell cabinet front. Under purpose she wrote: security audit per Kersey email. She put her initials and the time.

Mara leaned over and wrote: issued under family instruction—restricted. Her handwriting was simpler than the old script but it made a clear example of the word restricted, as if she liked how firm it was.

“You know the state of the causeway,” Lena said. “You know what that means if the house isn’t secure.”

“I know the causeway,” Mara said, and her mouth softened for the first time. “I’ve crossed it wet and dry. I’ve counted gulls on it to keep from looking at the drop.”

“Then issue the funicular lock,” Lena said.

Mara shook her head almost kindly now. “If it is under service, it is under service,” she said, quoting someone; Lena guessed the phrase belonged to Mrs. Linton or a manual.

“The logs,” Lena said again.

Mara put her hand over the ledger as if a bird might try to take it. “I’ll read you what you need when you need it,” she said.

“That will slow me down,” Lena said.

“That’s correct,” Mara said.

They stood there for a quiet too long to be comfortable and too short to be called a standoff. Down the corridor, someone laughed once, then stopped, as if they’d remembered where they were. A clock ticked somewhere with a reluctance like a drip.

“Tell me about last night,” Lena said. “Key movements after nine. Read them.”

Mara pulled the ledger to her and looked over the columns. “Nine-oh-five,” she said. “Jonah brought back the east terrace key. He took it for the furniture covers. Nine-twenty. Caleb signed for the north bulkhead key.”

“North bulkhead,” Lena said. “Purpose.”

“Water,” Mara said. “He wrote ‘water.’” She turned the ledger a little so Lena could see the scrawl: water.

“And return,” Lena said.

“Nine fifty-eight,” Mara said. “Returned.”

“Any access to the generator cage,” Lena said.

“No,” Mara said.

“The bell cabinet,” Lena said.

“No,” Mara said.

“The boathouse,” Lena said.

Mara ran her finger down. “No sign-out,” she said. “No one came to me for it.”

“Then someone had a key not recorded,” Lena said.

“Or the door wasn’t locked in the first place,” Mara said.

“Was it,” Lena said.

Mara closed the ledger again. “We lock what we can when we can,” she said. “You know how it is in a breathing house. People move through it. They get a message or a call or a pot boils over and they think of one case and forget another.”

“I don’t audit thoughts,” Lena said. “I audit keys.”

“Then audit,” Mara said again. “Within instruction.”

“Who wrote the X under ‘nursery corridor’ on the press,” Lena said.

“I did,” Mara said. “Yesterday.”

“To remind yourself,” Lena said.

“To remind anyone who looked,” Mara said, and lifted her chin. “Including you.”

Lena put her hand out. “Give me what you’re willing to give.”

Mara worked the hooks. The greenhouse key was a brass spear that felt old and square-toothed; the generator cage key was modern, milled; the blue shed padlock key had a nick like a shark bite on the shoulder; the bell cabinet front had a little porcelain disc wired to the fob as if it had been annotated in a second system. As Mara placed each in Lena’s palm, the fobs tapped against one another like a low iron chime.

“This one is sticky,” Mara said, flicking the generator key with one nail. “You’ll need to lift the handle.”

“Noted,” Lena said. She attached the fobs to her own split ring one by one. The extra weight dragged on her jacket pocket when she slid the ring into it. It felt like mass and permission.

“Now the register,” Lena said, nodding at the ledger.

Mara smiled with her mouth, not her eyes, and opened again to the key page. “You can photograph this page,” she said. “Not the rest.”

“And the housekeeping register for the upper floor,” Lena said.

“Not for circulation,” Mara said. “You can ask for a date and I’ll look.”

“Yesterday morning,” Lena said.

Mara went to another book. It was clothbound in faded blue, labeled by hand: rooms / tasks / initials. She put one finger in the top and one in the bottom and sniffed once as if to smell the time in it. She turned to yesterday and ran a nail down the column. “Upper corridor, dust, J. Nursery corridor—barred,” she said. “Mrs. Linton’s hand. No access.”

“Mrs. Linton is with us today?” Lena asked.

“No,” Mara said. “Off today.”

“Then I need the arrival/departure book,” Lena said again, to see if she could pry it.

Mara took the thin black book from the top of the file stack, fanned it, then set it aside. “Staff sign in,” she said. “Family don’t.”

“Staff,” Lena said.

“We’ll read it together,” Mara said. She turned to yesterday and read the names: “Jonah, eight-fifteen. Ivy, no. Ms. Ivy doesn’t sign. Caleb, eight-oh-three. Me, seven-thirty-six. Cass, no; not here yet. Kersey, not on the staff page.”

“Visitors,” Lena said.

Mara tapped the book closed. “Family instruction,” she said again.

“Fine,” Lena said, and took her phone out. She photographed the key press as it was, the empty hook with the weighted chain, the X chalk on the nursery label. She photographed the key sign-out page she had initialed, making sure the time was in shot. She did not ask permission for the second photograph; she just steadied herself and took it. The phone chirped. Mara didn’t reach for it, didn’t protest, only watched.

“Who holds the nursery key,” Lena said.

“That is retained,” Mara said.

“By you,” Lena said.

“By the house,” Mara said.

“In whose person,” Lena said.

Mara shook her head, not as denial but as the end of a line. “This is older than me,” she said.

“Everything is,” Lena said, and slid the phone back into her pocket.

They stood again in that narrow quiet. The kettle somewhere sighed and then clicked. The porthole window showed a gull hop up onto the garden wall and stare in, as if the office were a television and it wanted to see the weather report. Its eye flicked once and it opened its beak and closed it without any sound.

“You can put a note to Mr. Kersey,” Mara said at last. “If you like. I’ll carry it with the other mail.”

“I’ll call him,” Lena said.

“That too,” Mara said.

Lena nodded at the tin box. “What’s in there,” she said.

“Coins,” Mara said. “For the ferry that doesn’t run anymore.” It was said with no nostalgia, only the factual tone of an inventory. “And string.”

“For what,” Lena said.

“Everything else,” Mara said, and looked at the ceramic lighthouse cup with the pencils in it as if the pencils and the string were cousins.

“Right,” Lena said, and tapped the desk twice with the back of her knuckles as if to knock on the scene’s surface. “I’ll get on. If you have cause to alter your instruction—if the family alters it—let me know immediately.”

“Of course,” Mara said, which was a way of saying not likely.

“One more thing,” Lena said. “When Jonah returned the terrace key at nine-oh-five, did he say anything about the sealed nursery?”

Mara’s face didn’t move at the word sealed, though her eyes did a little softening outward that said she noticed how people dressed their words. “He said the covers blew,” she said. “That’s it.”

“Caleb,” Lena said. “At nine twenty with north bulkhead for ‘water.’ Did he bring anything back other than the key.”

“No,” Mara said.

“And Ivy,” Lena said.

“Ivy doesn’t sign,” Mara said. “You can find her and ask her yourself.”

“I will,” Lena said.

Mara slid the ledger under her elbow again and tucked the ribbon back between pages. The act looked like a nurse taking a pulse.

“Thank you,” Lena said.

“You’ll bring those keys back,” Mara said, not as a question.

“Yes,” Lena said.

“At noon,” Mara said.

“Noon is in two hours,” Lena said.

“That’s right,” Mara said.

“Make it one,” Lena said, to see if she could flex it.

“Noon,” Mara said.

“The generator needs to be inspected before noon,” Lena said.

“Then get there,” Mara said evenly.

Lena didn’t smile. She let the coolness sit between them the way a pane sits between rooms. She turned to the key press and read the labels once more, not because she needed to but because people appreciate being seen doing the rituals their opponents understand. She glanced at the chalk X, at the little porcelain disc on the bell cabinet key, and at the one missing pearl on Mara’s cuff. She left her card on the desk with her name and number and the block letters of the firm, as banal and blunt as a rubber stamp.

“Call if the family’s instruction changes,” she said again.

Mara looked at the card as if it were a piece to be put in the correct slot of the crocheted holder. “I’ll call if anything changes,” she said, which was not the same thing.

The corridor outside smelled like damp books. Lena walked it, the new weight in her pocket knocking lightly with each step. Behind her, Mara’s office settled back into the counting of what came and went, and the porthole glass kept greening the morning. When she reached the corner, the intercom in the wall gave that soft tongue-click again, the sound old wires make when someone somewhere touches a button and decides against the rest. She kept going.


==================== SCENE 5 ====================

The upper corridor to the nursery wing felt more like a bridge than a hall—narrow, paneled, with light traveling a long way before it found anything soft to land on. The windows along the seawall side were milky with salt and pitted at the corners, and the air had that gauzy, limewash smell old rooms give off when no one is sleeping in them. Lena walked with her hand on the rail, the ring of keys bumping her hip, her breath leaving small patches on the glass as she checked each door label. NURSERY—SEA, NURSERY—YARD, PLAY ROOM, NIGHT NURSE. The paint on the transoms had been feathered and taped, the edges tidied against drafts. One lens of wire-reinforced glass showed a pair of stacked cots in silhouette, upside down on a worktable, and a rolled rug bound in twine thick as a thumb.

A rolling scaffold sat halfway down, one wheel chocked. A pair of blue gloves drooped from the crossbar like pet birds. Beyond that, the corridor narrowed further, and a heavy fire door stood open. Lena raised her phone and shot wide, then detail—hinges, thresholds, the bracketed intercom box with its red eye cloudy and dead.

“You can’t go up here.”

The voice came flat and close. Caleb pushed the scaffold two inches to make himself a wall with it, his boot flattening the chock and then replacing it. He kept one hand on the rail of the thing and one hand lazy on his belt, thumb hooked at the pouch where he kept hex keys and a pencil. He wore a dust mask folded down under his chin like a second mouth and a fleck of plaster in his eyebrow. He did not look winded from moving the rig; he looked arranged.

“You’re already ‘up here,’” Lena said, easy. “I’ll be quiet.”

“Not about quiet,” he said. “About policy.”

“Whose policy?”

“House safety,” he said. “Insurance. Liability. Everyone’s name who would be on a paper if you fall and split your face on a hinge.”

Lena stepped to look over his shoulder. He shifted the scaffold half an inch inward. It squealed like a toy on the waxed floor.

“Nursery corridor is closed for stabilization,” he said. “Not open to the public.”

“I’m not the public,” Lena said.

“You’re not maintenance,” he said, and tapped the laminated tag clipped to his shirt with the house crest and his name block-printed in black. “No entry.”

“The ring came from here yesterday,” Lena said, looking at the seam where the fire door met the jam. There were three stickers like tiny flags: asbestos, lead, child safety. All had been initialed by the same hand. “Mara’s put me on the generator and the bell board. If the bell rang from in there, I need to see the line run.”

He glanced at the keys on her hip and then at her face, as if to see whether she understood the difference between keys and permission. “Those are for what she wrote down,” he said. “Not this.”

He made the scaffold into a piece of furniture. You could sit on it and eat a sandwich. You could not pass it without asking him to move.

Lena took out her little notebook, a thin calfskin thing with a rubber band. She didn’t open it yet. She looked down the corridor again, slow, letting him see she was cataloging, not challenging. The window casings were deeper here, benches almost, with brass latches and an exterior scupper visible at the far end, a gutter leading rain away into a carved lion mouth. The floor pitched the slightest bit toward sea, as if the whole house remembered being a ship.

“What’s in stabilization?” she said. “Crown molding? Lead paint? Loose balusters?”

“All of that and the idea of all of that,” he said. “Also: old glass. Unrated. Shatters like ice.”

“Then why is the fire door open,” she said.

“Air,” he said. “And because I’m here.”

He set the brake on the scaffold with his foot. It made a locking click that felt like a period.

“You’re making a liability argument,” Lena said. “My counter is time. I don’t need an hour. I need thirty seconds to run a line of sight from the pull to the bell board and take a photo.”

“There’s no pull,” he said. “It’s capped.”

“Who capped it,” she said.

“Maintenance,” he said, pointing to the crest again with the faked patience people use to soften refusal. “Me.”

“When,” she said.

“Last year when they sealed,” he said. “The cords are off. The holes are stuffed. There’s tape on the inside. Done.”

She put the notebook against the rail and drew a rectangle—the corridor—two windows on one side, three doors on the other, a fire door hinge icon, the scaffold an X. She marked the intercom box and the wiring conduit that ran like a vein along the top molding.

Caleb watched the pencil move. “If you draw another way in, that’s still a no,” he said. “Policy applies to all approaches.”

“They usually forget one,” Lena said, tracing the line of the conduit as if she were simply doodling. “Sometimes two.”

“Not now,” he said. “It’s all mapped.”

She let her gaze shift to the ceiling. The crown here carried a milled slot for picture rails. Above that, a series of grilles—painted many times—hid the old hot-air chases. Someone had pulled one grille recently; the screws were bright. She drew another little square on her map and scribbled “return” beside it.

“Those feeds carry dust,” he said. “And maybe old mouse. You want hantavirus, take your chances somewhere else.”

“Noted,” she said, and put a dot by it, not a star.

His hand went to the scaffold rail again, an unconscious little reclaiming of the wedge.

“Look,” she said, keeping her voice conversational. “I don’t need to disturb a thing. I don’t need to touch a latch. But I do need to know whether the feed to the bell runs this way or drops to the subpanel and crosses under the nursery. If it crosses under, the generator clock is out of sync with the bell pulses and the signature would be wrong.”

“None of that is my business,” he said. “My business is to keep this barricade where it is until Mara says otherwise and until the risk sheet gets a signature I can read.”

“Mara said the generator needs to be inspected before noon,” Lena said. “And this sits between me and the case.”

“This sits between you and the nursery,” he said. “You don’t need the nursery to see a generator.”

He had a point, just not the one she wanted. She took a photograph of the conduit with her phone, then the intercom plate. The phone made a tiny fake shutter sound that seemed too cheerful for the room.

“You can take all the pictures you want from that side,” he said. “You can also go back downstairs and do your generator thing like a good citizen and we’ll all go to lunch and no one needs to explain a scratched face to a client.”

“Who’s the client,” she said, because hearing what people called other people always sorted them faster than any résumé.

“Family,” he said. “The house. Whoever you write on your invoices, I don’t need to know. But when Mara says no, I put a thing in the way and then it’s a no you can see.”

“Did Mara tell you to stop me specifically,” Lena said.

“She told me to enforce the safety plan,” he said. “She told me you have keys and a list and she told me what’s on the list. The nursery isn’t on it.”

He looked at her keys again, and she could almost hear the silent math he was doing about copying, about young staff with ideas about getting into places and taking selfies with glass mobiles.

“Caleb,” she said. “Have you been up in that room today.”

He knocked his knuckles on the scaffold plank. “No.”

“Yesterday.”

He shrugged, the kind that means yes but not in a way that can be used. “I went to the bulkhead.”

“You bring anything back,” she said.

“Tools,” he said. “A wrench. A rag full of oil, because I’m not an animal. Do I need to list my pockets for you.”

“No,” Lena said. She put the notebook down on the windowsill and took one step left. He matched it. He did not square off; he turned just enough to keep his shoulder between her and the seam of the door.

“Tell you what,” he said. “You stand there and look until you feel like you’ve seen it and then you do something else. I’m going to keep this right here and we can waste the day that way if that’s what you need.”

“Do the windows open,” she asked, not moving her eyes from the grill.

“They open if I open them,” he said. “Which I don’t.”

She measured the window depth with her fingertips—two and a half of her fingers came to the sash. Outside, the ledge had a lip. Below, you could hear the chuff of a wave slapping the rock like a splayed, open hand. She put a small hash on her drawing to remind her about the exterior ledge and the gap between wings.

Caleb smiled without warmth. “If you climb out a window I will call it in before you get both feet on the sill.”

“Noted,” she said again.

The intercom on the wall made its soft wire-click. The same as downstairs. Someone somewhere touching a button and petting the line. Lena watched Caleb not watch it. He had that maintenance man’s hearing, tuned to pumps and motors and lift chains, the whole soundboard of the house, and he was choosing not to react in front of her. That was an answer too.

“You’re making this about rules,” she said. “I’m making it about minutes and not wasting them.”

“I like rules,” he said. “Minutes I get paid for even when we disagree. That’s the secret of rules.”

She drew a thin box where she thought the servants’ stair would land—back of the night nurse room, offset by half a flight, running down toward the laundry. Houses like this had redundancies. Ladders tucked behind panels, doors that looked like linen presses and weren’t. She penciled in a dotted line to guess an unopened door behind one of the bookcases farther down—a bookcase on casters with the wheels barely visible in the shadow. She marked it with a triangle. If it was what it looked like, it hinged toward the nursery wall.

He followed her eyes and set his boot gently against the caster, like you rest a hand on a dog’s head. “No,” he said. “Not that either.”

“How long have you been here,” she said.

“Long enough to know where the bad steps are,” he said.

“And how many are there.”

“Enough.”

She couldn’t make him say more than he wanted to say, and she didn’t need to. She needed the map and the habit of the place. She took one more photo of the conduit, then of the floor where the old bell wire would once have run—little nail heads grayed under varnish, a faded line like a vein showing where it had been.

“Okay,” she said, sliding the notebook back into her pocket. “I hear you.”

“Finally,” he said, more a puff of air than triumph.

“I’ll go do the generator thing like a good citizen,” she said, and met his eyes for the first time since he’d moved the scaffold. His pupils were small; he had been out on the roof or some bright place. His knuckles were scraped. His smell was rope and salt and metal filings. Everything about him said capable and set.

“Keys back at noon,” he said, stepping back a fraction just to ease the tension a body gets from standing on one leg ready to block. “Mara said.”

“She did,” Lena said. “I keep my promises.”

She gave the corridor another slow look, as if memorizing a face in bad light. The bookcase with wheels. The return air grille with bright screws. The service stair corner with the scuff that said foot traffic had once been a secret. The exterior scupper and the lion mouth on the drain. The intercom that clicked without anyone claiming it. She put two fingers on the cold brass of the window latch and took them away again, polite as if you avoided touching someone’s sleeve.

“What happens if the family’s instruction changes,” she asked.

“I move the scaffold,” he said. “That’s all.”

“And nothing else,” she said.

“That’s all,” he said.

They let the quiet sit where it wanted to. The sea thumped. The scaffold ticked as the plank settled. When she turned away, she did it without sighing, without looking back like a child being denied a candy counter. She walked the length of the corridor to the last window and looked at the angle of the roofline outside, drew a quick arrow for the route along the parapet that would cut behind the chimneys, and wrote: roof—parapet—east light—nursery—sea. She wrote: linen press? and a question mark after the word hatch, and she put the time at the top of the page: 11:18.

At the fire door, she paused and tapped its hinge with her knuckle. Solid. Warm from the sun on the other side. She left him with a nod like you give a crossing guard who has someone else’s children to consider and took the stairs down, counting the risers with her foot and the clicks of the intercom with her ear. The corridor to the nursery stayed exactly as closed as a scaffold, a shoulder, and a policy could make it. She filed the routes and the little bright screws in the part of her mind that holds other people’s house maps. Then she went to find the next piece that didn’t need anybody’s permission.


==================== SCENE 6 ====================
The lower landing sat in the lee of the rocks where the wind lost its edge and came back as salt and iron. The funicular car was moored to the rails like a patient animal: green paint scabbed, brass numerals, the control box locked with a puck lock and a length of chain that had turned the same color as the gull droppings on the beam. The cable hummed in its groove with the sea’s vibration. Lena stood with her notebook braced against the rail post, marking the slack of the tide and the shiver of the car when the swell hit the pilings.

The porter in the navy sweater was sweeping shells out of the slot where the rail met the landing. He wore a cap with the island’s crest faded to a pale ring. His broom rasped. From up-slope, the signal bell did a single, uncertain ping like someone testing a spoon.

Jonah came down from the incline house carrying a coil of line over one shoulder. He had the set look she’d already filed: competent and watchful. He gave her a glance that registered, adjusted, and then slid away to the cable housing as if the rust there were more urgent than any human face. His knuckles were scabbed. His forearms shined with oil that no amount of salt soap would take out.

Lena put the time in her notes and drew the little square for the lock. The chain and lock looked ordinary until the light hit just right and made a white comma of raw metal by the shackle. She stepped closer, not touching. The scoring ran in fresh arcs around the brass, chatter marks where a blade had skated and bit again. The padlock body had a thumbprint of clean metal where someone had braced it. The smell was faint and wrong—cut steel, not old salt.

“Porter,” she said, and kept her voice the way you spoke in a museum when you wanted the guard to look without shouting. “Has anyone opened this since this morning?”

The porter straightened, hand on the broom handle like it was a cane. “Not me,” he said. “Key’s up at the office. We run on schedule. You want the up-car, you put your name in. Orders from Ms. Mara.”

“Anyone been asking to run off hours?”

He tipped his chin toward the path. “Ask her,” he said.

The path answered for itself with the sound of smart shoes on the ramp boards. Ivy came down with her bag in one hand and her coat draped over the other arm, not wearing it, just using it like a punctuation mark. A man in a harbor jacket followed with a flat box and a respectful distance. Ivy’s hair was pinned in a way that made wind seem like it touched other people. Her nails were the red of good fruit.

“Ms. Hart,” Ivy said, and the hello in it was correct to the comma. “You do get around.”

“I ask where the stairs go and then I take them,” Lena said. “Welcome, Ms. Vale.” She looked at the porter. “Would you call up and let Mara know Ms. Vale has arrived?”

The porter had his cap in his hands already. He disappeared up the side steps with the broom tucked under his arm so it wouldn’t clatter.

Ivy set her bag down on the bench, clicked open the clasp, and took out a stiff folder with a tabbed spine. The harbor man with the box stayed with his weight on the balls of his feet, looking everywhere but at Lena.

“We can save time,” Ivy said. “I’ll keep it brisk. I am acting on Eleanor Vale’s behalf. Guardianship application is filed; temporary orders are in process. Until you hear otherwise, you report to me anything bearing on Eleanor’s health, safety, or estate. That includes comings and goings, inventories, and any conversations you have about the nursery.” Her mouth smiled without the rest of her face joining in. “I hope we can stay collegial.”

Lena looked at the folder, not taking it. “Filed where?”

“Breakwater Probate,” Ivy said. She tapped the corner of the page with a nail. “Kersey’s office filed this morning. You’ll see that the court intends to appoint me as guardian ad litem pending the competency evaluation on Friday.” She angled the top sheet so the header and stamp were visible. The stamp was the dull blue of an office machine that needed a new ribbon: received, with a date and hour. No judge’s signature. One field for “ORDER” blank.

“Is there an order today,” Lena asked, “or a motion pending?”

“In process,” Ivy said. “I don’t expect delays. It is in everyone’s interest to regularize authority.” She swiveled a glance toward the chain at the car, quick, then back to Lena. “Meanwhile, household staff have been told to coordinate through me.”

“I don’t work for the household,” Lena said.

“You’re on their property,” Ivy said. “And you are here because this family asked you to help.” Her tone had sand in it now, an intelligent irritation. “That help should be transparent.”

“It is,” Lena said. “As long as nothing is tampered with.” She angled her body to the lock. “Did you bring a key for this?”

“I don’t operate machinery,” Ivy said, as if that answered a broader question. “Jonah?”

Jonah had come to stand near the car’s rear wheel. He kept his hands at his sides, oil-dark and easy. He looked at the chain, then at the incline, then at the sky, and shrugged a small shrug that was a full sentence if you spoke Jonah. His eyes flicked to Lena: a warning, a we can do this slow.

“I’d like to note something,” Lena said. She put her phone out where everyone could see it and took three pictures in a row: lock full, lock tight, the arc marks like drawn moons. She set the phone to live and did it again from another angle, then scrolled the light up and down with her thumb to catch the gleam of the cut. She set a penny against the shackle for scale, then took one more. “No one touch this,” she said to the air and to the harbor man’s curious shoes.

Ivy watched without stepping closer. “Are we in a television show,” she said, polite as a dentist with a needle.

“We are on a dock,” Lena said. “And this lock was scored with a blade in the last few hours.” She looked at Jonah. “You see it?”

His mouth made a shape that was almost yes and then became nothing. He tipped his chin once. His gaze slid to the cable drum. He was doing the calculus of whether to answer to the woman with the folder or the woman with the camera, and the cable won.

“Is there any reason for someone to force this today,” Lena asked, “if they have a key?”

“I couldn’t say,” Ivy said. “I prefer people use keys. It’s cleaner.” She laid the folder on her bag and smoothed the top sheet with two fingertips. “Ms. Hart, I don’t intend to quarrel with you. If you wish to catalog every scratch in this place, that’s your compulsion. Mine is to protect Eleanor. That means stopping any more intrusions. I need you to brief me on the generator status, the bell board, and the nursery.”

“I’ll brief Mara,” Lena said. “And Ms. Vale. The living Ms. Vale.”

“Who is being exploited,” Ivy said crisply, and then she softened the word with a softer tone. “Influenced. By a good many people. We are narrowing that field.”

The harborside man shifted the flat box, wanting to be told where a box went. The porter reappeared on the steps, winded. “Ms. Mara says she’ll meet you up top, Ms. Vale,” he said to Ivy. “She says welcome. She says also, Ms. Hart, if you need anything for your — your work — you ask her proper.”

Lena nodded. “Thank you.”

Ivy closed the folder and pointed the corner of it at the lock. “Noted,” she said, as if she were making a minute entry in a meeting. “Please send me those photographs.”

“I’ll send them to Mara,” Lena said. “She can distribute.”

“Of course she can,” Ivy said. Her smile again didn’t bother with her eyes. “Jonah, would you bring up my bag?” She didn’t hand it to him; she stepped away and let the porter leap to take it first, which he did, caught between rules.

Jonah touched the handle of the bag with two fingers and then let the porter do it. He lifted the flat box instead, effortless, as if it were hollow.

Lena took a closer shot of the side of the lock where the shackle entered the body. The scoring made a telltale burr you could cut your finger on. She didn’t. She wrote lock—fresh scoring—12:47. She wrote chain—no corrosion at cut site. She wrote: witnesses present: Ivy, Jonah, porter.

“Ms. Hart,” Ivy said, pausing one tread up. “Your scope here is what we make it. Don’t mistake anyone’s patience for permission.”

Lena slid the phone back into her pocket. “And don’t mistake anyone’s vulnerability for an opening,” she said. She turned to the porter. “Has the car moved today?”

“All down morning,” he said. “We brought the painter up and a crate of coal down. Last car was eleven. After that, nothing.”

Lena put the time next to that, then underlined coal. The chain made a dry sound as the car shifted and settled again.

Ivy had already started up, the coat over her arm like a flag that didn’t need a pole. Jonah followed, the flat box balanced under one hand. At the turn of the stair he glanced back once, a brief acknowledgment that the score marks were real and there whether or not anyone wished them to be. Then he went up into the strip of noon light.

“Will you be wanting the car, Ms. Hart,” the porter asked, still polite.

“Not until Mara says so,” Lena said. “Meanwhile, if anyone asks to run it, call her. And me. Before anyone lays hands on this chain.”

“Right,” he said. “We’re not animals.”

“We’re not,” Lena said. She took one last photo with the sun full on the brass, the fresh metal shining like a new tooth in a battered mouth, and then stepped back so the sea could talk louder than everyone for a minute.


==================== SCENE 7 ====================
The boathouse was the only building on the island that felt poured from the sea itself—salt-bleached planks, windows veiled by salt crystals, the floor scuffed pale where boots and buckets and fish had ground their grains into the wood. Low tide was still a couple of hours off. The bay inside the pilings breathed and bowed, like a big, slow animal trying out its ribs.

Lena propped her notebook on the rail and clicked a stopwatch. The red second hand on her wristwatch was easier on the eyes in the blue light that came off the water. From the open doors the dock ran out like a tongue, then jogged left to the ladder and the floats. The tide had sunk below the first two rungs already, leaving them dark and slick. She drew a line under 16:14 and wrote: ebb steady, wind NW light, swell from E, ladder rung 3 just exposed.

“Thought you worked with sound,” Jonah said.

He came in through the side door with a coil of line slung over one shoulder and a net bag bulging with something bivalve-shaped. He set both on a workbench and stayed produced—arms lined with that outdoor rope-burn tan, shirt sleeves rolled once, jaw shaved badly, the kind of clean that admits haste. His eyes did a long, neutral sweep of her wristwatch, then of the ladder.

“Sound rides on air,” Lena said. “So does the tide if it feels like it.” She checked the stopwatch. “You know local slack?”

“Same as anybody with a tide book,” he said.

“Books are aspirational,” she said. “The cut through the bar winds it. Local variance?”

“Depends,” he said.

“On what?”

“Tide,” he said, and the corner of his mouth almost lifted at his own joke, then didn’t.

She walked to the rim of the open bay door and leaned out far enough to see under the dock.
