There, the soft torques and frictions of the little rip in the current were visible where the pilings broke flow. A bit of eelgrass got caught, then let go. She took out her phone and shot a short clip to get the rate of travel on the white chip of paint floating by. Stopwatch, camera, notebook, each its own rhythm.

“You planning a swim?” Jonah said, not unkind, not kind.

“I’m planning not to wait on anyone’s permission if there’s a medical need,” Lena said. “Access is a relevancy question.” She made a dot: 16:18—speed increase—eddies mid-channel.

He picked up the coil of line and let a loop drop, then gathered it back up again. He stayed out of the light, keeping to the shadow of the rafters where the smell of old diesel and rope pitch lived.

“You run all the boats?” she asked.

“We have one,” he said. “Two if you count the punt.”

“Do you?”

“Some do,” he said.

“Who’s some?”

“Depends who needs to puff the inventory.”

She glanced at the hooks on the wall. Spaces hung empty in precise shapes. The curves of two oars. The outline of a davit pulley. Eagles of rust where lag bolts had sweated. She wrote: gear gaps: oars, pulley.

“The skiff’s yours?” she asked.

“Belongs to the estate.”

“You run it.”

“When asked.”

“Who asks?”

“People who want a ride.”

“Names,” she said, flat.

He looked at her, and the pause was full of nothing but the soft drub of water against the float. “Mara. Caleb when he’s got a delivery. Sometimes Ivy. Sometimes the house, if something needs hauling.”

“The house asks who?”

“I get told.”

“By?”

“Whoever speaks,” he said.

“Keys?”

“Hang there,” he said, pointing with his chin.

There was a pegboard by the door with a stencil that said BOATHOUSE in spray paint, and six steel hooks. Four held cheap blue carabiners with tags in a tidy hand. The tags read punt, shed, freezer, cage. The fifth hook held nothing. The sixth held a ring with two brass keys and a triangular fob stamped: 2. She wrote: boat keys absent or elsewhere.

“Where’s the motor key?” she asked.

“On the ring,” he said, like it bored him.

“And where’s the ring?”

“On the hook when it needs to be,” he said.

“Not now.”

“It’s out, then.”

“With who?”

He reached into the net bag and lifted a handful of shells. They were cobbled with barnacles and fine weeds. He set them into a damp crate without looking at her. “You said you weren’t running the car today.”

“This isn’t the car.” She timed the rise and fall on the ladder rung. The green smear of algae made the tide’s handwriting obvious. “Who took the skiff last?”

“No one logs petty.”

“So it’s petty?”

“Most things are,” he said. “Skiff’s not a miracle.”

“You were around the funicular when Ivy came.”

“I’m around lots of things.”

“You saw the scoring on the lock.”

“I saw what you photographed.”

“You touched her box.”

“Lifted it,” he said. “Porter had the bag.”

“What’s in the box?”

“Box things. Styrofoam, probably. Violins. Cats.”

Lena let that pass. She walked along the inner wall where rolled charts and old posters of fish anatomy leaned in mildewed rows. Someone had stenciled a straight-line wave at waist height on the boards, a faded paint stripe marking an old flood or a prank. She measured it with her hand and made a note: spring max line ≈ 1.2 m above current. “I need windows,” she said. “From here to the head of the causeway. And from here to the east cove. I don’t work miracles either.”

He watched her this time. “You can read a chart.”

“Charts don’t tell you when Mr. Kersey runs his motors hard against the pier and throws everything off by thirty minutes,” she said.

“Then read the pier.”

“I am.” She shot the post where the rope rub had bitten high and made a clean band, and the lower, older rub, grimed and rough. She checked the time: 16:30—ebb faster, chop at mouth. “How close do you want to get?”

“To what,” he said, knowing the answer.

“The east cove. There a path on the bluff side? Or is boat the only sane way.”

“Nothing on that side is sane,” he said.

“So boat, then.”

“If you’ve got one.”

“You’ve got two if you count the punt,” she said.

“Some do,” he said again, patient as rope.

“Where’s the punt tied?”

He jerked his chin toward the shadow under the dock. The punt was there, a flat thing like a shoebox with water pooled in the cupped seats. A single oar lay across it, pitted with use. No lock. She wrote: punt present—1 oar—bailer missing.

“How far can you get on one oar?”

“I can get anywhere,” he said.

She looked up at him. “That your answer to everything?”

“Most things,” he said.

A gull walked the roof with slow, priestly steps. Beyond the doorway the bay had taken on that late-day glaze that made the whole world look like it had been lacquered. Lena set her phone on the sill and opened the compass app. The needle drifted, then held. She wrote: swell reflection off breakwater mid-ebb. Then: 16:42—rung 4 exposed—current 1.2 knots est by leaf.

“I need access after full fall,” she said. “Below these pilings.”

“Why,” he said.

“Because I didn’t get to see your floor at low tide,” she said. “And I like floors.”

“People who like floors don’t get hurt,” he said.

“That your experience?”

“Feels true,” he said.

“Are there traps,” she said, not asking about anything but traps.

“None I set,” he said.

“That doesn’t answer what’s there.”

“You’ll see when it goes out.”

“You’ll let me see?”

“I don’t lock the sea,” he said.

She pointed at the hasp on the inner dock door. It hung open, the padlock like an earring, left-on-left-off. “You lock this.”

“So kids don’t take the punt,” he said. He made it sound like there were kids. Maybe there were. Maybe that’s just what he called anybody who wanted something.

“You don’t have kids here,” she said.

He said nothing.

“Where do you fuel up?” she asked.

He shifted, then leaned on the bench, folding all his weight into his wrists as if the bench belonged to gravity and he to it. “Up top.”

“Generator shed?”

“There’s a tank,” he said.

“Down here?” she said.

“There’s a can,” he said, as if he’d give her one syllable at a time until she fell asleep.

She found the can under the bench—red, metal, dented. She sloshed it. A quarter full. The cap was grimed. On the neck, a smear of graphite where hands had opened it with dirty gloves. She photographed the can and wrote: fuel can—graphite on cap—level 1/4. She sniffed it. Fresh enough.

“Who’s been out in the last forty-eight?” she asked.

“If I’m out, I’m not here,” he said coolly. “So I don’t know.”

“You could check the tank.”

“Could,” he said.

“Will you?”

“Not my job to audit.”

“What is your job?”

“Boats. Lines. Dock. People go where they go.”

“They go with a boat,” she said.

“Or feet,” he said.

“Not at high.”

“We’re not at high,” he said.

“Then give me names.”

He looked at the hatch in the boathouse floor like it might help him. It was a simple rectangle with a ring on it. The ring had a tide-stain around it like a halo. “You got a lot of questions,” he said.

“You got a lot of answers that smell like dead bait,” she said. “You want to talk about it or not.”

“I don’t want things to blow back that don’t belong to me,” he said.

“Who do they belong to?”

“Ask,” he said.

“I’m asking you.”

“Then I’m saying ask,” he said.

Out on the water, a patch of darker blue rolled through as if some hand had pressed down from above. The chop sharpened; a line of popped bubbles behind a piling traveled faster than her eye wanted it to. She clicked the stopwatch. 16:56—acceleration at mouth—wind shift NNW. She knelt to eye the ladder again. Rung 5 showed its rust, rimed with sharp lace. She wrote: workable footing at 2 below current high, descent safe now w/ rope.

The ferry’s horn from the far shore came as a long hum. Sound rode over the water like a wire stretched tight. The first gnats gathered in the angle of the door.

“What’s the rule here for borrow/return?” she asked.

“No one says borrow,” he said.

“What do they say?”

“They say take it, bring it back.”

“And if they don’t?”

“They do,” he said. He said it with that flat fisherman’s faith that things richer than rules—self-preservation, the cold in water, the feel of a small boat’s spine—kept people honest.

“You ever say no?”

“Sometimes boats say it for you,” he said. “Look at that chop. That’s a no.”

She looked. He wasn’t wrong. The channel between the outer rocks was catching a wind against the ebb; it had that steep, ugly look of water deciding how much of itself it wanted to show. Still, she put a line through the box marked “east cove now” and wrote: no-go without powered assist.

She tried another angle. “You built this dock?”

“Before me.”

“You repaired it.”

“I keep it limping.”

“That pile patch is new,” she said, pointing to the fresher tar and the epoxy band and the clean cut where something had been Dutchman’d in with care.

“Winter chews,” he said.

“Somebody hit it.”

“Everything gets hit,” he said.

“What’s your night lighting?”

“Overhead, one flood, motion at door.”

“You ever cut power to it?”

“Why would I,” he said.

“I don’t know,” she said. “Why would you.”

“Wouldn’t,” he said.

She made the ticks smaller now as the light thinned—17:08—rung 6 exposed—slack predicted 18:40± local. The boathouse gathered dusk like a listening creature. Colors went to bone. He took a rag and wiped his hands, a nothing gesture that was easily a tell. She watched the rag. It was already stained with graphite and grease before he started.

“You’ll open for me later,” she said, not making it a question.

“I’m open,” he said.

“I mean the hatch,” she said, nodding at the ring in the floor.

“You open it,” he said. “It sticks.”

“It stick more when it’s wet or when it’s dry.”

“When it either,” he said, almost smiling. “You want me to say wet so you write it in a book.”

“I already wrote it in a book,” she said. “Tide lifts everything stupid.”

He set the rag down like he’d completed a ceremony. Outside, the sky had gone to that odd peach that only happens when the water means it. The light threw a blade under the dock and turned the punt’s pooled water flat silver. Her stopwatch buzzed gentle against her wrist to mark a split: eddy reversal near inner piling—17:22. She ticked it down. “Half-hour windows,” she said. “Two tonight, one early morning.”

“You up early,” he said.

“If I have to be.”

“You will have to be,” he said.

She looked at him at that, thinking it might pass for concern, or a warning, or neither. He caught the look and let it fall.

“The skiff has lights?” she asked.

“Works,” he said.

“Radio?”

“Sometimes.”

“Sometimes like it turns on, sometimes like it gets range?”

“Sometimes like sometimes,” he said.

“The coast station can hear you from the east cove?”

“If it wants to,” he said.

“You’re not interested in helping me.”

“I’m helping,” he said. “I’m keeping you from going out in a teacup when the bay is saying no.”

“I don’t own a teacup,” she said.

“You got a mouth,” he said.

She let that sit. The last of the sun threw a bar across the opposite wall and lit up a scab of white paint in a way that made it look like a map of someplace she didn’t care to go. Her phone buzzed with the hourly from the main tide station: -0.6 by 18:30; slack plus drift to -0.8 by 19:05. She wrote it, then wrote: local offset observed 12-18 min vs. station, mark windows accordingly. She drew boxes—the kind she could see in the dark with a phone light. 18:28-18:47: access under dock. 19:58-20:14: exposure at flats below pilings. 05:12-05:29: morning sweep best.

“I’ll be back at low,” she said, closing the notebook. “Leave the lock.”

He lifted his eyebrows slightly. “If it’s left, it’s left.”

“And the skiff,” she said. “If it’s back, it’s back.”

“You said you don’t work miracles,” he said.

“I don’t,” she said. “I work clocks.”

She took a strip of orange flagging from her pocket—the kind surveyors use because they refuse to be ignored—and tied it loosely to the inner handle of the hatch. She wrote a number on it in Sharpie: 05:12. She added: boathouse—low-tide sweep—priority.

“You labeling the sea now,” he said.

“I’m labeling me,” she said.

He made a sound that might have been a laugh but stayed in his throat. The light outside dropped another shade; the water went from peach to pewter. Her last note: 17:46—rung 7 exposed—access tonight w/ boots—jonah uncooperative. She underlined uncooperative because it deserved its line.

“Anything else,” he said.

“Nothing I’ll get from you,” she said. “You want to tell me why the funicular lock looked like someone who doesn’t live here opened it in a hurry?”

“No,” he said.

She put the pencil behind her ear. “All right.”

On the dock, the float banged gently against its bumper as the tide went about its business. She stepped down to the ladder, tested the slime with the edge of a boot, and decided she’d wait for two more rungs. She looked up. Jonah had moved to the door, a shadowed figure in a square of last light, the net bag at his feet.

“You coming back alone,” he said, not a question, not quite a warning, a sentence anyone could parse ten ways and make it fit.

“I’m coming back,” she said.

He nodded once and reached without looking to switch on the overhead. The single flood lit the boathouse in a thin, insect-white slab and made the water under the float go black. She stepped into the wider dusk and wrote one more thing, not for anyone else: bring headlamp; expect stick; rope and gloves.

The bay breathed again, the dock answered, and the boathouse held its shape around them like a mouth holding its words. She clicked the stopwatch to stop, then pocketed it, the tiny plasticky click too small to argue with anything.


==================== SCENE 8 ====================
She woke before the alarm, with the sensation of a window closing. The room had the kind of gray that didn’t own itself yet—black at the edges, a pale wash in the center—and the lighthouse far off gave one slow turn, then another. She pulled on the boots, the rubber stiff where the salt had dried, slid the headlamp over her knit cap, and checked the bag by touch: camera, extra battery, chalk stick, flagging, evidence envelopes, nitrile gloves, pencil, tape measure, single-use chem light, and a small squeeze bottle of fresh water. The stopwatch read 05:03. She tapped start. The number she’d written in Sharpie on the flag hung in her head as if someone had spoken it in the next room.

The path to the boathouse had the hush of cold grass and soaked weeds. Her breath made small clouds in the cone of the headlamp. No wind, just the mutter of the flats and the slow, steady pop of tiny creatures working the mud. Up close the boathouse was a shape cut from a darker piece of dark, the flagging she’d tied to the inner hatch handle ghosting in the air like a tame fish.

She didn’t turn on the overhead. The lamp on her forehead made a narrow world she could manage. The float moved once beneath her boots, a polite nudge. The brine stung the throat. She crouched at the hatch and swept the beam along the sill. Kelp grit lay where it had not yesterday: a smear of frond fragments reduced to tender confetti, still damp, clinging to the roughness of the painted lip. She touched a fleck with a gloved finger. It stuck, then slid. Wet.

“Okay,” she whispered, not to the room.

She trained the light higher. Boot scuffs: a pair, heel-first turn marks where someone had pivoted, then a ladder of partial crescents leading toward the rear wall, where the plank floor ended and the cribbing began. She shot the scuffs with the camera from angles and with scale. She laid the tape measure alongside and framed them again. Her glove squeaked against the camera body; she adjusted for that because the squeak felt too human.

She moved along the line of scuffs—dark arcs in dust as much as in dirt—past the winch with its coil of green rope, past the net bag Jonah had left leaning in a corner like a companion that had decided to sleep standing. The scuffs were inconsistent, as if someone had come back in, then gone out in a hurry. At the cribbing the footprints disappeared at a gap where a plank had been replaced; the new wood took impressions too shallow to hold. She bent, followed the plane of that plank to the stone pier and down to the ladder.

The rungs showed last night’s sheen where the tide had wrapped them. Rung seven—the one she’d written—was bare of algae. It had been scrubbed by hands or by a heavier shoe. She thumbed the stopwatch: 05:11. She leaned over, bringing her light flat to the metal, and took the first series of photos. The swaying beam made the growth on the pilings seem to breathe; barnacles opened and closed in slow startle. A crab slid sideways to a place that felt like a better idea. She climbed down three rungs and paused, testing the slime with the boot toe, finding the tack of sand the tide had left.

“Don’t get clever,” she said, because cleverness was where ankles broke.

The flats beyond the dock were exposed like lungs, ribbed by wavelets and cut with trickles of remaining water that made blue veins under the starlight. Her headlamp turned the first twenty feet into a theater of pale mud and shining eelgrass. The smell of rot and clean brine mixed into something she knew better than she wanted to. She stepped down, shifted weight, and placed her boot to leave a full imprint; she wanted to see what a fresh bootprint looked like here, now, under this exact water content. The heel spread, the toe held, the edges feathered with a draw that would crisp as it dried. She took a photo of her own print and etched an L in the corner with the chalk—LENA—so she wouldn’t confuse herself later.

The scuffs hugged the under-dock shade, where the drip kept the mud soft. Someone had chosen the dark path, where a light in the boathouse wouldn’t reach. She noted the distance from the ladder to the first print: two and a half of her boot-lengths. The stride length was shorter than her own, careful in places, longer in others where the flats were firmer. Down along the shadowed run between the pilings, the grit of kelp reappeared in tiny fans where the shoe had slid. She knelt, and the cold wicked through the knees of her pants. She dabbed a fleck into an envelope and wrote with the pencil on its margin: BH sill match? Predawn 05:17. She took pictures, set the camera down, held the envelope and the grit for the camera, then sealed it.

The time window narrowed in the back of her head; she could feel the pull change even before the flats showed it. The waterline at the far edge made a faint whisper it hadn’t made when she came down the ladder. She followed the prints under the dock until the dock ended and the open flats began. There, the boot marks grew bolder, then broke into a smart pattern that avoided the deeper troughs. She paced it, photographing at intervals: every fourth print, the matched pair at a turn, the tiny scrape where a barnacle had been kicked and had bled a smear of its own chalk. She marked those with chalk dashes on the mud and the nearest piling—just enough to re-find the line if she had to backtrack in a hurry, not enough to read like an invitation.

When the pilings of the main house came into view—thick, tar-painted, gone wild with mussels—the line of prints angled for the rock outcrop that the house straddled. The outcrop was a bluff only when the water filled the space; now it looked like a lunging animal caught mid-leap, ribs and spine exposed to air. The lowest lip held a seam like a grin set too tight: a long, low mouth with cracked teeth of stone and weed. She put a hand to it. Cold. The kelp tufted at the seam was fresh-cut—knife-ends, clean in the light, not rotted tears. She put her nose close. It smelled like a torn cucumber.

Her beam slid into the seam. The light struck wet rock, drawn sand, and a shelf wide enough to take a person sideways if they turned their shoulders. Footprints again: compressed sand grains packed in the dim with a damp shine. The edges still sprung under pressure. She took a photo of the first two treads inside the seam before she put her boot anywhere near them. The camera lens fogged; she wiped it with the inside of her sleeve and shot again. Evidence envelopes didn’t want to open; she coaxed one with a fingernail and pushed a pinch of seam grit into it—grain pollen, tiny bleeds of rock, a hair-thin strand of weed.

“Right,” she said. “In we go.”

She checked the stopwatch: 05:21. Eight minutes before the edge started to carry weight back in, maybe ten before the lip spat. She went in sideways. The headlamp flattened the colors—greens were soap, browns were bruises. The roof of the seam was low enough in places to be a conversation with her hat; she ducked where the rock suggested she should. Her bag scraped once against a tooth of stone; she angled it to keep it out of the splash zone. There were little cities in there, tiny limpet towns and a file of snails moving like thoughts. The prints ahead of her bounced to the left over a hump of rock, then flattened again where a trickle of sand had made a firm path. A long strandline of eelgrass had adhered to a ridge like a banner.

She switched off the lamp for a breath and let the gloom own itself. The outside light, pre-dawn thin, came in along the floor as a silver. Inside, the dark was complete except for a far pinprick that might have been just stone reflecting stone. When she snapped the lamp back on, her own breath plumed in new white and the beam filled with salt dust like snow.

The seam widened a palm-width, then a little more. The prints grew clearer on a patch of clay where the sand had been eroded. One heel had a nick—a small crescent missing near the outer edge—and that showed in every station where the heel found purchase. She framed the nick three times, once with the tape measure laid across, once with her own heel for comparison, once crouched lower so the reflection off the wet clay became a line. Then she crawled over that patch, placing her knees and hands in the mud to leave as little on the print field as possible. Her gloves turned the color of night coffee. The bag knocked again; she lifted it and kept going.

Once inside the first bend, the ceiling rose half a foot and she was able to turn a shoulder and breathe without the rock telling her how to do it. The air had the particular stillness of a cave that sees sea twice a day and tolerates it. A trickle dripped from a seam somewhere overhead and counted her breath for her. The sand line on the walls was easy to read: scum up to here; higher eddies upstairs. She looked for strandlines with flotsam—straw, paper, string—and found none. Whoever had used this passage, used it when it was like this.

There was a point where the floor turned from sand and clay to a ledge of smooth stone worn by feet, knees, or just time. The path there took a peculiar step that had made her smile in other contexts: a mild hop, a plant, a skid mark on the far side. She took a photo of that hop with the bag laid off to one side as a silent witness. The headlamp flared on the walls and caught something else: chalk marks. Not hers, different chalk, darker in tone, as if made in a fresher sweat. Short ticks at waist height, one, two, three. She raised the light. They were on the left side only, with a longer horizontal line below them at about knee height. She put her finger beside the ticks and took a picture at that angle, the nail blue with cold in the beam. She wrote on her palm with the pencil: 3 ticks, left wall, 05:26.

Her stopwatch said 05:26 as if it were making a point. Behind her, the sound of the flats changed again; the whisper went to a first consonant. She could feel the change through her hands in the rock. She moved forward.

The seam took her under the main house. She knew it not because she could see the pilings—she could not—but because the geometry of the cave changed from natural to human-aided. The roof flattened; there were wedge holes where blocks had been set long ago. In one place, a timber had been braced against the ceiling to keep the slab from spalling. It had a shaved edge. The sand line here was lower, as if the overhang sheltered it; the prints were crisp as pastry.

The nicked heel again. A toe drag in a hurry. A tiny dot of something that was not rock or mud—the faintest glitter when her beam caught it. She knew that glitter from a hundred other rooms: mica, graphite, the thin shine of something soft ground against something harder. She put a piece of clear tape on the dot, pressed, lifted, and held it in front of the beam. The dot rode the tape as if it owned it. She laid the tape on a card and shot it, flipped the card and shot it again.

She reached up and drew her fingers along the underside of the bracing. They came away with a smear of white. She touched the smear to the underside of the card and held her light at a slant. It made a weak line when she dragged a pencil across it. She photographed that, labeled the card. She didn’t stand there and wonder about it. She looked forward and moved.

The seam opened into a short chamber that had not decided what it wanted to be: more cave ahead, a side crack to the right that smelled like rot and old rope. The prints went straight. The side crack had the old rope. She lifted the headlamp. A fray of line hung like a beard from a jammed rock, cut square. She took that photo, then three more, then noted the cut face of the rope was clean of any new salt rime. She did not want to touch it, so she did not.

Beyond that short chamber, the floor rose. Her shoulders found the rock again. When she came up into the last of the seam before it pulled itself tight, her lamp hit the place where the cave made a speaking tube into the underside of the house. It wasn’t even as formal as that; it was a hole in the rock filled with light sifted through a slatted gap. Planking above. Daylight came through in threads from between boards. The space smelled like wood that had had a good, long conversation with salt. She could hear a distant tick: the same overhead the boathouse gave when a gull chose a rail. The prints led to that gap and stopped, then came back; the returning prints stepped on the outgoing in one place and to the side in another. She shot the set, then laid the tape measure in a straight line from the last clear print to the gap and shot that too, then a shot with her hand at the slats for scale.

She switched off the lamp again, let herself hear it. There was no other movement here than what the sea would always do. She switched the lamp back on and looked for anything else to fix in place. A single strand of hair was caught on a barnacle near the gap, pale in this light, kinked where it had been dragged across a rough surface. She took that with tweezers and an envelope and wrote quickly: hair 05:31, gap under house.

The stopwatch said 05:31. The whisper behind her had become words. She backed out the way she came, placing her boots in her own steps as best she could, then stepping to one side when the rock asked for it. The bag banged only once. At the hop she hopped. At the clay she planted her hands on either side and held herself away from the prints she had not yet shot on exit. She paused at her chalk marks and took them again, so that the second image would carry whatever had changed. When she re-entered the seam with the cucumber-cut kelp in it, a small tongue of water licked her boot at the heel and told her what time it was.

On the flats, the blue veins had fattened. The upper edges had gone oily with the first smoothness that said run, however the mind dressed it up. She allowed herself a straight line back to the ladder, abandoning the under-dock shade for open mud—the prints she left were hers, she’d marked them. The barnacles on the pilings wore a echoed edge of white froth. She climbed up, paused at rung seven because she wanted to, then was on the float and then on the boathouse floor, which felt like a continent.

Inside, she did turn on the overhead flood because the beam fulfilled its promise: a slab of thin insect-white that left the corners untouched but put her work in a bright enough circle to see it all at once. She laid out the envelopes on a clean board: kelp grit sill, seam grit, tape with glitter dot, chalk smear, hair. She wrote the time and the place on each with a hand that had warmed enough to be steady. The camera downloaded a small parade of thumbnails to the phone. She scrolled through, checking the criticals—rung seven, the nicked heel, the hop, the chalk ticks, the gap under the planking with daylight threaded through. She didn’t need them to be pretty. She needed them to be there. They were.

She went back to the hatch and knelt at the sill again. The strip of kelp grit you could see from across the room had now been joined by an arc of lighter scuff: one heel, turning out. She took that photo too, then stood in the big light, smelling salt and wet wood, while the boathouse made small night noises around her. She wrote in the notebook on her knee:

05:12–05:34 sweep. Fresh scuffs BH interior→ladder rung 7 clean→under-dock prints + kelp confetti→rock seam access→seam prints (heel nick)→chalk ticks L wall→short chamber w/ rope fray→gap under main house planking w/ light→return prints overlapped. Photo trail captured.

She underlined: route confirmed beneath house.

Outside, the tide put its shoulder back into the pilings and found its old path. Inside, the flagging on the hatch handle moved once, a small gesture anyone could read. She put her hand on the wood to feel the next push, then picked up the bag and made herself light again.


==================== SCENE 9 ====================
Lena set the blue enamel kettle on the hotplate and let it hiss while she cleared a lane across the estate office’s lab bench. The bench had been a shipping table once—dovetailed legs, scarred top—now faced in a roll of butcher paper she pulled tight and taped at the corners until the wood disappeared. She laid the morning’s envelopes in a line, then put the notebook open at a fresh page, date and time already penciled in block letters at the top left.

Kelp grit sill. Seam grit. Tape with glitter dot. Chalk smear. Hair.

She set a jeweler’s mat under the tallest bag and snipped the tape, lifting each sample out with tweezers, arranging them on microscope slides she’d label in a minute. The light from the single north window was good, flat, and promised to stay so until noon. The office’s old switchboard hummed. The generator meter sat quiet, needle slack at zero. The boathouse flood she’d left on had been turned off—Mara or Caleb, hard to say; either knew where the breaker was and which lever rattled without effect.

The kettle clicked. She poured water over a teabag and forgot it, eyes on a dash of glitter that didn’t quite belong in a boat shed. Under the stereo scope, the glitter was a factory square glued to backing—craft-store stuff, dime store. She wrote: adhesive dot: PVA? gold foil.

On the next slide, the kelp grit looked like chips of licorice glass. In the seam grit she found two short synthetics in a loop like the mouth of a bracket: translucent monofilament, relatively thick, abraded at one end to a floss. She measured with the reticle: 0.46–0.48 mm. She measured again because the first numbers were clean. She took out the travel calipers and got the same story. Fifty-pound test, give or take, and cheap by the haze of its extrusion.

She reached under the bench and pulled out the plastic tote she’d been pretending she didn’t have to open. Inside: the watch in its evidence box, foam cradling it like an egg. A familiar ugliness—a chunky men’s field watch with a stiff silicone band, the protective film still ghosted around the bezel. “Oliver” stamped on the dial in a serif that tried to be heritage. She had photographed it, logged it, bagged it again when the old nursery had called out of a sealed door, and then she had put it away because a thing like a watch could be nothing but a lost thing.

She popped the lid and took three more photographs—face, crown, clasp—then turned it over. Caseback engraving: OLIVER 120M, quartz, a model code and a serial that read like a date someone had tried to mask as numbers. She wrote it all down. The crown had sand under it, a talc of mica you got from certain flats if you were sloppy rinsing your hands afterward. Lena turned the watch to the light and saw, under the film, a Barber’s Bay Bait & Tackle sticker peeled off in two halves, sticky siding sticking to the edges of the protective plastic. The shop applied those to knives, to license lanyards, to the little LED torches they kept in a fishbowl by the register.

She put the watch back in its cradle and reached for the estate office phone, a Bakelite that had learned modern tones. She dialed the number she’d written on a used envelope last night and pressed the receiver under her chin.

“Barber’s Bay Bait,” said a woman’s voice over a shudder of compressor and a slop of water. “If you’re a salesman, we’re closed.”

“I’m not selling anything,” Lena said. “I’m calling from the Oliver estate on the point.”

A pause, then the woman said, “Oh. The old house. You all right out there?”

“We’re fine. I’m hoping to ask about two things you might have records for. I can pay your time.”

“If it’s license renewals, I can’t do those till lunch. State server’s—you know.” A muffled slap of a screen door; the gulls outside cut off with it. “What things?”

“Fishing line and a watch,” Lena said.

“Same line as I always sell or fancy line?”

“Monofilament, roughly half a millimeter. Off-brand, kind of smoky. I pulled a strand this morning that measures like a fifty.”

“That’s the Easton bulk spools,” the clerk said at once. “We sell by the arm’s length from those. I mark the bag with the test if folks don’t take the cardboard.”

“You keep a ledger?”

“You sound like my father,” she said, amused. “Yes, I keep a ledger. Cash box ledger gets tallied on Sundays, then into the spreadsheet if the Wi-Fi holds. Why?”

“Because I need to match some purchases to a set of times,” Lena said. “And the watch. Do you sell watches there?”

“We sell knickknacks. We got knives and nail clippers and bobbers shaped like corndogs. Last month we took a shipment of those Oliver watches—some outfit decided our bay would bite on a house-branded waterproof. Ten bucks margin on a fifty-dollar face. I put them next to the license wallet chains.”

“How many did you sell?” Lena reached for the tea and tasted it cold.

“Three the first week when the ferry townies came through. Two since. You with the estate—does this have to do with that lady inside? I don’t want to be in the middle.”

“It’s not about her,” Lena said carefully. “It’s about establishing where something came from.”

“So provenance,” the clerk said, making the word softer. “I like that. Makes me feel like the Montauk auction.”

“Can you check your ledger for those watches?”

“Hang on.”

The phone breathed in Lena’s ear. The office clock—a municipal clock face rescued from a courthouse lobby—ticked with an honesty that had no time for the fact she’d taught it to keep seconds on generator. She slid a micrometer across the monofilament again and wrote the numbers twice, then re-read what she’d underlined earlier: route confirmed beneath house. She rolled the calipers over the sample till she caught a nick that matched the heel nick from the prints. The world made small, true noises. She let it.

“All right,” said the clerk, back, the sound of a ballpoint clicking against wood. “I got my little list. Watches. I write those on the sundries pad, so different from the bait side. One to a summer kid with a Canadian card, his name’s a dot to me. One to Anders, the painter with the dog that eats bees. One to... Jonah. You know Jonah.”

“Which Jonah?” Lena said, though everyone meant one.

She heard the small smile in the clerk’s voice. “The only Jonah who brings me coffee when he’s been up since four. He took a watch. He’s got rough wrists, I told him he needed a band that didn’t bite. He said ‘It’s waterproof, right? If it dies I’ll pitch it into the bucket.’ I told him to bring me the dead and I’d make a shrine.”

“Do you have a date?”

“I put dates whether the state asks me to or not. Let me check. This pen is... hang on, I tore the flip.” The page made a dry wing in the speaker. “Jonah’s watch was... morning three days ago. Two days? Lord, I can’t tell since that freak tide. Friday. That was the day my freezer was acting up because Peregrine took apart the breaker and didn’t tell me. You ever watch a man fix something by listening to it? Anyway: Friday, 11:12 a.m. I remember because he made a joke about halves. He bought the watch, a bag of whole mackerel, two dozen sandworms, and twenty arm lengths of fifty-pound mono. I put the mono in a Ziploc with the green sticker, because he’ll wad it in his pocket otherwise and swear on his mother he kept it neat.”

Lena set the pen down. “Can you check if he bought line again since?”

“Hold your horses. If the line went in cash it’ll be on the bait side ledger. Monos I tally by the spool when I get down to the cardboard. We had three spools last week, only one new in. Let me think.” Paper again. “Jonah—what you want to bet he came between six and eight like he always does. Here—Tuesday at 6:58 a.m., he took ten arms of eighty and ten of fifty, three eels, and a hot coffee because my machine finally steamed. Thursday, he took squid and six arms of forty, and he made that face at me when I recommended the nicer leader. Friday’s the one I said with the watch. Saturday he didn’t come in, he was probably at the boatyard. Sunday we don’t open. Yesterday he came by after noon and only bought cornmeal and a pack of batteries, double As. For what, I don’t ask.”

“Your ledger reads the time on those?”

“If I’m at the register. Mornings I’m at the tank cutting bait. After eight I get a chance to sit and write. If someone I know hollers on their way out, I put a clock number down so I don’t have to guess.”

Lena flipped to her tide chart, the one she’d penciled in last night with causeway windows the color of old bruise. Friday low was at 12:03, extreme. Tuesday low: 5:48 a.m., also deep. Thursday: 6:24 p.m., middling. She circled the Friday entry.

“The times you put are written right when he bought them?” she asked.

“Give or take five minutes if there’s a line, and I mean a line of three men stamping their boots because they’ve forgotten cash again. Friday he didn’t stamp. He came in with kelp on his cuffs. Which is never a thing I say to him, because he rolls his eyes.”

“You’re a treasure,” Lena said before she could stop herself.

“I’m a woman with a ledger,” the clerk said, pleased. “Is this something you need me to print?”

“Not yet. Another question: the monofilament. The Easton bulk.”

“What about it?”

“Do you cut it with a knife or with the little cutter under the tape dispenser?”

“Cutter. The knife mangles it. The cutter leaves a neat, flat end, but sometimes it leaves a burr if the blade’s dull.”

“Does the bag get anything on it, sticker-wise?”

“Green circle sticker with the test written on it with a fat tipped Sharpie. Sometimes the sticker falls off because I’m cheap and they hate the cold. Then I write on the bag.”

“And the watch. Did you put a shop sticker on those?”

“I stick our rectangle on a corner. Makes returns easier. I suppose you could peel it, but you’d be surprised how lazy people are.”

Lena looked at the watch again, the half sticker ghost on the film. “Do you have any left in stock?”

“I got one in the case that ticks slow and one that’s not ticking at all. The battery shipment got wet. The Oliver man said he’d send replacements in the next mail.”

“Who supplied them? The Oliver thing—the brand.”

“A wholesaler in Seaside. They had a pallet with the town names printed. I only took ours. They’re all the same watch. Do you want the invoice number?”

“Yes,” Lena said, and wrote while the clerk read the columns in a staccato that felt like a different kind of tide. “Thank you.”

“I’d ask what this is for but you sound like you’ll tell me only what I need.”

“I need to match your ledger to a chart,” Lena said. “So I can say something is or it isn’t. You’ve helped with the is.”

“Glad to assist. You bringing Mara into town later?”

“Maybe,” Lena said, and surprised herself by smiling. “If I do, I’ll bring the bad battery.”

“You do that. I like shrines. Anything else?”

Lena hesitated. “Does Jonah ever buy graphite pencils? The big soft ones?”

“Lady, I sell bloodworms and gum. If I sell a pencil it’s the one I write IOUs with. Jonah pays cash. He never takes my pencil.”

“Understood.”

They said their goodbyes. The receiver made a sound like cloth moving when she set it back.

She went meticulous then, a speed she knew not to rush. She lined up the ledger entries in the notebook, wrote the dates and times in a column, then drew a parallel column for tide lows. She arrowed from Tuesday to Tuesday, from Friday to Friday. Each arrow kissed a window she had felt in her ankles that morning on the flats—the hush before the push, the blue veins fattening.

On the bench, she took one of the monofilament hairs in a clamp and cut it with the edge of a spare tape dispenser blade, then looked at the fresh end under the scope. The cut was blunt, square, with the slightest hangnail where the metal had left. She pulled the strand from the seam grit back into view and compared ends. They matched where you’d expect them not to if someone had snapped the line by hands; this had been cut clean.

She found an old Ziploc with a green circle still stuck to it in her kit—one she’d used to hold coin batteries last month, and then thrown back as a reusable. She smiled without moving her face; the sticker adhesive felt the same when she peeled it—tacky, reluctant.

She took the watch and read off the model code from the back again, then wrote the invoice number next to it. She did not stroke the sticker. She did not peel. She photographed the caseback with the handwriting from the clerk’s phone call under it. She took a fresh sheet of paper and printed in block: Barber’s Bay Bait & Tackle stock: Oliver 120M. Receipt identification: Friday 11:12 a.m. Sale items: Oliver watch, fifty-pound mono x20 arms, whole mackerel, sandworms. Jonah.

On the computer the estate used to catalog oil paintings and rifle scopes, she tapped out an email to herself, subject line ledger match, body a list of the dates and times, attached photos of the watch and the monofilament under the scope with her measurement reticle overlay. She backed it up to the external drive because she had learned to back things up even when no one was looking.

When she pulled the tide chart in front of her again, the pencil annotated it with the neatness of a person mapping a worksite: causeway window 10:40–12:20. Boathouse flats accessible: 10:55–11:50. Ledger Friday: Jonah watch + mono purchase 11:12. She drew points, then lines through the points. The lines looked like a thing, the first thing all morning that had nothing to do with the texture of sand. She drew no conclusions. She wrote the numbers. She left them.

The office air had warmed so the tang of mackerel from the sample bag rose up. She sealed it deeper in its slot. The hair on her slide—long, dark, broken at one end—she taped the cover slip and wrote where she had taken it, and from what. An hour had passed without her moving more than fingers and a neck.

Her phone buzzed in a polite, local way. A new text from the clerk at Barber’s Bay: You didn’t ask but I looked, and yeah, Jonah stares at the watch case each time he comes. He says “These’ll do in a pinch.” Not a bad thing to say about a thing, I guess. Also, if he asks, I didn’t sell you his business.

Lena wrote back: You didn’t. Thank you. Then she turned the phone face down and drew a small map of the under-house route in the margin of the notebook, not because she needed it but because the hand liked the shape.

All her work sat there in the circle of north light and it was not clean, not a courtroom anything, but it was something. A name on a ledger, a time, a tide, a line cut with a dull blade, a watch that came from exactly where the name spends his mornings. She had a stack she could point to, not finger-pointing but pointer-pointing, and she felt the balance move inside her away from maybe toward yes by a fraction enough to be called a measure.

She packed the watch back as it had been, made a note to take it to town to see if the clerk’s dead-battery shrine would take it, and rinsed her tea out carefully at the lab sink until the brown was gone. The needle on the generator meter shivered and came up a hair’s breadth with the fridge down the hall, then settled.

On her way out of the office she checked the bell board the way a person checks their stove, a glance that read more than it rightly should. Everything was as she’d left it, wires not humming, lights dead, the old hands sleeping. She left the room the way she’d found it, but with her notebook slightly heavier, which was the kind of change nobody saw until they were made to.


==================== SCENE 10 ====================
The light was a kind of pewter—late, mean, enough to make everything readable and nothing friendly. Lena moved along the generator room corridor with her pack unzipped so she could work on the run. The place smelled like warm varnish and clean oil. The auto-start would hit at 18:00; the panel said so in embossed labels that had outlived the man who stuck them on. 17:56 now, her phone told her in a square, strict font.

She clicked the voice memo and let it run. “Day three, evening. Scheduled start in four. I’m at the corridor junction—generator on my left, bell board two doors up, nursery landing past that. Lights are off. I’m not touching anything.”

She laid out what she needed on the floor in a neat triangle: camera with the fast lens, the little LED wand with its dimmer taped so it couldn’t strobe, the paper evidence sleeves. The camera strap went cross-body. She tested the shutter on silent. It was as close as a device could get to not being there.

From the generator room came the softest of pre-lives—tiny clicks as relays stretched awake. She breathed in and out two-counts. “I’m expecting vibration,” she told the phone. Her voice sounded different in the corridor, thinner. “And air. If the ducting changes, the house moves a little.”

She killed the phone screen so it wouldn’t bloom light. Through the door’s wired glass the generator sat like a sleeping animal, orange and confident. She didn’t need to see it to know it would take its breath and then eat. She stepped backward until she could see down the run to the bell board: the old oak case with its ranks of lenses and brass pupils. Past it, the nursery corridor turned, tight, new paint still matte where everything else had been glossed to death with age.

17:58. From the generator: a deepening of the hush, as if quiet had weight and that weight shifted. A prep fan whirred for three seconds, stopped.

Her phone gave her 17:59.

“Okay,” Lena whispered, and it came out as if the house could count it. “Okay. Now.”

The start wasn't dramatic. The generator clucked, engaged, built its own wind, and then settled into a purred uniform. At the same instant—no lag she could feel—the air in the corridor lifted. Lena felt it on the hairs at her wrist. A draft pulled from behind her, from the nursery side, as though the house had exhaled from a different lung.

The bell board trembled along its glass, not a ring but a sympathetic twitch, the way a sheet of music moves if you shut a door. The very lowest of the brass indicator arms hopped—once, twice—and then sat. There was a hiss as the thermostatic louvers in the generator room snapped to their new angle. In the nursery corridor, somewhere inside the bend, there was a sound she would have mistaken for a moth striking a lampshade if she had not been waiting: a tiny tick of paper on wood.

Lena moved. The camera lifted into her hands the way a tool goes to the familiar task. She slid past the bell board, saw for an instant the nursery label under glass—a neat enamel tile that used to mean someone could tap a button and get a nurse—and then took the turn.

A white square lay under the seam of the nursery door where the paint had not sealed perfectly at the threshold. It had not been there forty seconds ago. It had not been there ten. Now it was, perfectly centered, face up.

She put her boot against the opposite baseboard so she could work without crowding the thing. The LED wand came on at its lowest blue and she feathered it left to right to throw shadows along the paper. The whole corridor looked like bone.

“Second note,” she said, because saying it made it separate from her. “Appeared concurrent with start. Low-pressure event from generator exhaust duct? Draft pulled from west to east.”

The camera chirped without a chirp as she shot the first set at sixty-fourth, then a second pass at a thirtieth, bracing against the wall. The paper’s surface took the light in a way that said graphite, not ink. The strokes had that metallic lie that pencil tells when you tilt it. There was the word, the same word, lettered the same way: Oliver. The tail of the r like a hook, the O thick on one side and almost kissed shut on the other.

She put her left hand flat to the floor to steady the light and raised it toward the door seam. The old house provided: dust on her fingertips, a smear of black that had no business being so fresh where everything else had settled long ago. She held the tips under the wand, turned them. The black on her skin shone the way pencil did.

“Graphite from the threshold,” she said. “Transfer contact.”

She shot the note’s edge at raking angle. Tiny glitter-flecks rose like stars in the grain. “Shine suggests soft lead, B grade or softer. There’s particulate on the fibers consistent with rubbing rather than writing. No ridge compression around the letter stems—likely a copy.”

The house hummed on, unconcerned. She kept one eye on the seam under the door. It was a coin’s thickness of gap. Enough to slide a folded card if you guided it.

“What moved,” she asked the door under her breath. Then louder, for the cheap microphone. “What moved: Louvers opened, negative pressure through the landing. If there’s a cache above the door, one flap could flip.”

She swept the wand up slow to the top jamb. The paint was chalky. At the very top corner on the hinge side, the trim had a hairline that wasn’t a crack so much as a seam disguised as one. A sliver of new wood, unfinished at the cut. No screw heads. No putty. On the jamb itself, just above her reach, a pinhole that had been filled and then sunk in.

She stepped onto the baseboard and got one boot to bite. The wand in her teeth made the world a cave. Up close, the pinhole had a faint bloom of black around it. Not soot, not mildew. Graphite again. She put the LED sideways and saw a pencil smear along the crown moulding, a shallow arc like a scuff where something had swung.

“Mechanism traced by contact graphite,” she said. “Arc present along top moulding. Suggests moving member rubbed pre-dusted surface.”

Her shoulder gritted against the wall and she climbed down.

She took the folded paper with gloved fingers only after she had shot the hell out of it from every angle. It made the same dry whisper against the glove as the first one had. She slid a sheet of Mylar under it and a second on top and lit it flat. Beneath the word she could see the faintest pattern—like the memory of something underneath.

“Indentation mapping will show it better,” she said, “but we have transfer specking. Specks are evenly distributed along the swells, not clustered at downstrokes. That’s not a hand’s pressure profile.”

She turned her head, held back her breath, and listened for feet she knew weren’t there. Nothing. The generator cycled a fraction smoother as it reached its operating temperature.

Back at the bell board she shot the glass with the wand’s light raked low. On the underside of the nursery lens, around the old enamel label, grains of silver-black sat in a collar. If she moved the wand, they made a tiny galaxy. She swabbed with a dry tip, bagged it, labeled quickly.

“Graphite dust at nursery lens,” she recorded. “Not consistent with coal dust distribution. Same sheen. Collection method: dry swab.”

She set the LED in the corner and took the note from its sandwich onto the clean board she used for field prints. Under the dim light she could see lines ghosted in the paper that her eyes wanted to call writing until she let them just be grooves. She pulled the angle lower again, brought the wand kiss-close.

“There,” she said, and the word was more breath than voice. “Template indentations. Horizontal registration marks faint at top margin. Pinpricks at left—two—spaced a centimeter and change apart. No ink spread. No feathering. Graphite flakes visible at edges of letterforms.”

She kept her hands moving. Photograph, annotate, sleeve. The camera breathed its soft little breaths and rewarded her with the spinning after-images of a job being done.

She went back to the nursery door and lay flat this time, ear to wood. The floor was cold. She put her mouth almost to the seam and said, softly, “Good evening,” and smiled at how foolish that had to look—alone in a hall—then stopped smiling because the work was the work.

She reached up to the top moulding again and, with the gentlest brush of the glove, gathered trace. The glove came away with a sheen so fine it could have been makeup. She held it next to the note and compared the way light skated along both.

“Same,” she said. “Same composition.”

On instinct—the good kind, the useful kind—she checked the corridor wall opposite the nursery door again, chest-level. If a cord or a wire had drawn back when the draft kicked, it would want to travel where the slack was marginal. At shoulder height was a hair—a short one this time, lighter, caught under a paneling seam. She tweezed it out and slid it into a little envelope. Not a revelation; an accumulation.

She took her phone off the floor and ended the memo. Not yet. One more thing. She toggled it on and opened the camera app. She set the timer to thirty seconds and placed the phone on its back so the lens watched the nursery threshold. She went back down the corridor toward the generator room until the angle would show if the air pulled again when the louvers cycled.

The generator developed a new hush as the thermostat closed a degree. The air moved again, weaker. The phone blinked out its countdown in silence. Nothing slid under the door. Of course it didn’t. The event was done. The mechanism had had its moment, and it had used it.

She retrieved the phone, reviewed the thirty seconds anyway, quick-scrubbing with her thumb like she could move time with it. The frames were clean of change. That earned a small nod to nobody.

She sealed the new note in a sleeve and wrote the time in three places. Then she looked back along the corridor at the bell board, at the polite brass hands that had twitched when the house exhaled, and at the orange confidence of the generator around the corner.

She said into the phone, as she ended the memo, “Appearance coincided exactly with auto-start. Air movement from generator likely triggers a concealed release above the nursery door—graphite traces on moulding indicate pre-dusted contact. Note shows transfer specking and template indentations. Not handwriting in the moment. A mechanism made a window, and someone used it.”

She shut the light off. The hum held. The corridor’s pewter got a shade darker, like dusk had put a coat on. Her pack was ready without her looking. She took one last shot of the door seam at raking angle and of the bell board lens for the nursery. The lenses all stared back. That was fine.

On the walk back, she said once, lightly, to the quiet house, “Thank you for cooperating.” Then she filed the thank-you away like everything else.


==================== SCENE 11 ====================
The kitchen clock over the pantry door clicked to 3:10, its second hand skipping forward with a neat plastic tick. The house hum was different out here—steady, domestic—somewhere between refrigerator compressor and the polite insistence of the generator down the corridor. The light on the stove hood mapped a soft trapezoid over the cooling racks. The table had been stripped to wood and wiped dry; Lena had cleared a landing strip across it, pushed the bowl of apples against the backsplash, and pulled the shade half down against the sour glare off the water.

Cass came through the mudroom in a gust of wet wool and paper smell, a messenger bag across her chest and a hard-sided portfolio under one arm. She shook off the hood with one practiced hitch of the shoulder and set the portfolio on the table like something heavy but cooperative.

“Sorry about the ferry. One captain, two dead gulls, three announcements,” she said, catching her breath without sounding like she had run. “Where are we?”

“Here,” Lena said. She flicked on the lightbox with her thumb; it came up bright and clean under the butchered wood. The cord hummed. “Three notes from the nursery corridor. Two from before noon, one at one-forty. All bagged. And the journals?”

Cass opened the portfolio. The hinges gave a leathered sigh. Inside, wrapped in brown tissue, were three clothbound books with the frayed neatness of objects that had been well-used by careful hands. She peeled the tissue away without rustling it.

“Oliver, eighteen months’ worth,” Cass said. “His hand is confident. Not to the point of arrogance—no ostentatious speed brags—but there’s muscle memory. He had a left-slant on bad days and a rightward lift in the tails when he was excited. He loved his capital O’s like they were signatures, which is useful.”

She set them out with her fingertips braced at the corners, like placing tiles. Lena’s eye registered the way the paper took ink, the cream that had gone slightly brittle at the edges. She set the three evidence sleeves beside them, each marked with time and locus in her small all-caps: NURS DOOR 10:26; NURS DOOR 11:13; NURS DOOR 1:40.

Cass adjusted the shade above the sink another inch without asking and dropped a loupe by the lightbox with a sound like a coin. She laid a sheet of vellum over the surface and clicked a small clip-on ruler to the box’s edge. “We’ll work clean,” she said. “I brought gloves; I know you already know.”

They snapped nitrile over their fingers.

Lena slid the 10:26 note onto the box. The light rose under the paper and showed the interior of the strokes like bones. It was the same polite voice each time: Oliver’s hand, requesting, affirming, coaxing with little flourishes like he was amused to be himself.

“Start with this,” Cass said, laying one of the journals open to a page where Oliver had written a shopping list in a jargon of the estate—bell grease, lamp oil, linseed—and then in the margin, in bigger script: Don’t forget the bell key. “This is his O. It’s round, open at two o’clock, entry stroke low, exit high. His pressure is front-loaded, then he relaxes through the curve. You can see the fiber displacement here and here.” She touched the paper with the tip of a wooden skewer, not quite touching. “It swells a hair past the ink where his nib dug in.”

“And the note,” Lena said.

Cass slid the note into the light, side by side. The house hum clicked over a degree. “Watch the pressure profile.” She brought the loupe down, then shifted it so Lena could look without bumping glasses. “The line is superficially similar. But it’s uniformly fat through the curve, like a bodybuilder in a medium shirt. No taper at the exit. The ink sits on the paper instead of into it. And—see this? On the downstroke, there’s a scallop. That’s a microscopic hesitation. Someone’s hand following a template. Real writing never pauses exactly at the apex of a flourish. It pauses at the thought.”

“Or the word,” Lena said, because her mouth had learned to catalog speech the way her eyes cataloged rooms.

“Or the hand’s fatigue,” Cass agreed. “Oliver’s hand shows fatigue at the end of a sentence, not at ornamental loops. Whoever did this doesn’t trust the loop.”

Lena let the loupe rest on the bridge of her thumb. The letters did what Cass said. They were almost right, like a portraitist with an excellent eye and a clumsy wrist.

“Let me show you,” Cass said. She took a sheet of acetate from the portfolio, taped it at one edge to the lightbox with low-tack tape, and slid the note under it, aligning the margins. She chose a 2H drafting lead and warmed it in her palm. “I’m going to make the same O with different kinds of hand. Fast, slow, and traced.”

She drew the O at normal speed, letting the line breathe. It had a tiny shiver at the exit, human and unafraid of being seen. She did it again, slow, with perfect care. The line got a little paralyzed. Then she laid the lead on the acetate right over the note’s O and pulled the stroke while looking at the outline instead of her point.

The pencil went slightly blocky at every turn. “See the lift?” she said. She held the angle so the light caught the shine on the acetate where the pencil had dug. “A tracer presses at the corners because that’s where their fear lives. Oliver never did. He trusted his corners.”

“You’re telling me the note’s a trace,” Lena said. She kept her voice flat. Saying anything else would have meant stepping already into the conclusion, and conclusions needed to be nested in something with stamped numbers.

“I’m telling you the line quality is unpracticed in exactly the places the genuine hand is free.” Cass lifted the acetate and slid it aside. “Look at the entry strokes on the lowercase e’s. Oliver lands like a glider—slant, slide, commit. Here, the pen noses in and wobbles. And the spacing between letters—there’s a twitch of metronome. That’s a thing people get when they’re aligning to an underlying guide.”

Lena opened the second note. The same voice again, the same proportion, the same friendly tyrant of a hand that made you want to trust it.

“Baselines,” Cass said. She reached into the bag and pulled out a small steel ruler with a felt pad. She set the edge along the bottom of a line. “Oliver’s baselines on free paper drift up. He had an optimism bias.” She smiled, small but real. “These baselines are dead level. Levelness is not a crime. But it’s rare when the writer doesn’t have the page ruled. Was Eleanor likely to have pre-drawn faint lines for the nursery corridor to write these on?”

“The nursery corridor is varnished and dusty,” Lena said, cutting out the ‘Eleanor’ from habit. “The notes arrived when the generator louvers cycled. The first two at auto-start; the last at thermostat kickdown.”

“Right,” Cass said. She reached for a different journal and flipped to a page with one of Oliver’s letters to a supplier. “Look at the dotting habit. He dots i’s late, sometimes on the word to the right, sometimes not at all. Whoever did the notes dot on the stroke like they’re afraid of losing track. And there’s overcorrection on the capital L’s. Oliver made a long leg and a short foot. These have two equal feet; that’s a book form.”

Lena took her phone out, scrolled with her thumb to the shot she’d taken of the graphite on the moulding. She set it face-up so the lightbox lit it from below, faintly. “There’s pre-dust on the door trim. Someone was touching something over the door before the notes dropped,” she said. “There’s hair caught under paneling. I can’t say whose. I can say the notes appear exactly when the generator initiates, which is a repeatable time.”

Cass nodded, absorbed. “So. If you were going to make an Oliver note fall when the louvers opened, you’d have to pre-stage it. Pre-stage means no spontaneity, no hand-to-god moment. That usually means a transfer. The line quality on these is consistent with a lifted pigment rather than a soaked one. The ink has a thin meniscus—see how it beads microscopically along the fiber ridges? You get that when there’s carrier fluid but not the traditional soak from a pen. It’s like a stamp, but with a different solvent. Whoever did these—”

“—might have used a wet transfer. I know,” Lena said. “I’m not putting Eleanor’s name on that sentence. Chain of custody is absent. These notes could have been introduced by Jonah, by Caleb, by Ivy with a mind toward bluster. Or by Mara covering herself. Or by a ghost with a solvent kit.” She folded her arms, then took them down again because posture in a lab was as particular as tools. “I’m not the court. I’m the witness. I can’t name a forger without a path from hand to page.”

Cass slid her loupe aside and looked up for the first time since the lightbox had come on. Her irises reflected the white of the box. “I’m not naming anyone. I’m telling you how this is done.”

“Show me on paper that isn’t mine,” Lena said. “Show me mechanical proof from the thing, not just a profile match. Otherwise this is a lecture that won’t survive a chain.”

Cass reached into the portfolio again and brought out a zip-top bag with sheets of translucent paper inside, freckled with light, irregular spots. She put it on the far, clean corner of the table and didn’t open it yet. “These are from the trash behind Mara’s shop. Practice transfers. You can see the spatter where acetone flashed off. But we’re not introducing them yet,” she said quickly when Lena made a small sound at the word ‘trash.’ “We’re going to replicate the method with an innocuous sample so you can watch the line quality change. Then you can decide what you want to do with what I found. All right?”

Lena inclined her head. “All right.”

Cass took out a photocopy of a page from one of Oliver’s journals—the grocery list from earlier, now in grayscale—and placed it on the lightbox. She laid a blank index card over it and taped only the top edge. She produced from a side pouch a flat little bottle with a hinged cap, the label rubbed mostly off, and a fistful of cotton rounds in a sterile sleeve.

“This is not exotic,” she said. “It’s acetone. It lifts toner. With graphite rub under it, it’ll pick up the impression. People use lighter fluid; I don’t like the smell.”

Lena slid the kitchen window an inch. The generator’s polite thrum got a little more insistent in the opening. Cold air came in with a skein of sea-salt.

Cass wore a respirator while she soaked one cotton round and laid it gently against the back of the photocopy. The lightbox showed the darks blanching in blooms as the solvent passed through. She pressed the index card down with the heel of her hand, even, the way you lay a stencil.

She counted out loud to ten and peeled the card back. The copy of the grocery list had ghosted onto the index card in a fine, perfect gray. Not thick like a stamp, not wobbly like a human; the transfer knew no fear.

“This,” Cass said, setting the card next to the 10:26 note, “is a transfer. See the beading at the fiber ridges? See the uniformity of pressure along the curve? It’s like a print. And the acetone leaves those little stars when it flashes off too quickly.” She turned the photocopy so the underside faced the light; the surface showed tiny paler constellations. “Now compare to the note. Same stars at the edges of the heavier strokes. Not everywhere—so it wasn’t done on this exact paper type—but the physics don’t lie. It is, at minimum, a mechanically assisted reproduction.”

Lena brought the loupe down again, slid from the replicated index card to the evidence. The distribution of those tiny pale flecks was not poetic. It was the pattern of a process with a solvent that dried when it dried regardless of who held it. She felt the body of the argument settle—unfussy, assured.

“I have a problem,” she said. “If we go with that—and it makes sense—we still need a path from Eleanor’s desk to the corridor. We still need a hand. The court will ask whether Eleanor had the capacity to plan a rig like that and whether the content was hers even if the hand wasn’t. I need an object in the path. Tape residue on the moulding. A print on a hinge. A transfer sheet with live cells on it. Something.”

Cass nodded, pleased without triumph. “Good. So don’t call the notes fake. Call them mechanically assisted. Then go get the materials. Find the spent sheets. Find where someone cleaned a brush—acetone leaves a very particular smell in wood grain, and it flashes off but the gray it carries doesn’t. If someone’s sloppy, there’ll be wiping patterns.”

Lena’s mind moved through places like she was flipping light switches in them. The generator room, the little sink in the south hall closet, the underside of the nursery transom, the window seat in the library where Ivy had stood with her phone and the sill had tack to it. She exhaled, measured.

“Show me the lift points on the capital L again,” she said.

Cass shifted the 11:13 note under the light. “Here. Entry stroke. Pause. Then the down. Here. Pause. The thing about someone tracing is they don’t trust gravity. They make artificial gravity with stops. And this—” She tapped the flourish at the end. “There’s a double line. That’s a retouch. Oliver never went back over his own tail. It would have offended his sense of economy.”

“He never used more movement than he had to,” Lena said, half to herself. She’d felt that in rooms too: economy in the way doors closed, in the way he’d hung tools so their absence was obvious. “If I get you paper from the door moulding—flakes, any—can you match fibers or residues to an acetone event?”

“I can tell you whether the paper was in proximity to a solvent, yes. I can also look for graphite lift, plate class, and any errant fingerprints if somebody didn’t glove up. And if you find a piece of tape, even invisible mending tape, I can tell you where it was cut. People have signatures even in the way they rip tape.”

Lena angled the 1:40 note and held it in air, light coming through it clean. “There’s no reason this would be the original,” she said. “There’s every reason someone would have copied content from a known body. If the content came from the journals, you can find echo phrases.”

Cass already had a page flagged. She flipped the second journal to a page where Oliver had written, in a mood, Dear God keep them from reorganizing the south cellar again. The note said: Keep off the south cellar.

“People steal phrases,” Cass said. “It’s easier than inventing them. Forgers cheat on meaning; it leaks into form.”

Lena put the note down. She made herself not look toward the corridor even though the house was very linear and the corridor was a straight invitation from the kitchen to the nursery. She slid the notes back into their sleeves one by one, pressing the zip seals closed with the butt of her thumb.

“All right,” she said. “We proceed. You’ll catalog the line qualities, the lift points, the transfer evidence. I’ll match timestamps to the generator cycles and the bell board. I’ll go frame by frame for anyone near the corridor. I’ll pull lint from the mouldings. And I’ll find where they mixed or stored the solvent.”

Cass unlaced her gloves, stripped them inside out, and let them cough into the trash with a snap. “And if you find transfer sheets, we’re done talking about line quality. We talk about possession of tools.”

“Chain of custody,” Lena said. It wasn’t an argument now. It was an itinerary. “From hand to paper to fall to floor. From floor to bag.”

Cass slid the bag of spattered translucent sheets back into the portfolio without comment, the zipper sound small and neat. She stacked the journals, wrapped them, and left one open to a page with a little ink blot shaped like a whale. “Take this one,” she said. “If someone compares his ink to these notes, they’ll need to know his pen blotted in heat and on cheap paper.”

“I’ll keep it with me,” Lena said. She checked the clock. 3:32. “You’ll stay and write your notes, then? I’ll bring you the moulding scrapings when I have them.”

“Give me a corner of the table and an extension cord,” Cass said, already unclipping a little keyboard from her bag.

Lena plugged in a power strip and slid it across. She tucked the journals into a drawer where the napkins should have been and took the clean, labeled envelopes from her pack, the small vials with caps that clicked when they were closed properly. The lightbox buzzed like a breath held for the right reason.

“Go,” Cass said without looking up, the keys beginning to patter. “I’ll be here. Don’t step in your own dust.”

“I never do,” Lena said, and took the sleeves in her pocket like they were permission slips. She left the kitchen clinically neat except for the rectangle of light and the quiet, systematic typing at the table.


==================== SCENE 12 ====================
The mudroom held its breath around the workbench—hooks crowded with damp canvas, the sharp ammoniac tang of old mop heads, the faint salt-sour of boots left to dry. The window above the bench was quartered with salt crust, turning the late light into pale squares on scarred wood. A galvanized washtub sat upside down to make room. The bench itself was a mosaic of knife cuts, paint rings, and a burn the size of a quarter.

“Four o’clock,” Lena said, mostly to mark it. She set down a folded terry towel for a clean field and tugged the gooseneck lamp into place. “We’ll use this surface. Cameras to the left, sheets to the right. Nothing wanders.”

Cass came in with the portfolio clamped to her chest, jaw set, hair frizzing from the coastal damp. She slid the zipper open and drew out a transparent envelope, then another. The second had jagged freckles across it, crescents and stars struck into the plastic.

“Acetone spatter,” she said. “It kisses and abandons. You see how it blooms around the dots? That’s contact residue. I didn’t want to show you until you had your mouldings. But it’s time.”

Lena snapped on nitrile. The acetone smell reached before the visuals, floated up from the envelope like a used nail polish remover pad left in a hot car. “Vent the window an inch,” she said. “We’ll take our notes fast.”

Cass cracked the window. The coastal air slid in thin and cold; the smell of kelp gantries and tar sealed roof shingles rode it. She set the first envelope down on the towel and tapped the corner to settle the stack.

“The transfer sheets match the content of Eleanor’s ‘Oliver’ notes,” Cass said, voice even in a way that meant it had been honed soft. “Not the ink, obviously—toner, not fountain pen. But the same hand, same flourishes, same flourish hesitations. And—”

“Artifacts,” Lena said. She kept her hands flat on the bench. “Let me see.”

Cass peeled open the envelope and lifted out a translucent vellum. The sheet was clouded where acetone had bloomed. Along one edge, faint ghosting: lines that tried to be letters, pale and backward. Lena angled the lamp, bringing the surface up in relief. The letters resolved. An O with a bite out of it, a hanging tail on an r, the hesitation dot where the pen would have lifted but the toner didn’t.

“Mirror,” Cass said. She slipped a black card under to make the ghosts pop. “The first lift prints in reverse if you go contact to contact. If you press to a second sheet, you get a corrected orientation, but weaker toner yield. What I’ve got here is the primary lift—the mirrored one—and then repeated use of it.”

She pulled a second sheet. This one was more worked—acetone freckles grouped along the left like a tiny constellation. The same O-bite, the same tail, the same nick in the second l. “See the repeated defect? This notch right here. It repeats in every single sheet. That means the defect is in the source. A chip on the glass or a ding in the toner layer. It doesn’t show up in actual hand-writing.”

Lena nodded once. She caught her reflection faintly in the window’s quadrant. “How many sheets?”

“Eight with legible artifacts, four with partials. All spattered. They were slopping solvent, or working in hurry, or both.”

“Catalog them,” Lena said. “We’ll do a sheet-by-sheet. You call out, I’ll record.” She slid the legal pad closer, aligned a numbered evidence card at the corner of the towel. “Sheet A1.”

Cass took a breath, the valve of action opening. “A1: Translucent vellum, nine by twelve. Solvent spatter, upper right quadrant and lower margin. Toner lift in mirror. Visible glyphs: capital O with negative inclusion at two o’clock; lower-case l with notch half-height; loop of r with flattened exit. Repeated defect: notch in second l at consistent offset.”

Lena wrote, block letters, bullet points arcing quick and clean. Her pencil had a waxy feel on the paper. She numbered the corner of the sheet with a tiny removable sticker, the number matching the evidence card. “Photographing. Macro with scale.”

She brought the camera in—a mirrorless body with a stubby macro lens, the scale ruler in black-and-white. Cass held the sheet with glass-tipped tweezers. The gooseneck lamp made a bright channel; the toner ghosts popped like dried bruises.

“Raking light,” Lena said. She adjusted the lamp so the acetone blooms stood up in micro texture, pale halos on pale vellum. Click. “One with scale, one without. Rotate fifteen degrees. Again.”

The camera chatter was small and precise, insects at work in bright weather. Cass’s mouth was a fine line. She reached for the next sheet with the confidence of someone laying cards.

“A2: Similar vellum, nine by twelve. Spatter clustered at left. Mirror toner lift; repeated defects consistent. Added defect, artefactual ring like a halo; likely contact ring from a jar base set down on solvent. The o in Oliver shows a flared shoulder. That’s a toner tear.”

“Got it.” Click. “Rotate. Next.”

They moved fast. Sheet after sheet, the same family of flaws reappeared. Some had shimmery areas where acetone had puddled and evaporated, leaving a tide mark. One had a perfect dime-size void in the toner ghost, like someone had lifted a dot with tape and never put it back.

“Compare to Eleanor’s note from the nursery,” Lena said, without looking up. “You still have it memorized?”

“I could write it with my eyes closed.” Cass blew air up at her hair. “The flourish on the v in Oliver, the backward lean, the tail on the r that doesn’t quite make it to the baseline. All show here. Except here, they’re mechanical. The tremor repeats. Real hand tremor doesn’t repeat that cleanly.”

“But.” Lena looked up now. “Where were they working?”

“In here,” Cass said immediately. “Or the kitchen. The droplets on the envelope edges? See these little fans? That’s acetone hitting plastic, not paper. Someone put these sheets down on something already spattered. Your mudroom has solvent freckles on the light switch plate. They’re feathery. That’s atomized.”

Lena glanced at the switch. Tiny white plumes like frost, almost invisible until the lamp turned the angle. She set her jaw. “You almost told me that like you were teeing me up to storm down a hallway.”

“I am.” Cass’s eyes were bright. “We have enough. This is not handwriting, it’s a toner lift. The artifacts repeat. The mirror tells the story. Kersey’s going to pressure a signature tomorrow. We go to him now. We put this on the table. We stop the theater.”

“No,” Lena said.

Cass blinked, incredulous. “No?”

“We build the timeline. We place these sheets next to generator cycles, bell events, and bodies moving in the corridor. We count where the acetone was and when.
