Then he was out under the door, into the cold mouth of the corridor beyond, the red-slit penlight deep in his fist and the taste of creosote hung behind his teeth. The funicular bay kept its secrets the way a cliff keeps the bones of anything dropped into its shadow: efficiently, without comment.


==================== SCENE 26 ====================

The radio shack had been a pantry before Ivy stuffed it with iron lungs of signal. Old racks, green paint scabbed to primer, vacuum meters bright as aquarium eyes. On the wall, a pencil line marked flood heights from storms past; the newest line had no date, someone’s thumbnail etch half an inch above the last. A map of the headland curled at the corners in damp. The glass in the little window breathed fog inward.

Ivy set her coffee on a coil of coax and leaned into the hum. Her hair was tucked up under a knit cap, her mouth close to the mic like she was telling a secret. The old VHF base threw a steady carrier, squelch defeated, tone-kill engaged. She worked the patch panel with tiny clicks.

“Say again your tag,” she said, not to anyone in particular. Into the nothing she sent a soft, continuous tone, then another, a weaving: a whistle only the radios heard. She set one handheld to a looped weather alert that had no station id, the robotic voice trapped at “gale war—gale war—gale war—” every time the loop came around.

On a second bench, a cheap scanner pulsed through the county’s tourist channels; Ivy pegged the volume and laid the speaker face down, letting the wooden bench turn the chatter glossy, smothered and indistinct. Above, something dripped—condensate off the vent pipe—tick, tick, tick into a Folgers can.

When the headset crackled with Lena’s call sign, Ivy didn’t answer. She lifted the transmitter and added a whisper of heterodyne, just enough to smear the consonants.

“Fort courtyard to Breakwater—breakwater—do you copy?”

A thin hound of static bayed back. Ivy watched the needle. She leaned a bare wrist against the cabinet’s rack face and felt the metal’s cold in her blood. Then she slid the slider up another breath and the needle steadied, fat with her carrier.

“You’ll get your turn,” she told the void, voice mild, the way you talk to an insistent child. She keyed the mic and sang a single clean note through her teeth, a fluted, domestic sound, like testing a glass for a crack.

In the courtyard, the rain was a lid. The bell board’s timbers bulked black against the cloud. The generator shed’s window was a blind square. Lena had both radios on her breastbone, one strapped, one clipped, and both hissing. Her knit cap was soaked to its thread’s memory. The courtyard had the attention of an empty room that knows you’re there.

“Mara, you got Cass?” Lena said. She pressed the transmit and angled her shoulder away from the gust as if that could shape the signal.

“—ra here.” It came back flayed, a voice without vowels, or its bones rearranged. “—funic—watch—”

“I need Cass at north stairs,” Lena said. “Now. I’m getting motion at the shed.” One of the pinhole cams winked its tiny red once—wind? rats?—and the app threw up a push alert that refused to pull down the image. The status wheel spun and spun.

“Breakwater, it’s Lena at the fort. We’re live on the plan. Confirm your position.”

The reply unfurled like crumpled foil. “—Ninety-two marker. Water over. Repeat, over—”

Then the gale voice cut in: “gale war—gale war—gale war—” and held.

Lena cupped her hand over the mic. “Get off my net, you cheap—” She bit it back. She tried the other radio, the old one with the tape on the battery cap. “Breakwater, do not attempt the dip. Hold at the shoulder. Skiff option if needed. Acknowledge.”

The courtyard carried the patter of rain and the soft slap of the flag halyard against the pole, nothing else. The eucalyptus at the wall’s edge pressed its wet palms together.

Across the holdfast lawn, the administration building was a rectangle of blackness except for the brittle domination of one office’s desk lamp. Kersey had the lampshade tilted, a cave of light on invoices like a stage on a single actor. The emergency LED at the baseboard glimmered a weak fishbelly blue. The windowglass was a mirror of storm.

At the desk, he worked a keyboard with a pianist’s economy. The server rack in the closet had sighed when the subpanel tripped; now its UPS beeped every minute in a faraway, kittenish way. He had a login box up on a monitor streaked with salt—another flood season’s small insult—and an archive field open beneath it. The messages were listed with his name, her name, the deputy’s to-from chain, subject lines like “Escrow receipts—” and “Eleanor’s signature at risk—”.

He scrolled without changing his breathing. He selected Lena’s outbox items, dragged them to a folder named “temp,” and then nowhere. His finger hovered half a second a very short distance above the enter key, then pressed. The folder emptied. He opened the system log and flattened it, the way one smears a fingerprint with a thumb. He altered the time on the machine by four minutes and thirty seconds, set the NTP sync to manual, typed a line in the comments field. He clicked Print on a clean page of “Radio log—Operations,” then filled it with neat, small handwriting in a pilot black: “2100 blackout— 2113 test of siren— 2117 false alarm— 2121 all quiet.” He dated it. He breathed.

The landline was a stupid plastic thing with each key worn to a shine under the numbers. He picked it up and got secretary-busy. A right-hand drawer squeaked; he stopped it with his hip. He took out a stack of admissions forms and laid them to the left as if someone could come in at any moment and he’d be mid-duty.

“Caleb,” he said to the nothing, as if calling down a hall. No answer. He didn’t try again. He dragged the trash icon to the icon that emptied it and watched this imaginary choreography with a clean focus. On the sill, the spider plant had one brown frond collapsed like a rope. Outside in the dark, the rain drew lines straight down the glass as if scratching the night.

“Fort to Breakwater,” Lena said, now in the courtyard’s arch where rain couldn’t pick at the words as much. “Do you copy? Do not take your truck into moving water. Acknowledge.”

A pause so full it felt like someone had put a huge soft hand over her mouth. The first handheld coughed a pulse of someone else’s laughter from a restaurant four counties away. The second emitted a long tone so steady Lena felt it in her molars.

In the radio shack, Ivy adjusted the antenna switch to the old whip on the roof because the coax to the weather mast had iced with salt and she could feel the mismatch as a pinch in the audio. The looped robot was tapping the nerves of the net like a spoon on a jar. She got up, looked out through the small window, saw nothing but her own reflection and the faint motion of rain, then sat again. She keyed in a different PL tone and let it ride.

She took the headset off and hung it on the mic stand. She gave herself a drink of the coffee and grimaced because this cup had already been reheated two times. She set the cup down in the ring it had already made. “You’ll tire yourself,” she said. The ribs of the room answered with the old buzz of copper and intent.

On the causeway, the deputy’s truck nosed the brown sweep and became a boat for the length of its own wheelbase before the fan coughed, and the dash lit its little confession lights. Water came up black and busy on the doors. The deputy flicked the wipers to faster, watched the arms stutter like a metronome in panic.

He had come in hot because that was sometimes the thing that worked—with puddles, with people. But the bay didn’t care. The king tide reached up and touched his axle and the engine, earnest and willing, took a breath you should never take and quit. The lights stayed. The engine kept rolling the idea of itself. The idea faded.

“Dispatch, Breakwater One,” he said into the dash mic. His voice had that patient idea-of-calm you put on for old ladies and for yourself. “Stalled on the causeway. Water at mid-door. Not moving.” His breath fogged the cab in small bursts.

“Breakwater One, copy,” came Dispatch, bright and far. “Hold. Do not exit vehicle.”

He looked out. The headland light worked its furl through the rain and then was a smear, then not anything. To his right, the bay breathed a little louder and put a hand under the truck’s frame just to say hi.

“Fort station, this is Breakwater One,” he said on the other channel, the old one the birdwatchers and fishermen liked. “Lena, if you’re on, I’m stuck at the ninety-two. Can’t make you. Skiff will be a bit. Repeat, I am pinned here till slack.”

“—break—war—” came the net, smothered under a persistent, glassy whine.

He lifted his boots, because the wet on the floorboards rose without asking. He fished his waterproof out from behind the bench seat and shrugged it on inside the cab with that shoulder-shimmy dance, hitting the horn with his elbow once and making himself jump. He turned the heater to on even though there was nobody home under the hood to heat anything. He pulled his phone and got no bars. He tried the county app anyway and looked at its cheerful whale-splash screen, the blue cartoon; the wheel turned and turned.

He put the mic back on its hook, took a breath you could see and said, “Okay,” to nobody.

In the admin office, Kersey’s cursor hovered over “Archive Export.” He thought of nothing, which is to say he had corralled his thoughts into one cow and put it in a small fenced field. He ran the export; the progress bar sluiced left to right like a tide. He slid an old ledger book from the shelf—real paper, marked in careful fountain-pen hand by someone long gone—flipped to a blank page as he always did, and wrote the date and “Power Outage.” He listed “personnel present” and left Lena’s name off, as neatly as if it hadn’t existed.

He opened the file directory for the cameras and found the generator shed feed. It had a thumbnail of a rectangle of nothing with a timestamp. He deleted yesterday’s clip that showed the test and renamed a different one with the same numbers. His hand was as steady as if he were replacing a stamp exactly in its case.

He checked the hallway through the crack in the door to see if anyone was there; the hallway checked him back with a strip of linoleum, the dust at the baseboard, the smell of wet stone.

“Fort to Breakwater,” Lena tried again, moving now—moving warmed her—across the courtyard to the arch by the old cistern. The cistern was a throat of damp. She stood at its lip and tried the deputy again, then Mara, then Cass. “Cass, if you have eyes on the lift, say so. Do not leave that station without telling me.”

“—ena—” came Cass—a shiver, then a nothing.

“Do not climb on anything. I repeat—” Her own voice, thrown back to her by something mechanical on the other end, blurred, double. “Gatehouse to courtyard,” she tried, switching to the cheap blister-pack FRS units they kept for tourists. “Mara, if you answer me I’ll buy you a coffee that isn’t made of rain.”

The FRS crackled with a child’s voice from some other island: “Can anybody hear me? Over.” She closed her eyes. The rain had a way of asking questions on her skin: Are you done? Will you stop?

In the shack, Ivy put on the headset again and did an inventory with her ears. Lena’s call sign, Cass’s off-key whistle when she got nervous, the deputy’s clear county diction—she separated them like threads on her knee. She mynah-birded back plain air and tone. She let a little of the weather channel leak into the net and then pinched it off.

She took a small screwdriver and opened the side of one handheld, the cheap tourist radio, and slid its battery a fraction so it would half-die, half-live, that ghost state that can keep a channel like a bad storyteller. She set it down on transmit and tucked it under the bench where the metal would make it sing wrong.

Footsteps cracked the gravel outside and she looked up. She set her palm over the main panel like a person covers their plate when someone sneezes. The footsteps went on past, rain patting them flat.

“Don’t worry,” she said to the room, to herself, to a line that had gone tight across all of them. “No one is coming up this hill with a rope in their teeth. That’s not the story tonight.”

At the causeway, the deputy balanced his clipboard on the wheel and filled out the Incident card with mittened fingers, block letters. “Flood. No injury. Engine stall. Await slack.” The water had something to say and said it in tapping on the undercarriage. He flicked the lightbar to steady on, not to ask for help, just to be a lighthouse for anything dumb enough to try for him.

“Dispatch, Breakwater One, status unchanged,” he said after a stretch. “If you’ve got a skiff on the inside, call them.”

“Breakwater One, copy. Unit four says the outboard won’t prime. She’ll call back in seven.”

“Copy.” He set the mic down, because his fingers needed to warm under his thighs. He breathed on them. The heater fan clicked once on and then died again, a good-hearted corpse twitch.

Across the lawn, in the admin building, the printer coughed and the printed “Radio log—Operations” ejected. Kersey lifted it carefully, as if ink had a body that could be bruised. He paperclipped it to the previous day’s log. He put the clip on straight. He slid the dockets into the file drawer labeled OPS: in compliance.

He looked at the email client. He opened an empty inbox. He closed it. He took a breath and let it out through his nose. There was a wet shine on his cheekbone from nothing he would name. He closed the ledger and slid it back, the leather along the wood making a small, satisfied noise.

In the courtyard, Lena looked up at the bell board because habit meant you did that when the island went weird. The board hung in its frame with more authority than any person out here. The rain around it silvered. She thumbed her throat mic.

“Breakwater, if you can hear me, do not try to cross. Sit tight. I will try you at five-minute intervals. Repeat, five minutes.” She put the radio down on the cistern lip and it slithered and she snatched it before gravity took it. Her other radio, the one on the strap, hissed and then went dead, the battery icon a little white plea that never finished.

She looked toward the gatehouse and saw no sign of anybody. The world drew in until it was her, a handful of stone, the wet and the wanting inside her sternum.

“Cass,” she said again, on the tourist channel now because what else was there. “Tell me you’re there.”

A child on another island, unsupervised, droned into the channel: “Bananas. Over. Bananas.”

“Copy bananas,” Lena said, because she had to say something into a human shape. “Over.”

In the radio shack, Ivy let the carrier lie, a low ceiling over the night. She closed her eyes and listened to the through-line. She waited until the deputy tried the channel again and then she dropped the tone half a frequency, just enough to make the syllables fritz without fully killing them.

“—stuck—at—ninety—” came the county voice, a puzzle of numbers and breath.

“Too deep,” Ivy murmured to the board, to the hammering weather, to the pencil line on the wall that meant one storm only and not every storm. She nudged the volume of the loop. “Gale war—gale war—gale war—”

Kersey locked the admin office and took his keys from the door with a soft kiss of brass on brass. He pocketed the ring and held it a second longer than needed. He wiped his fingers on his handkerchief and then put it away, folded to the same quadrants as always. In the hall, he smoothed his shirt sleeve and walked down to the stairs slow, like someone whose shoe soles know each step.

On the causeway, the deputy leaned back and listened to the sound of his own breath in his hood. It made a tent, a small cloth world. The rain said what it always says. He watched the water line against the door seam and put a dot in his head for each centimeter it climbed and then another when it held.

In the courtyard, Lena’s radio wheezed once and then, miraculously, opened for a second like a fish mouth:

“—ena. Funicular—watch—north—”

“Cass?” she said, stepping into the rain without meaning to. “Say it again. Cass, say it again.”

The radio returned only the weather robot, who never tired: “gale war—gale war—gale war—”

Lena lowered the radio and in its absence felt the entire fort lean in on her, walls, gutters, stairwells, the old cistern’s throat, the black window of the generator shed with its dead little pinhole eye. She was a person with two hands and a few inches of spectrum and she had none of the latter.

She put the radio back to her mouth and tried anyway. “Breakwater,” she said. “I’ll call you again in five.”

In the shack, Ivy’s hand hovered over the switch and stayed.

In the office, the shredder gave one quick whisper, then a lumpy stop as the power hiccuped and bled.

On the causeway, the deputy watched his breath make ghosts and named each one for the minute it was. Then he stopped naming them because naming them didn’t help.

The fort held. The net, such as it was, hummed, but for Lena there was no person on the other end of it, only a storm and a room with a woman in a knit cap who didn’t answer, and a man at a desk with neat handwriting, and a deputy in a dead truck under a long, moving roof of water. The courtyard’s air got shorter by inches and the night put its arm around her shoulders and pressed.


==================== SCENE 27 ====================
Rain stitched the courtyard shut and rattled on the generator shed like a handful of nails dropped on a drum. The pinhole lens in the shed’s eave caught a tremor of motion—black sleeve, gloved knuckles, the wet blink of a headlamp beam shearing past—then the alert buzzed in Lena’s pocket with the blunt insistence of a fire alarm.

She palmed her phone. A square of grainy gray appeared. It saw rain, a corner of doorframe, the corrugation’s fluted shadow. The shadow moved.

“Don’t,” Lena said to the air, and then to the screen, and then to the shed. Her voice steamed out of her in a pale ribbon. “Hey! Don’t touch that.”

The figure was already up on the concrete pad, shoulders hunched in a slicker the color of wet kelp. Hood up, head down, boots smearing rain into black slashes. The crowbar hung at the end of their arm like an extra limb.

“Caleb?” Lena called, moving fast and then stopping two paces short of the shed wall. The generator’s louvers breathed a damp diesel ghost. “Say your name.”

The head didn’t lift. The crowbar’s hooked end found the eave, testing, tapping, patient as a beak. Ding. Ding. A pause, a tilt of the hood like someone listening for a hollow. Then the bar slid in hard and pried. The wood groaned.

“Jonah?” she tried, louder. “Look at me.”

The head turned just enough to flash the headlamp, which lanced straight into her pupils and then jittered off to the gutter. All she could read was a jawline inside the hood and the neck of a rain shell dark with runoff. The figure’s free hand steadied against the siding. A cuff showed for an instant: canvas, a seam mended by hand with black thread.

“Don’t do this,” she said. Her phone in one palm, the other lifted like she could ward off a swing with nothing but fingers. “You’re on camera.”

A laugh came up out of the hood. It was a bare exhale, and it had no smile in it. The crowbar’s point popped the trim. A shallow shower of gray pulp and dead wasps fell like ash.

“You hear me? You’re on camera,” Lena said again, dragging the phone up to give the lens a second eye. The little view showed the frame wobbling as the bar levered. She swiped one thumb on a screen full of tiny icons and felt their indifference. The rooftop dark bled into everything. On the feed, white drops streaked. A glove heel pressed into the field, black and wet as a seal.

“I don’t care,” the hood said, and the voice was scraped, weather-blunted. It didn’t give away a person.

“You want to explain that later? Because we will talk about it later,” she said, and heard how cheap the sentence sounded in the storm. “I have backups.”

A gloved finger found the wire like a fishing line and plucked it twice, quick, expert. The image on Lena’s screen jumped and tore and jumped again. The crowbar slid farther under the lip.

“Step back,” the hood said, soft, like an instruction, not a threat.

Lena didn’t. She inched to the side, angling to see under the eave, to make the phone see what her eye couldn’t. The shed stank of old fuel and salt and mouse nest. The fort’s gutters gurgled at throat height. Her shoulder brushed bramble. She kept it in her breath: Face and hands. Scar? Ring? Anything. Anything to hang a name on.

“You touch that lens and I file, and it leaves here,” she said. “Right now. You’re not fast enough.”

The hood turned back to her a fraction and stood very still until the rain made a web between them. Then, still watching her, the gloved hand reached and pressed the camera itself into the crawl of the crowbar, pinning it for the blow.

“You’re not fast enough,” the hood replied, same tone, as if they were agreeing on a weather report.

She lunged on instinct to slap the crowbar down. Metal rang off the corrugation. He stepped sideways, smooth, like he’d expected it, and the tool came up and around. It hit the eave right next to her ear with a sound so raw it tasted like pennies. Splinters peppered her cheek. She flinched, but her hand—stubborn or stupid—had gotten hold of the bar a second time, and got nothing but the slickness of rain and a smear of grease. She lost it, and the crowbar came down again. The eave coughed the tiny camera out of its notch like a tooth.

“Stop,” she said, and didn’t recognize the word coming out of her mouth.

The hood didn’t. Two fast motions: the crowbar head pinning the camera to the concrete, the boot following, weight behind it. Plastic skipped and squealed. The little eye stared up at the storm for one last flicker. The bar’s peen came down. The lens gave with a small animal sound. On Lena’s phone, an image smeared into a brilliant white starburst, then gray, then a single frame of the hood’s chest—buttons, canvas, a looping stitch in black thread through a torn seam—and then nothing.

“No, no—” She jammed her thumb at the screen, swiped, toggled, cursed, tried to push the clip into the world with a finger. A green dot spun once and stalled. A bar said queued. Another said failed.

The hood bent, hooked the crowbar under what remained of the pinhole unit, and heaved. Wires came away stringing wet, fine as seaweed. He pulled until the screws gave a squeal and then lifted what he’d torn free to eye-level. It dripped, its guts open, its card door sprung.

“Leave the card,” Lena said. “Leave it and walk away.”

The hood looked at her the way people look at a shut door—absently, already past it. The gloved thumb found the card with a clumsy tenderness, plucked it, held the sliver like a scale off a fish, and with a quiet, deliberate pressure snapped it into two pale halves. They fell and disappeared into the black slipstream at the slab’s edge.

“You’re committing to it,” Lena said. Her heart was a fist hammering in the hollow of her throat. “You know that.”

“Step back,” the hood said again. Not loud, not angry. A simple shape dropped from a mouth.

She didn’t move. Rain entered her collar like a cold hand. She held the phone above her shoulder and it held nothing. From the louvers, the generator’s restart relay clicked and failed to catch. The pump in the subpanel hiccuped somewhere underfoot.

“You turned the bell circuit earlier,” she said. “You tripped the subpanel. You’ve been busy.”

The hood’s head tilted. The headlamp’s bright core tracked the barbecue of rust along the hinge and landed on her face. It washed her eyes white. She made herself not blink.

“We’re all busy,” the hood said.

“Caleb,” she said.

“Jonah,” the hood returned, and it landed like a joke you tell to stop a dog.

Something shifted to her right, beyond the shed—movement through the hedge, a huff, the scrape of a crate slat. The head didn’t turn toward it. The crowbar rested on a knee for a second, casual, and then came up again. The hood used the chisel end to rake the cavity clean, dragging out the last of the guts—jelly-sleeved wire, a square of foam, a single bent screw—and flicked them into the puddles with the tip. The foam bobbed like a tooth on a string and then vanished down the drain grate.

“Say your name,” Lena said one more time. “You’ll say it sometime anyway. Say it while I can hear you.”

The hood stepped off the slab and closed the distance between them by half. Not fast. Close enough that she could see water beading on the stubble at the edge of the hood’s shadow. Close enough to smell metal off the crowbar and salt off the slicker. The gloved hand reached up, not to strike, but to pinch the corner of the phone where it stuck out through her fingers. He wiggled it once and then gently spun it in her grip so that the black glass was facing down, to the ground, to the water, to nothing.

“Eyes down,” the hood said.

She did it. Her hand obeyed the way a child obeys. The phone pointed at the concrete. The screen reflected the blue bruise of the sky and two feet, hers and his, pressed into it by the rain. One of his boots had a notch in the heel. The notch filled with water and emptied and filled.

“You can’t pull the bell board,” she said into the concrete. “Leave me one.”

The hood was quiet a moment. A drone of storm ran its teeth along the roof edge. Somewhere the funicular cable throbbed like a bass string as the wind vibrated it on its catenary.

“You should go inside,” the hood said finally. “Now.”

“You’re done here, then?” she said.

The crowbar knocked the door with a clunk that sounded like a knuckle on a tooth. The latch gave. The hood put a palm to the door, pushed it an inch, then closed it again with care and slid the bolt. The wasps and splinters and card halves swam in the puddle at Lena’s feet and made a map of a country you couldn’t visit twice.

“I said go,” the hood said, but it came out with less weight this time, like the language had worn thin under the rain.

She lifted her phone a fraction and caught, just for a beat, the phone’s own camera framing the slick black of the crowbar, the hood’s chest, the seam with the black repair thread, the notch in the heel. The hood saw what she was doing and took one pace backward, disappearing into a curtain of runoff.

“What did Eleanor ever do to you?” she asked, because there was nothing else to ask.

“Nothing,” the hood said, and now the voice turned away and flattened against the shed wall and didn’t belong to anything but wet air.

The crowbar’s head came up once more and bit the eave for good measure, a quick neat strike to make sure, and then the figure stepped off the pad. Boots sloshed through the fenceline runnel. The lamp’s cone swung low, lighting thigh-high grass hammered flat by rain, then went out. The dark closed over the shape the way water closes over a thrown stone.

Lena bent and dug blindly at the drain edge with her nails. She found something like a scale in the grit and pulled it up into her palm—a half-fragment of the microSD, ragged, thin as a fingernail. She held it under the shed’s drip for a foolish second, rinsing mud away so it could be clean while it remained useless.

Her phone showed a list of files with jittery timestamps and red exclamation marks beside all the important ones. One clip had a thumbnail—a sliver of motion: a slicker shoulder, the line of a seam. She tapped it and it gave her three seconds: the headlamp blurring the wall, a breath that wasn’t hers, the black stitching, the notch in the heel.

Three seconds, then a square of color that said error and held.

The generator tried once more to catch and didn’t. The rain pulled a white hiss across the broken trim. The courtyard swallowed her in its wet open mouth. She put the card fragment in her pocket like a seed, because hands need to do something, and stepped away from the shed where the camera had been and would not be again.


==================== SCENE 28 ====================
Dawn came up like a bruise over the harbor—purple in the seams, yellow seeping through. Slack tide held the cove as if with two gentle hands. The eelgrass lay flat in long cursive lines; the floats bumped each other with the soft patience of sleepers. From the boathouse, the dock ran out in old cedar, tar shining in the hairline cracks, cleats slick with salt. Lena could smell diesel, rotted kelp, the mineral penny of cold. Her fingers worked the flare cap by feel.

She heard him before she saw him: boots taking the ramp in two quick thuds, then the dock’s different timbre under weight. The boathouse doors stood ajar, a wet rope making a dark diagonal between them. He hit the jamb with one palm to swing in, then the other to swing out again, a duffel slung crosswise, a shape cased in canvas under his arm. Caleb’s hood was down. He’d cut his hair shorter. His eyes were set like knots.

“Don’t,” she said, her voice flat as the water.

He didn’t see her yet, not until she thumbed the striker. The flare caught with a spit and then a harsh, rushing light like a piece of the sun torn ragged free. Magenta bled across the dock boards; sparks pinwheeled and stuck.

“Jesus!” He flinched, one hand up. The wrapped shape under his arm knocked the door edge, thumped. He shifted his grip and squared his shoulders into it like a man carrying a plank through a crowd. “Move.”

She stepped to the rail and held the flare sideways so it washed the dock with heat. The air shivered between them. His pupils pinched shut. He tried to look past the heat into her and couldn’t.

“Drop the bag,” she said.

He tried bargaining with speed. He went for a dart around her, the dock narrow enough to turn it into contact. He didn’t expect the second flare. It was already uncapped in her other hand, her thumb finding the damp grit on the striker. The second light lanced his side-on approach, the heat close enough to wave his jacket’s cuff hair. He jerked back, elbow crooked hard against his ribs.

“Drop it,” Lena said again, and the tone she found surprised both of them: subtractive, without any seasoning.

He looked past her, to the skiff tied with two half-hitches at the ladder. The water under it was so still it held a lobster buoy’s ragged paint like a mirror. He tried calculation on his face. He’d have to go through her to get to it, and the flares wrote No in the air.

“What is wrong with you,” he said, squinting, and she noticed the jaw twitch that meant he was lining up a swing.

“Your wires burned my camera alive,” she said, not because it would change anything, but because words sometimes made shape out of this kind of moment.

“That camera shouldn’t have been there.”

“And Eleanor’s cradle shouldn’t be on a lift,” she shot back.

He gave the wrapped thing a shove up under his arm like a running back. The duffel’s zipper mouth showed a lash of nylon straps and an edge of metal, grease already ground into the fabric. Then he broke for it, an honest sprint—weight forward, shoulder low, the way people run through doors and remember themselves in the first stride. She stood her ground.

He hit the flare’s heat like a wall and swore; his jacket smoked at the cuff, a single thread peeling black and curling. He’d lifted his forearm on reflex, blocking his face. She brought the second flare down not to strike but to blind, the white heart of it held at his eye-line, and the dock narrowed down to that point of light. He twisted, trying to shoulder past, and she stepped into him.

The wrapped case knocked her hip. The flare spit sparks against the wood. She ran her knee into his thigh, hard, to break his line. He went to shove her back and his hand smacked the burning end of the flare. The sound he made wasn’t big; it was the surprised bark of someone stepping into a hole in the dark. He dropped the hand back like he didn’t trust it anymore, and the canvas-wrapped thing under his arm sagged. She brought the flare close enough to make the nylon on the duffel pucker. He took two involuntary steps back, heels finding the warped edge of a board.

“Drop it,” she said, and he did. The duffel hit the deck with the dull weight of tools. The wrapped case slid and stopped at a knot.

“What—what even is this, Lena?” He breathed the words into his sleeve, eyes slit. The skin at his knuckles flushed salmon, already angry.

“It’s you at a dock,” she said. “Hands in front.”

He laughed then, short, more air than voice. “You’re not going to—”

“I am.” She pulled a zip tie from her jacket pocket with the hand that wasn’t full of fire and flicked it open with her thumbnail. It made a little insect sound as it flexed. “Wrists. Front.”

He looked right, toward the skiff; left, toward the ramp; down. Her flares wrote two little suns in his vision that followed wherever he glanced. He tested a feint—weight left, shoulder down—but the light told him what it would feel like to try for real, and he didn’t.

He put his hands out.

She stepped in. The flare heat rolled along his forearm hairs again, raising them like a wind. The tie bit his skin and clicked as she drew it tight. He pulled back reflexively and the nylon answered with that small, implacable zip. She took his other wrist and made a second loop over both, cinching this one slower, watching the tongue travel through the square head, teeth catching, a bright neat sound.

He made a show of compliance that was two beats too late. “You’re over your head,” he said, voice level. The flare hissed between them like a kettle.

She tested the bind by twisting his hands a fraction. The plastic didn’t give. “Turn,” she said.

He turned toward the cove, toward the bruise-yellow sky. The light made his stubble silver. The boat rope ticked where it lifted and settled against the ladder rungs, that small metronome of the slack tide.

She could feel the flare burning down in her palm, heat climbing the cardboard barrel, the timing of it. She tossed the first into the water. It hit with the sound of rain on tin and kept burning, a little underwater star throwing fish-scale light up the ladder. She shifted the second into her dominant hand and used her free hand to toe the duffel away from his reach. It thumped against the boathouse threshold. The wrapped case she kicked under the bench. Something metal inside answered with a dulled bell note.

“Sit,” she told him. “On the cleat.”

“I’m not—”

“Sit.” She didn’t add anything. She put the flare’s heart where his answer would be if he had a different one.

He lowered himself onto the cleat, awkward with his wrists bound, knees open in the posture of stubborn men. He looked at the skiff again.

“You’ll just—what—keep me here?”

“Yes,” she said.

“What do you think this is going to—”

“Caleb,” she said, and the flare hissed. “Stop.”

He stopped. The tide stayed still, the pause between breaths. Gulls worked the far flats, their voices clipped. The boathouse door chain tapped against the jamb with the small steady sound of a clock.

He lifted his bound hands as if to fix his hair and winced when the skin pulled. “You burned me.”

“You burned my shed,” she said, and then let that be the end of that math.

He shifted on the cleat. “You don’t know what’s in that bag.”

“I know what you use it for.” She nudged the duffel farther with her boot until its handle snagged a splinter and hung.

“You’re going to get somebody hurt,” he said. That old quiet in his voice, that undertow he used on people. It didn’t pull her.

“You are somebody,” she said. “And you’re going to sit.”

He gave the dock the look men give when they imagine pushing it out from under themselves like a rug. But the island had gone quiet. The funicular above the boathouse sat as if forgetful. The causeway cut the horizon like a thumbprint. The skiff’s outboard waited with moth-dull paint and old names scratched over newer ones.

“Keys,” she said.

“They’re not—”

“Pocket.”

He twisted his hip to show her an empty place like a conjuror, then grimaced when she said, “Other pocket,” and turned that way. She slid her fingers into denim gone slick with salt and found the cold elliptical bite of a key ring and the lighter with the carved fish. She dropped the keys into her jacket, the lighter into the water. It flashed once like a minnow before it sank.

He watched her do it, jaw flat.

“Phone.” She kept the flare between them.

He made a small show out of thinking, then tongued his cheek and said, “Inside coat.”

She reached and found it in the lining, warm from his body. She thumbed it off. He didn’t argue.

The flare bit her palm again; the cardboard was softening, the flame wanting more of a mouth than it had left. She lobbed it into the cove. It landed near the first and the two lights burned underwater, milky and fierce, two unblinking eyes under the dock.

They sat with the quiet, with the tuck of rope and slap of a lazy wave against a float bumper, with the early thin insect of a distant outboard growing and fading. His breath came steady. Hers answered. A gull walked the dock three planks away with the flat-footed confidence of someone who’s seen this all before.

“You don’t have to play hero,” he said, softer now, trying a different door.

“I’m not,” she said. “I’m playing stop.”

His hands worked uselessly against the ties, testing the teeth, finding the same answer again and again.

He tried her name like a tool measuring tolerances. “Lena.”

“Caleb,” she said. “Quiet.”

He shut up. He looked at the water until it looked back at him.

She paced two steps toward the boathouse and two steps back. The wrapped case under the bench sat where she’d kicked it, a corner of canvas abraded to show the contour of a handle beneath. She put one foot on the duffel in a way that made it clear it would not be going anywhere. The light grew; the bruise color bled out of the sky and left a pale, hard thing in its place. A wind from the west brought the iron smell of the flats.

He tested the ties again. She didn’t look at him when she said, “If you stand, I will put you on the deck.” The voice came easy. She believed it.

He didn’t stand.

A crab pot bellied up under the dock and drifted out again with the idle undertow, its wire a black box with one broken panel. The tide held still. The dock took their weight and made no comment. The water to the skiff had never been shorter or longer. Caleb’s boots tapped once, twice, then went still.

“Hurts,” he said finally, nodding toward his burn, as if offering her a place to put some mercy.

“Good,” she said.

He laughed, a sound with no edges.

“Watch your tone,” she added, because he lived on tone.

He watched it.

A heron came off the far mud with its legs trailing like dropped straps. It crossed over them without changing pace. Its shadow went over Caleb’s face and then was gone. The dock, the boat, the bag, the case, the keys in her pocket, the phone warm in her hand—everything put its name on itself and kept it.

Lena stood and did not move, and the water, for the length of a held breath that reached from one piling to the next, did not, either. Caleb sat, wrists turned white around the ties, and the line to the skiff stayed slack as a thought never acted on. She kept him and the dock kept them both. The day arrived, blunt and clean.


==================== SCENE 29 ====================

The funicular track cut a seam up the cliff like a measuring stick, iron teeth biting into stone. Cass crouched at the overlook, knees gone stiff from the cold and the wait, the wooden rail leaching heat from his forearms. Down below, the lower platform slept under a lacquer of tide spray and grit, the carriage stalled mid-run, braked and humming faintly the way machinery hums after being used wrong and made to think about it. The morning had come up hard and colorless. His fingers had to relearn each key on his phone.

“Come on,” he said to the signal bars that stuttered to life, then went away, then held. He slid the envelope from inside his jacket, the one he’d taped against his ribs, and fed its contents to the camera in quick, neat spreads on the bench plank: wire confirmations; escrow disbursement authorizations; screen prints with the black banner of a bank’s portal shorn into screenshots; a ledger page with green gridlines and numbers that didn’t apologize; a transfer memo typed so exactly you could hear the keyboard.

He set a small folding ruler at the edge of each sheet to scale the evidence. He shot them again.

He looked up at the track out of habit, the pulley wheel housing the size of a hay bale filmed with salt rime, the cable fat with grease where palms had found it in the night. He smelled the grease on the wind. He filed that away without adding any story to it.

Signal solid. He compressed, attached. In To: he wrote: Lena. In cc: he added the Breakwater deputy’s address, and Kersey’s. Subject: Escrow disbursements synced to removals | timestamps + chain. He typed the briefest cover he could make himself type.

From: Cass Adler
To: Lena H.
Cc: Deputy R. Vyne, Atty. R. Kersey
Subject: Escrow disbursements synced to removals | timestamps + chain

Lena—

Four transfers out of Kersey IOLTA to “Winn & Sons Marine Services LLC” on dates/times matching funicular removals logged in your board notes and generator alerts (see your 3/24 timestamps). Memos: “temporary relocation—fixtures,” “hoist,” “crating—nursery,” “truck retainer.” Third transfer includes note “C. Wayment cash rec.” Attached: PDFs, screenshots, ledger export. See hashes below. Originals preserved. Call me; I’ll talk you through the chain.

—C

He pasted in the list of checksums, the SHA-256 block looking like a quarrel without vowels, and he added the stills he’d taken of the funicular’s brake housing with grease prints like parentheses around the release lever. He hit send, counted to five, and felt the phone buzz back at him with its small success.

He called as the cloud shadow moved off the platform and light took the rail in a clean stripe.

“Answer,” he said into his shoulder.

Lena answered on the third ring with the sound of a door latch in the background and the hollowness of a large room waking up. “I’m at the desk.”

He could see it in his head, the lighthouse office with the blind slats lifted to a white day, the metal desk with the enamel chipped down to iron, the tray of incoming mail that never saw mail anymore.

The desk lamp was still on when she sat. She slid the inbox key aside without looking. Her inbox threw its hungry line count up like dice. Cass heard her not breathe while it lit.

“Got it,” she said.

She opened the first PDF. It came up like the weather. The bank logo in the corner, the black-and-white visual that made all money the same. Disbursement Authorization—Trust Account (IOLTA). Beneficiary: Winn & Sons Marine Services LLC. Amount: $6,800.00. Memo: temporary relocation—fixtures. Authorized by: R. Kersey. Time stamp: 20:04:16. Execution: 20:15:01. Date: 03/24.

She clicked to the next: $1,900.00—hoist. Then: $4,250.00—crating—nursery. Then: $2,000.00—truck retainer. A note under the third in a fine exact hand: C. Wayment cash rec’d; reconcile on delivery.

Her mouth did not do anything. “You’re sure about the times.”

“UTC in the portal screenshots; local in the ledger export. I aligned them. Your generator alert for 20:16; bell board motion at 20:12; funicular autosense at 20:18. Kersey’s authorization sits in that window. Check page four; there’s the audit log.”

She clicked. His screen captures showed the bank’s own invisible eye: User—R.KERSEY@— Activity—Approval—Time—20:14:37. IP—68.112.15.19. She reached for a pen she didn’t need.

A chime in her speakers: another email landing. From: R. Kersey. Subject: Re: Escrow disbursements synced to removals | timestamps + chain.

Lena docked the window up alongside Cass’s voice and opened the reply. It was rapid, salted with confidence.

From: Atty. R. Kersey
To: Cass Adler
Cc: Lena H., Deputy R. Vyne
Subject: Re: Escrow disbursements synced to removals | timestamps + chain

Mr. Adler—

You are not authorized to access or disseminate trust account information. The referenced disbursements were made in the ordinary course of securing the premises and safeguarding the ward’s estate during an outage—temporary relocation of at-risk items for preservation and inventory. Any insinuation of impropriety is defamatory. Cease and desist.

—R. Kersey

Cass read it in silence. The wind at the overlook took a different note, higher, thin around the rim of the rail.

Lena said, “Opening your attachments now,” as if she had not read Kersey at all.

“Do page nine,” Cass said. “It’s the wire note for ‘crating—nursery.’”

She did. The attached “Vendor Packet” PDF blinked up, fax-shade gray. There was a completed W-9 for Winn & Sons, a one-page service agreement that called everything “as-is,” and, stapled digitally to the back, a text-only email from Kersey to Jonah, CC Ivy: Please coordinate with Caleb re: funicular access. Use ‘nursery—crate’ description for billing. Do not email details.

The deputy’s email chimed a second time. From: Deputy R. Vyne. Subject: Received. He didn’t write anything else. The cc sat on its own like a witness who’d walked into the room and chosen a chair against the wall.

Cass said into the call, “Chain is: vendor packet from Kersey’s account to Jonah’s shop, last Tuesday, 09:43. Disbursement approval Friday night, 20:14. Platform load recorded by your camera at 20:18. Crates off the lower platform at 20:42; I’ve got the gate cam still if you want it. Funds left IOLTA 20:15:01; deposit to Winn & Sons 20:17:49. Your Note: generator auto-start 20:16. That’s four independent clocks agreeing.”

“Open the PNGs,” Lena said. She opened them: the funicular brake housing with gaunt fingerprints around the release, grease shining like thought; the track bed scuffed against fresh paint by crate edges; the lower gate latch with the tiny triangle of gray cloth caught like a flag from a pocket—its weave visible in the macro. Cass’s photos had rulers in them, folds of the bench slat checker-shadowed, the date overlay in the corner set to UTC.

Her phone vibrated in her palm with the deputy’s number. She put it on speaker. “Vyne.”

“Copy your cc,” the deputy said. Water sounds were in his line, flat and slapped. “Seeing the transfers. Adler, where’d you get the account docs?”

“Client packets forwarded by Winn & Sons with their quote,” Cass said. “They cc’d. Kersey forgot to BCC. I grabbed the headers at the time. I’ll forward the full header block. I also exported the portal screens while I had access on the office machine yesterday; I left the machine on and took the network log from the router. The hashes in my email match the originals on a thumb I can place in your hand.” He glanced down the incline instinctively, as if the deputy could see him from the skiff. “Lena can attest to my capture time—I called her at 11:03 when the portal stamped my session.”

“I have the missed call,” Lena said. She clicked through her call history; it was there: 11:03:18. “And my notebook scribble. I wrote ‘IOLTA portal live—Cass’ with the time. Take that with salt, but it’s what I wrote.”

The deputy said, “Headers and router log will hold. Kersey, you still on this thread?”

A ping. The lawyer’s reply came back quickly, words built to withstand.

From: Atty. R. Kersey
To: Deputy R. Vyne
Cc: Lena H., Cass Adler
Subject: Re: Escrow disbursements synced to removals | timestamps + chain

Deputy—

Vendor selection and payment were undertaken to prevent waste and secure evidence during a known outage and storm threat. ‘Crating—nursery’ is an internal descriptor only. No items have been alienated; all are held at the vendor’s facility pending inventory and court instruction. Trust account disbursements for preservation are proper under 1.15. Please direct any further questions through counsel.

—R. Kersey

Cass read the last line twice with the same neutral temperature he gave weather. He said, “Page eleven, Lena.”

She opened it. It was the vendor intake form, scan crooked, Winn & Sons letterhead a fish skeleton with a hook through it. Under Special Handling: “crates to Oliver ferry route staging—no photos; night load OK.” Under Contact Permissions: “Phone only. No email.” In a narrow slanted hand, a note: “Cash tip to C.W. received 40.00—see Kersey rec.”

“I’m reading out the memo fields, Kersey,” Cass said, steady as a metronome. “They’re not internal if they speak to direction. ‘Use nursery—crate for billing. Do not email details.’ You chose the words. The bank recorded the timing. The funicular camera caught the movement. The vendor wrote ‘night load OK.’ That’s not preservation mode. That’s concealment.”

“Mr. Adler,” Kersey wrote back, still in the email window, not the phone, the clicks audible in Lena’s head. “You may not publish my clients’ private arrangements in this manner. If you persist in this fishing expedition, you will be held to account.”

“Stop,” Lena said, not to Cass and not to herself. She forwarded the file marked “Disbursement_Log_Export.csv” to the deputy with no extra language and then, on another thread, she attached two shots of the funicular carriage floor from earlier in the week, her microscope lens overlaid: funicular grease print ridges matching the pattern they’d pulled from a nursery fixture crate. She added one more image—Caleb’s cash receipt for “loading crew—funicular,” date-stamped to the hour of the hoist memo. These had waited in her drafts for the moment that had now arrived. She cc’d Kersey without comment.

The deputy said, “That’s matching, Ms. Lena. You got the original on that cash receipt?”

“In the file box,” she said. “Pulled with the ledgers from the office last night. I photographed each page in sequence before I moved it. I can testify to the chain: I took possession at 22:31; I sealed the box with tamper tape; I logged the seal number and stuck it to my wrist. It’s still on my skin.” She looked down. The tape was there, grimy, its number unkindly clear. She took a picture of her wrist and sent it up the line to the deputy.

Cass knuckled his jaw once against the cold and took one more shot down the track. “Deputy, I can meet you at the head of the trail and hand you the thumb and my notes. The breeze will make you swear.”

“Already swore,” Vyne said, sounding like his mouth was full of the bay. “I’m on the skiff. Be ten. Chain is clear. Funding is clear. Motive’s not a question you need me for, but it’s sitting out on your desk in black type. Ms. Lena, hold that office. Mr. Adler, don’t move your boots.”

Kersey’s reply came again, this time addressed only to Lena and the deputy, leaving Cass off, as if he could unname him.

From: Atty. R. Kersey
To: Deputy R. Vyne, Lena H.
Subject: Re: Escrow disbursements synced to removals | timestamps + chain

Deputy—

I will make myself available by phone at ten hundred hours for any formal inquiry. For now, I ask that you refrain from characterizations and preserve all documents for the court. Any suggestion of “motive” is speculative and prejudicial.

—R. Kersey

Lena replied with the photo of the memo line “C. Wayment cash rec’d; reconcile on delivery” cropped to the words and nothing else. She added no text. A minute later she forwarded the funicular autosense alert—a simple text item, black font on white: CARRIAGE ENGAGED—20:18:03.

She stood up from the desk and went to the window. The boathouse roof lay low and tar-papered; beyond it the flats lay ironed, the water hung as if hung there by a leveler. Caleb was where she had left him, anchored by zip ties and circumstance in the cut shade of the dock rail, head tilted back as if listening to something above him. She could see the wrapped case under the bench, the corner of canvas worn to the handle. She watched him not move. She let the cold in, out, in.

“Cass,” she said, returning to the phone, “call him.”

Cass blinked once at the cliff face and the line of birds making their deliberative way against the grain of the wind. “Kersey?”

“Put it in his ear. No more emails to push around. Make him hear the times in the order they happened. I want to listen to him say no to a clock.”

“Copy,” Cass said. He scrolled and tapped, then merged the call when it lifted on the third ring with a clipped “Kersey.”

“Kersey,” Cass said, voice like a level on a workbench. “I’m on speaker with Lena. Deputy is on the cc. I’m going to read you four times. Tell me which one is ‘preservation.’ Ready?”

“I object to the—”

“20:14:37: you authorize a trust disbursement. 20:16: generator auto-start logs. 20:18:03: funicular carriage engages. 20:42: lower gate opens and the carriage comes down with crated nursery fixtures. Your vendor intake states ‘night load OK—no photos.’ Your memo line references cash to C. Wayment.”

Silence, except for the line noise of a body of water doing what it wanted. The deputy’s breathing, slow as oars. Lena’s hand on the desk, one knuckle pressing the wood grain to remember it.

“Mr. Adler,” Kersey said after a beat that had no give in it, “you seem to think you’re a tribunal. You are not. You are a trespasser with a camera. The estate directed a vendor to stabilize and relocate until we could properly inventory during daylight hours, with a sheriff’s presence, which you and your companion have made necessary with your interruptions. The funds used were lawfully reserved for this purpose. If Mr. Wayment accepted personal cash, that is his affair and he will answer for it to his employer.”

“And your memo line?” Lena said, stepping closer to the phone, a flatness in her voice like iced metal. “Your hand writes ‘reconcile on delivery.’ Delivery where.”

“To the secure facility,” Kersey said. “Which I have already named. The one I will not name again over unsecured lines.”

“Winn & Sons,” Cass said. “Three miles inland at the old cannery lot. Night gate code in Ivy’s text to Jonah at 19:57. I’ve got the screenshot. The chain is closed.”

“Which you have obtained unlawfully.”

“You attached us by accident,” Cass said.

Again, the small muscle in the call that meant a pivot. “This is finished,” Kersey said. “Deputy, I will present receipts and contracts in an orderly fashion when you are here in person. In the meantime, stand down and prevent Ms. Hart from further interfering with my clients.”

“Noted,” the deputy said in a voice set like a compass. “We’ll be in touch in person. Hold your phone.”

The call dropped on Kersey’s end with the little merciless chirp all hang-ups make.

Cass let the quiet pour back into his ear. The wind rattled the overlook’s stale prayer flags of tape and old caution ribbon. He took the envelope again and slid the last two sheets back into it. He stood, his knees complaining. He looked down at the stalled carriage, at the run licked clean by morning.

“Lena,” he said.

“I know,” she said. She clicked the print icon she had not wanted to click, and paper started to come. The machine was old; it worked as if each page were some small difficult truth it had to get over itself to make.

On the fourth sheet, just above the memo “crating—nursery,” the fax machine put a faint heat across the paper that made the ink look alive for half a second. Lena tapped each page to square them, set them in a pile. She took a dated photo of the stack with the desk clock in frame and the tape on her wrist visible. She wrote across the top sheet: RECEIVED, 07:18, L.H.

The deputy’s voice came back, wind-shaved. “Two minutes out,” he said. “Hand to hand.”

“Front office,” Lena said.

Cass folded the empty envelope and slid it flat into his pocket. He looked one more time at the funicular, the track stitching cliff to shore. He wasn’t waiting for anything to reveal itself. He was satisfied with what the paper said. He put his phone in his jacket, zipped, and started the climb down toward the trailhead, boots rapping the planks, the cold finding his teeth and not letting go.


==================== SCENE 30 ====================
The paneled wall didn’t look like anything except expensive stubbornness. Dark oak, shellacked until it refused to show grain, with a run of framed regatta photographs no one had dusted since last season. Lena pressed her thumb to the little crescent divot Eleanor had worn into the edge of the seventh panel from the right.

“It sticks,” Mara said quietly. “You have to lean like you’re asking, not telling.”

“Since when do walls like being asked?”

“Since Eleanor,” Mara said. “On three.”

They both put shoulders to the seam. The panel sighed like a tired chest and slid inward the width of a palm. Cold air puffed out. The smell was cedar shavings and old carbon paper and something faintly medicinal—wintergreen, from a jar on the shelf with a cracked glass stopper.

“Careful,” Mara said. She reached in and found the cord: a bell pull stripped of its tassel. She tugged sideways, not down. Somewhere a latch let go with a click that felt private. The panel traveled the rest of the way, and the alcove opened enough to take a person.

The space was something Eleanor had willed into the house by force of wanting it—half a closet carved behind the wall, lined in unfinished tongue-and-groove and lit by a single under-cabinet strip Eleanor must have wired herself. A schoolhouse chair sat in front of a narrow desk built into the back wall, the wood darkened where elbows had lived. The desk was only as deep as a shoebox, but neatness had made a vault of it: ledger books stacked like bricks, blotter, carpenter pencil sharpened with a knife, a gum eraser the color of an old peach. The left side shelves were labeled with masking tape in Eleanor’s tidy block letters: BOAT. BELL. HOUSE. GIFTS. LOANS (E). LOANS (OTH).

Mara reached in with both hands and took down the top book from LOANS (E). She set it on the desk and flipped the front cover back with two thumbs like breaking the spine of a fish. Each page was ruled in blue and black, the headings hand-inked: Date. Item. To/From. Terms. Witness. Notes.

“She kept two sets,” Mara said. “This is the one no one was supposed to have to see.”

Lena stood sideways to fit and used the wall to edge in until she could stand over Mara. Her wrist tape tugged as she braced. She didn’t touch, yet. She read.

08/12 — Ship’s bell clapper—bronze (Gleason Foundry mark) — To: J. Hart — Terms: overnight for casting measurements — Witness: M — Notes: returned nicked; no fee.

08/26 — Windlass pawl—iron—To: Kersey, E—Terms: documentation only—Witness: V (Dr. Vale)—Notes: not returned; invoice disputed.

09/03 — “Oliver” note—graphite transfer—From: unknown—Terms: evidence hold—Witness: C (Cass)—Notes: see journal O-14.

Mara’s finger went further down the page where the tidy writing turned harder for a line, like the nib had been angry.

09/10 — Polished teak cradle (nursery)—To: C. Hart (Caleb)—Terms: repair—Witness: M—Notes: moved to shed; see photo.

She turned the page. The date columns kept their pulse. “She tracked everything,” Mara said. “Even when they told her it was ‘confusing’ to keep duplicates.”

“Who told her that,” Lena said, though she could taste the names.

Mara kept her finger moving. “Kersey. Ivy. Depends on the day. Mostly they said she was making work for herself. That she didn’t need to.”

The alcove’s light hummed. The wind under the east windows made a low seethe. Somewhere the fax machine in the front office stuttered like a dying cicada and fell silent. Lena felt the house shift and resettle around them, all its weight trying to decide what to hold and what to let go.

“Here,” Mara said. “Loans with dates.” She pulled the next ledger from LOANS (OTH). She had to put the first on the chair seat to make room. This cover was smudged with thumbprints in a pattern that meant use, not neglect. Inside, the headings were the same, but the Notes column was fuller, denser, as if the truth had to be wedged in sideways.

She lifted a page. There were three entries in a row that had all been starred later in red pencil:

10/02 — Bell board panel—oak—To: Kersey, retainer hold—Terms: ‘securing for evaluation’—Witness: I (Ivy)—Notes: promise return by 10/07; signed.

10/02 — Coal chute gate—iron—To: J. Hart (Jonah)—Terms: salvage ‘loan’—Witness: Caleb—Notes: no slip provided; verbal.

10/02 — Lantern pair—brass—To: C. Hart—Terms: show to dealer—Witness: Ivy—Notes: no documentation.

Mara slid a folded paper out of the back pocket of the ledger. It was a carbon of a carbon, the purple of it gone more lavender. “This is what she made me file after,” Mara said. “She insisted.”

She handed it to Lena. “Escrow transfers—Kersey Retainer Trust,” Lena said. “Cass forwarded these.” She could see the dates at the top, time stamps like a stutter in a heartbeat: 10/02 19:52. 10/02 20:05. 10/03 09:17. The amounts were clean, not meant to shock anyone: $2,800. $1,400. $3,200. Enough to pass as business if you didn’t know the house’s breath.

Mara pulled another ledger: GIFTS. Eleanor’s writing slowed there, got gentler. Weddings and baptisms and a scallop shell given to a girl on her thirteenth because she had asked good questions about tides. Names in ink, little check marks when the thank-you note came. Mara set it aside and went back to LOANS (E), turning to the back where Eleanor had taped in envelopes with string ties. She undid one and slid out a photograph.

The photo was in black-and-white, but not for effect; Eleanor’s ancient Polaroid had been fixed to shoot whatever film she could find in the thrift store. The funicular carriage was in the background. In the foreground, a length of wrapped teak on a dolly, the cradles’ rails peeking through paper like a bare shoulder. Caleb’s boot at the edge of frame, heel scarred. A white grease fingerprint on the paper, oval like a coin.

“She put the dates on the backs,” Mara said. She turned it over and there was the blocky, insistent 10/04, and then: ‘moved w/o slip—ask again.’

“She asked again?” Lena said.

“Every morning,” Mara said. “She made it part of the list she kept in her apron pocket. Same as the kitchen order. Flour, sugar, cradle.”

She set the photograph down in a safe spot and reached higher to the shelf that read HOUSE. The topmost binder was thinner than the rest, with a heavy elastic around it and a label: ESCROWS—CHECK. She undid the elastic and opened to the first page. Eleanor’s columns again, but with a new header added in pencil in the margin: ‘follow money.’

There were dates and bank names and ledger numbers and, against each, what looked like a thing that had moved that week. 09/28—Anchor chain length sold—notation arrow to ‘deposit to Kersey RT $1,800.’ 10/02—Bell board panel ‘secured’—arrow to ‘transfer RT $2,800.’ 10/03—Lantern pair ‘show to dealer’—arrow to ‘RT $1,400’ and then a question mark so hard the paper had torn.

Mara blew out a breath through her nose. “She wasn’t sleeping,” she said, but it wasn’t pity. It was an acceptance of the hours Eleanor had turned into lines.

Lena took her phone out of her jacket and held it over the page. She hesitated just once. “We do this now?” She kept her voice low. The alcove picked up sound and held it like a confession.

Mara nodded. “I’m done pretending. If they want to call this interference, they can do it to my face.”

Lena snapped the photo, the shutter sound smothered in the small space. She took another, wider, with the column headings, the arrows, the question mark. She made sure the time was on the screen.

Mara flipped to the next page. 10/07—Nursery bronze hooks ‘for repair’—arrow to RT $900—note: ‘no work order; asked C twice.’ 10/09—Funicular brake ratchet ‘evaluation’—arrow to $2,200—note: ‘removed during king tide—unsafe.’ A little exclamation mark this time, almost embarrassed at itself but there.

“Look.” Lena put the escrow printout Cass had sent next to the ledger. The printed bank line items sat like gray stones. She lined the ledger dates up with the lines. They matched. The ridiculous satisfaction of a column of numbers reconciling.

Mara worked her jaw. “She knew to follow the retainer trust because Kersey made a joke about ‘parking it for safekeeping.’ She said, ‘Nothing is safe if it isn’t visible.’”

“She was right,” Lena said. She took another photo and another until the phone chirped a low battery warning, and she slid it into the inside pocket where heat might keep it alive a little longer.

Mara went back to LOANS (OTH) and found a page with a smear where a hand had rested on wet ink. 10/12—Bait-shop ledger (copy) ‘borrowed for comparison’—To: C—Terms: 24 hours—Witness: L—Notes: leaked page; returned swollen. Lena remembered the hour she’d had it under a fan. She smiled, quick and involuntary, then let it go.

“You show me the ones with Ivy,” Lena said.

Mara turned more pages, careful and fast. “Here.” 10/13—Nursery rocker dowels—To: Ivy—Terms: ‘in her car’—Witness: none—Notes: asked for slip; told ‘later.’ Then a red pencil bracket closing around the entry and one word: NO.

Mara tapped the NO with the flat of her nail. “She started writing that when she felt cornered. She would say it under her breath and write it in the margins. She said if the paper said it, then she didn’t have to spend her voice.”

Lena reached past her and touched the red pencil still threaded through the ledger’s spine as a bookmark. It left a dust on her fingertip. She wiped it on her jeans. She moved to the shelf marked BELL and pulled a slim ledger so old it had gone soft at the edges. Inside were drawings—Eleanor had sketched the bell board and numbered the wires, the way an old-school skipper draws his anchorages in a homemade chart. Next to each wire name, a tiny circle: intact, intact, scored. The word scored was underlined twice. A date next to it: 09/29. An arrow to ‘generator auto-start.’

“Cass’s lightbox shows the transfers. Eleanor’s wiring notes tell the rest,” Lena said. She set the bell ledger aside as carefully as she could without reverence folding into her hands.

Mara looked past her to the slit of the room beyond the panel. She listened. House noises, wind, the clink of a loose spoon somewhere. No boots. No ring on the nursery line.

“If we take them out,” she said, “they’ll say we tampered.”

“I’m not taking them,” Lena said. “I’m taking pictures.”

Mara nodded, then reached to the backmost shelf, behind a slim row of meaningless dictionaries Eleanor had stacked as decoys. Her hand found the thing she’d put there four months ago when Eleanor had made her swear that if it ever came to it she would know where. It was a cloth-wrapped bundle tied with twine. The twine had been looped twice to make a figure-eight that caught on itself in a neat trick that could be undone with one pull. Mara pulled. The cloth fell open on the desk, revealing a ledger thinner than all the others, with a front cover cut away to reveal a square window under which lay a pressed fern. The label under the fern said simply: PRIVATE—M.

Mara didn’t look at Lena. She opened it.

The handwriting was Mara’s.

The first page was a list she had kept for herself in the months when Eleanor’s hands had shaken too much to write every number, or when the argument wore her down. It was blunt, almost ungrammatical in places, written for speed and memory: 10/02 clang proof—Caleb w Jonah took panel. 10/03 Ivy had trunk open in courtyard—lanterns in blanket. 10/04 Kersey said “chain of custody” — put cradle on dolly anyway. 10/05 Caleb to shed at 22:13—no paperwork.

Mara went to the back where she had pasted screenshots she had printed from the office computer when the bank portal had been open on Ivy’s laptop. She had centered the printouts and taped them with little flags of blue tape. RT ledger numbers glowed faintly in grayscale. She had written to the side: matches 10/02; matches 10/07; ask Cass for full.

She pushed the ledger toward Lena. “You won’t have this from anyone else,” she said, voice level. “If they want to call me disloyal, let them. Eleanor taught me to pay attention. That’s all this is.”

Lena felt the point of it in her chest—not gratitude exactly, but a steadiness. “This is enough,” she said. “More than enough.”

Mara exhaled. “I know what they do when they’re scared. I’ve seen it.” She lifted her eyes to Lena’s. “You’re holding the light. Keep it steady. I’ll open what you need.”

They worked quiet then, the way a kitchen becomes quiet when the last rush has passed and the work left is cleaning and putting things in their places. Lena photographed pages, dates, screenshots. Mara turned leaves, untied and retied the cloth wrap to make a little cradle so nothing touched the desk that didn’t need to.

At one point, Mara paused with her finger on an entry that had torn through the paper where the pen had gone too hard: 10/11—Generator shed padlock replaced—To: Jonah—Terms: ‘security’—Notes: funicular lock shows scoring. She swallowed and shook her head once the way you dislodge an old pain, then moved on.

They reached the end of ESCROWS—CHECK and there, on the last page, Eleanor had totaled a column and written in the margin: reconcile at hearing. Under that: bring witnesses. There was a small doodle of a bell with a crack drawn in and then erased.

“Dates align,” Lena said. She laid Cass’s printouts left to the ledger. “Every time something moved, Kersey’s retainer account twitched like a fish.”

Mara did not smile. “Then we are not guessing.”

“No.”

Mara lifted the red pencil and drew a clean line down the ledger’s margin from the first 10/02 to the last 10/13. It wasn’t dramatic. It was a line a teacher would make when she was showing a student where the subject of a sentence ran. She capped the pencil—though it didn’t have a cap—and slid it back.

“Close it,” she said. “Put your phone in your pocket. Help me set it back how she had it.”

They stacked the ledgers in their exact bricks. The schoolhouse chair creaked as if relieved to be empty again. Mara flicked the under-cabinet light off and the alcove went to dim, the smell of cedar deepening without the electric hum. She pulled the bell cord sideways, gently, and the latch caught. They shouldered the panel until the mercury of the seam smoothed away.

In the hallway, the house resumed being a hallway. Regatta photos on the oak. Dust. The scratch on the floor where a trunk had once dragged.

Mara wiped her hands on her jeans and looked at Lena. Her face had settled into something that would not move unless she told it to.

“Front office?” she said.

“Front office,” Lena said.

They walked together, not slow. In the office, the fax stack lay with Lena’s RECEIVED at the top. She set the phone photos uploading as she spoke the file names to herself under her breath: LOANS_E_pg1. ESCROW_check_pg3. BELL_wires_0929. MARA_private_pg4.

Mara stood at the doorway, eyes on the corridor, listening. The morning light off the water made the lines on the floorboards look like all the lines they had just read.

“It corroborates,” Lena said, not for the tape recorder, just for the room. “Pattern’s clear. Dates line up with escrow. Fractions match whole.”

Mara nodded. “Then we’re done hiding,” she said. Her voice wasn’t loud and it didn’t ask for agreement. It was a thing put on a shelf in plain sight.

“Then we’re done hiding,” Lena said back.

Mara took the top sheet of the fax stack and added to Lena’s writing with one line in her own block hand: SEE LEDGERS—HOUSE ALCOVE. Initialed: M.S.

She set the pen down. She didn’t look toward the paneled wall when they left the office, but as they passed the regatta photos she reached up and straightened one that hung one degree off, a small correction in a house of small corrections. They walked toward the front without hurry. The tide was slack enough you could almost hear the mud breathe. The air in the hall was cold and clean.


==================== SCENE 31 ====================
The fog lay like a wool blanket on the slope, muting the clank of the funicular’s top pulleys to a damp heartbeat. The track head was a platform of old planks black with salt and iron dust, a winch house tucked under a nest of cables, and the squared mouth of a chute that used to feed coal to a pier that wasn’t there anymore. Over the side, the rails dropped toward the cove in a straight gleam that disappeared into vapor.

Jonah stood with his hands tucked in his sleeves, shoulders up like he expected rain even if the sky couldn’t commit. He had a scratch on his cheek that had wept and dried white. One cuff was darker than the other. He looked like a man who had slept in a small chair with his head against a cold window.

“You’re early,” he said.

“It’s dawn,” Lena said. She set the case down on the platform and opened it. Inside: a yellow tape, a grease pencil, her phone already recording, a bag of numbered tags, two straps with cam buckles, and a stapled incident form with coffee on the corner.

“Deputy?” Jonah asked without moving his head.

“On the skiff,” Lena said. “Men in boats get here when the water lets them.”

He took that in, eyes dropping to the case. He didn’t look at the phone. He did turn, just a fraction, toward the track where the car sat two-thirds up, cradling three crates banded in black—a highchair with carved arms. A painted cupboard door with yellow tulips. Something heavy with a brass bracket showing at the corner. Everything tied with the same cheap cord you get at a bait shop.

“You said—” he started.

“I said I’d include your cooperation,” Lena said, neat as shelving.

He exhaled through his nose, then sniffed like the wind had shifted. It hadn’t.

“They were going tonight,” he said. “If the tide had stayed high enough. If the fog held.” His mouth jerked at one corner. “If, if, if.”

“Route,” Lena said.

He rubbed his eyes once with his thumb and ring finger like he was trying to pinch awake. “Boathouse to sea lane,” he said, steady now. “Ride the rack down on the quarter and move to the tunnel on the trough. You wait for that big intake in the cave, the one that’s like a bellows. It pulls the echo so you can open the gate without anybody hearing the latch. Then you slide the skiff under the light shelf. That shelf’s important. Doesn’t catch light from the lanterns. You hand along the chain—they left the chain slack on purpose—and you’re in the dark before anybody at the breakwater can say who was on the beach.”

“Names,” Lena said.

“Caleb on the subpanel,” Jonah said. He didn’t hesitate. “Kersey on the phone. Ivy on timing. I don’t know who else at the big end. I didn’t ask. I didn’t want to know.”

“You knew enough,” Lena said.

He looked up at her then and it was all the heat he’d let out all morning. “I knew enough to carry what I was told to carry. I knew enough to keep it from drowning. You think that’s nothing? You think I didn’t—” He cut it, jaw jumping once. “You wanted the route.”

She set the tape measure in his hand and held her phone under it. The red light blinked. “Start at the boathouse door,” she said. “Words. Then numbers.”

He nodded, swallowing. “Boathouse door goes quiet at zero-eight past,” he said, eyes going to the winch house like the clock he was seeing was in there. “We used the bell board to cover sound if we had to—pull the nursery bell once, drag chain. No more than twice. Generator auto-start hits at the half if nobody resets it, so we never pulled between and half. We waited for the crank to stutter so it would mask.”

“Your path,” she said.

“Door, skids, rail bed on the east side, toes on the ties. That keeps you off the shine if somebody’s looking from the bluff.” He walked as he talked, his body remembering the angle, the way to put a foot where it wouldn’t creak. “You keep your shoulder below the cable light so you’re a shadow, not a shape. When the car’s at the second stanchion”—he pointed to a rusted iron post—“you’re clear of the house windows. After that you can stand up if you have to lift. We lifted at the third, always. That’s where the grade starts to favor you. Take it down one, lock on the catch, breathe. Two more on the brake so it doesn’t scream. At the bottom, don’t step on the flat plate. It bangs. You walk the side gravel. The tunnel inlet’s armpit height right now with slack. That changes. On king, you crouch. On neap, you can walk bent. Today? You’d get your hair wet.”

“Distances,” Lena said.

He thumbed the tape out, hooked it on the weathered edge of the platform just to have something to catch. He pulled to the stanchion. The tape rattled. “Fourteen feet six to the first post,” he said. “To the second it’s another sixteen four. To the brake lever,” he stepped along, careful, the cable thrumming, “thirty-three even from the house, if you measure the way my uncle taught me, which is: to where your foot knows it without looking.”

Lena didn’t argue with that. She drew the grease pencil across a laminated printout of the cove that she’d stuck to a clipboard, marking in tight, knifed letters: fourteen-six, sixteen-four, thirty-three. She shot the marks with her phone and kept the frame on his hands.

“You disguised the crates how?”

“As scrap,” he said. “Old paint. Sacking. They still look like what they are if you know what you’re looking at. But people don’t if they don’t want to.”

“The transfers,” she said. “You got paid where?”

“I didn’t say I got paid,” Jonah said, automatic teeth clench.

Lena just looked at him.

“In kind,” he said. “Boat fuel, cash posted on the chalkboard at the bait shop. Cash on the Friday. Not mine, you hear? They said it was the museum’s. That it was just—” He made a shape with his hands, as if the lie had a geometry he could hold. “I know what it was. I know it now.”

“Timing,” Lena said. “From your first lift to the tunnel mouth.”

“Seven minutes if it’s just one crate,” he said. His voice got sure again when it was the physics. “Ten with three and nobody panics. You panic, you pull the brake too hard and you burn the shoe and everybody for a mile hears it. We never did. We went slow enough that if a gull called you could pretend that was the noise anybody heard.”

“And the skiff went where?”

He glanced at her. “You don’t know, or you want me to say it.”

“Say it.”

“South face around the black tooth,” he said. “There’s a line you can take that keeps you in the lee even when the tide bounces the outer stones. Hug the straight line until the red marker looks like it’s leaning. Then cut across in the backwash where the foam covers your wake. You can put a crate in that hollow on the kelp side and from shore it looks like the sea’s got a bruise, not a box. If you go later, the lobster guys will see you from their decks. You go then, you’re just a fool.”

“Landing?”

“Breakwater’s cut has a dead angle. You nose under it and your mast—if you’re a mast man—ticks the beam. We had no mast. We kept it low. We put blankets down so the brass wouldn’t ring. We never stopped talking,” he added. “You keep talking like you’re telling a joke, you look like you belong.”

Lena’s pencil paused. “Who’s we?”

“Just me,” he said. “Just a habit.”

She didn’t call him on it. She lifted the phone, framed his face, waited. “Say it again from the top,” she said. “Boathouse to skiff to cut. No poetry.”

He did. Slow, flat, like a list. She made him add clock times. He did that too. When he got to the part about the bell board, he looked down, then made his voice bored, like he was telling her how to clean a stove. “Caleb yanked the nursery bell wire to pull them upstairs when we moved.
