Kersey called the deputy once to ‘report a disturbance’ on the wrong side of the island so the road would be occupied. Ivy jammed your radio. We didn’t get the deputy. The causeway had water. That was the lucky part. It wasn’t skill.”

“You rehearsed this,” Lena said.

“Yeah,” he said. “And then we did it.”

The fog thinned in a long breath that revealed the car and the slate water and the faint white of the surf kissing rocks far out. The crates looked smaller with all that distance behind them.

“Open them,” Lena said.

“You’re documenting first,” he said. “You said you’d—”

“You’re opening them so I can see they’re what they are and so they don’t leave this platform without a photograph. That’s your price.”

He nodded and went to the car. He flipped his hood up and leaned into the wet ropes. He cut the bait-shop cord with a folding knife and peeled back burlap. The highchair’s finial shone under the rot—little dolphins carved on the arms, scratched with generations of small feet. He brushed his knuckle along the back, a motion he made small.

“Next,” Lena said.

He pulled the second cover. The cupboard door’s tulips were bright where the sun had never hit, the yellow un-faded as yolk. He slid the third crate to the edge and split it. The brass bracket had four screw holes and an engraved letter: E.

Lena shot them all, front, side, back, with the crate slats measured against the tape. She read out the numbers and the time. She slapped numbered tags on each piece and wrote in block letters: RECOVERED AT TRACK HEAD, DAWN SLACK. She handed Jonah the other strap. He threaded it under the car’s base.

“You’ll keep them here till the deputy—” he said.

“Till he takes custody,” Lena said.

“And he’ll—”

“That’s not my part.” She ratcheted the cam tight. The strap sang a low plastic note.

They both stood with their hands on the crates like people at a small altar, then stepped back.

“You’re writing it down that I told you before they went,” Jonah said. “You’re putting that in.”

“I am,” Lena said.

“Because I’m not going to jail for the rest of them. I’m not. I’ll say what I did. I’ll say who told me. I’ll say what I carried and when. I’ll say what the route was, what the timings were, I’ll say the thing about the bell because it was true. I’ll say Caleb tripped the breaker and I’ll say Kersey said ‘museum’ like it made it okay and I’ll say Ivy used her own name because she thought you’d forget it if she said it like a dare.” He stopped. His breath made little ghosts in the air. “But I’m not going to say I knew what they were going to do with her. I didn’t. I didn’t ask. I didn’t want to.”

“You’re saying what you’re saying,” Lena said. “I’m recording exactly that.”

He nodded, jaw doing that jump again. He looked down the track. “You zip-tied Caleb?” he asked, without rancor.

“By the boathouse,” she said. “He’ll be there when we get down.”

Jonah rubbed at the scratch on his cheek until it went pink. “He’ll spit,” he said.

“Then he’ll spit.”

“You going to ask me why I didn’t stop the car last night when I could have?” he asked.

“No,” she said.

He stood there a second with that. Then he moved. “You want chalk?” he said. “I marked the safe steps with chalk when it was wet. Rains pocked it. But you can see where my heel hit.” He dug in his pocket, came up with a half-stick blue as a storm sky. He snapped it into a sharp, new edge and handed it over.

“You mark,” Lena said.

“I’m not—” He stopped fighting himself because it cost less to do it. He bent and drew a short dash on the plank where a nail head rose. Another on the tie where sap had bled. He went down three steps, stopping to mark the shadow line that would hide a man. “You put your foot here,” he said quietly. “Here. And here. You don’t look up when you hear the bell because it will make you look like a man hearing a bell. You do what you were doing.”

She followed with the clipboard, not looking up when the cable sighed.

At the winch house, she took photos of the brake lever, the trip shank, the notch where a heavier man had thrown the lever too hard and bit the iron. “You taped the bell wire here?” she asked, pointing to a knot worn shiny.

“Caleb taped it,” he said. “I just held the bundle. He said it was temporary, but he didn’t take it down.”

Lena peeled a piece of blue tape off the wire and folded it into a square like a small flag. She stuck it to the incident form. It looked ridiculous and permanent.

A gull landed on the cable and swung three inches and then stilled, yellow eye bright. Jonah glanced up at it and let his mouth crease. “That one knows me,” he said. “They know the car. They ride it for fun.”

“Names,” Lena said, bringing him back.

“Caleb. Kersey. Ivy,” he said, like a catechism. He looked toward the house on the hill without moving his boots. “And me.”

The fog peeled back another layer. The cove tucked itself in, the black tooth showing dark against the steely water. You could see how a boat would slip behind it and be gone from the breakwater’s eyes.

“Write your initials next to your marks,” Lena said, passing him the grease pencil.

“They’ll say I did it all,” he said as he took it.

“They’ll say lots of things,” she said. “Write.”

He wrote J.R. at the top plank where the chalk said to put a foot. He wrote it again at the second stanchion under the bolt that had tasted his skin once when he’d misjudged a night and the tide had made the rail sweat. He wrote it small and clean, a mechanic’s mark.

The radio on Lena’s hip coughed and then cleared, a voice on the other end doing the staccato that meant the skiff was inside the point and the motor had a mind of its own. She didn’t answer. She didn’t need to. The straps were done. The tags were, too. The route, in his voice, lived on the phone. The chalk said the rest.

Jonah put his hands in his sleeves again. He looked at the crates, at the car, at the mark of his initials like something that could be found later and identified as his but not only his. “You’re going to make me carry them back up,” he said.

“Yes,” Lena said.

He nodded, like that was fair. He blew out a breath that didn’t leave fog. “You want help with Caleb?” he asked.

“I like him right where he is,” she said.

He almost smiled and then didn’t. “Okay,” he said. He put his shoulder to the car. Lena took the other side. The brake creaked under her palm. The cable throbbed. The car didn’t care about whatever men had said to other men; it cared about weight and slope and friction.

They lifted together, in the cold clean air, and the crates stayed on the platform, banded, tagged, documented, waiting for the boots on the planks that would come when the water let them.


==================== SCENE 32 ====================
The office had two clocks that disagreed by nine minutes, both ticking, both insisting. The windows were old glass, full of tiny waves that made the fog outside look like it was being pulled on a loom. A tide chart, pinned by a brass tack, lay under a blotter that had been used as if blots were a profession: rings of ink, a triangular burn. On the wall, a framed menu from a long-closed ferry café, prices written in chalk cursive, hung crooked over a nailed-up shelf of warranties and folded maps.

Lena stood behind the desk as if she’d been sent there to take dictation. The laptop she’d scavenged from Cass sat on the blotter, bright enough to make its own weather. The subject line was anemic and gray and final: RE: Re: Capacity Evaluation Letter.

Kersey didn’t sit. He dripped into the doorway, rain beading on the shoulders of his wool coat, and kept one gloved hand on the jamb as if he meant to leave again in the middle of a sentence. He took in the room—ledgers, file boxes banded with masking tape, the metal lockbox open like a small empty mouth—and then the screen.

“What is this,” he said, not a question.

“An email,” Lena said.

“From who,” he said, although the preview pane said it in black: Vale, Ruth A., M.D.

“Read,” Lena said.

She turned the laptop toward him and stepped back. Kersey pushed off the jamb and crossed the rug with the slow careful step of a man who knows his shoes will stain a good thing and does it anyway. He bent at the waist. His coat creaked.

He read it. There was no performance in his reading. His face did not register the stages an actor would give you. Only his breathing slowed.

Dr. Vale’s email began three lines down from Lena’s last ask. It had the shape of letters written by people who do this every day and value their licenses more than they value flourish.

Ms. Anders— Thank you for your message and attachments. Upon review of the additional materials and records disclosed this morning and in view of the circumstances of my prior involvement, I am withdrawing my letter dated [redacted], and any opinions stated therein regarding Ms. Eleanor Myles’s capacity. My preliminary observations were made without a complete, independent examination and without access to the full medical record, and I have learned information suggesting potential conflicts and procedural irregularities in the referral and constraints placed around my contact with Ms. Myles. In light of these facts, I cannot support any petition or application that relies on my previous letter. Please accept this email as formal withdrawal. I have notified counsel of record. I regret any confusion my earlier communication may have caused. Respectfully, Ruth A. Vale, M.D. Massachusetts License No. [redacted] CC: K. Kersey, Esq.; I. Blight

Kersey read it twice to see if it changed the second time. It didn’t.

“Call her,” he said.

“It’s dawn,” Lena said.

“She copied me,” he said, as if the CC meant an invitation.

“She copied you to make a record,” Lena said.

He straightened. His jaw clicked. He pulled his phone out of his inside pocket as if drawing a light weapon he preferred not to use. His thumb made three choices, and then he hit speaker.

The ring tone did not bring anything in from the fog. It rang, then the clean voice of a receptionist—early, pale with fluorescent light—gave an extension. Kersey pressed nothing. It rang again. Voicemail. The doctor’s recorded voice was plain, almost dry: You’ve reached Dr. Ruth Vale. Please leave a message for callback. If this is an emergency—

He didn’t leave one. He cut the call and put the phone facedown on the desk, a dark slab.

“Professional risk,” he said, to the glass. “Why now.”

“You already know why,” Lena said.

He glanced at the lockbox again, at the rubber-banded stacks inside with names written by other hands—Mara’s tight lines, Cass’s long slanting notes, numbers, dates, the escrow transfers printed neat with bank logos. Documents like weather. He looked back at the screen.

“Withdraw is a particular verb,” he said, clipped. “People amend. They clarify. Withdraw is extreme.”

“She wrote withdraw,” Lena said.

“You sent her the ledgers,” he said.

“I sent her context,” Lena said. “The watch. Your Friday. The blackout timing. The limits on access. The blockages.”

“She evaluated in person,” he said, automatic. “The letter stated—”

“It stated she spoke to Eleanor with the door propped and a hallway between. A staffer present. Ten minutes.” Lena’s voice was flat, bureaucratic. “You put in your petition that there was ‘direct observation’ and ‘deterioration consistent with dementia’ underweighting the environmental pressures. The recordings show otherwise.”

“She isn’t her neurologist,” he said, as if to himself, as if the wall might offer a footnote. “She consults. The court asks. We supply.”

“You supplied constraints,” Lena said.

He looked at her then, direct, a man measuring whether it would be wise to say what he was thinking. He didn’t, because wise was all that was left. “So you’re moving this to the clerk,” he said.

“I already did,” Lena said. “The deputy too.”

He had to sit then, because the knees went soft in ways men like him don’t permit. He chose the chair by the filing cabinet, a steel thing that squealed once and then took him in with a grievance. He took his gloves off with one slow pull per finger and laid them on his knee, straightened them, made them behave. The two clocks ticked, out of sync, like a bad heart.

“Do you understand the reputational cascade you are engineering,” he said. It was almost clinical, stripped of furniture.

“Dr. Vale engineered her own,” Lena said. “By reading. By choosing.”

He pressed two fingers into his left temple. The rain made tiny tapping sounds on the glass, a Morse. He looked back to the screen but didn’t read it again.

“You think I put my name on a thing like this if I believed it wouldn’t hold,” he said.

“Yes,” Lena said.

He exhaled, a decision falling through his body. The water outside lifted and eased along the edge of the rocks. He watched it not watch him.

“You’ll file a notice,” he said to the room.

“I said I did,” Lena said.

“And you’ll push for dismissal,” he said.

“I’ll let the record show what it shows,” Lena said. “The medical support is gone.”

He flinched at the phrase, the legal tendon of it. “There are other physicians,” he said, because men like him never run out of possible verbs.

“Dr. Vale was your bulwark,” Lena said.

He reached for his phone again, but didn’t pick it up. The habit of spinning out alternate paths beat once and then lost its rhythm. He shifted, looked down at the gloves like they might suggest a script.

“You go to court this morning with what,” he asked. “Ledgers and a skiff and a zip tie.”

“A withdrawal,” Lena said.

Something old in the office—a condenser in the wall, a voice tube to the kitchen—clicked. The heat came on and put its thin breath behind them. The clocks disagreed in the same way they had before.

Lena tapped the trackpad and brought up the sent mail. She didn’t turn the screen this time; she narrated for a man who needed nouns and dates to feel less like air.

“Filed to the Probate Registry with Dr. Vale’s withdrawal attached. Copied to you, to Ivy, to the deputy, to the judge’s assistant. Acknowledgment received. Case number in the header. Time stamp four minutes ago. Receipt attached.”

He took that in. His eyes moved left to right, though he was reading nothing.

“You will use this to allege impropriety,” he said, and the way he said allege told you what floor he lived on.

“I will use it to stop a removal,” Lena said.

He stood. The chair made its small complaint again. He looked older by a notch or two, the notches you notice only if you are also counting ropes on a dock. He slid his gloves back on his hands with a care that was not softness.

“Then we’re done here,” he said, formal as a closing letter.

She nodded once. “For now,” she said, which wasn’t a threat or a promise, only a calendar entry.

He lifted the phone, pocketed it. He scanned the desk with a final, acquisitive look, as if something might have grown out of the paper since he’d walked in. The mask that had cracked a millimeter reasserted. He adjusted the knot of his tie, though it didn’t require adjustment.

“You think you’ve done something clean,” he said.

“I’ve done something documented,” Lena said.

He gave her a professional smile that admitted nothing. “I will confer with my client.”

“You do that,” she said.

He left without offering his hand. The hallway took him, his coat dark against the painted beadboard, his steps measuring distance like lawyers measure time. The door closed. The rain tapped.

On the laptop, the screen saver didn’t come on. The email stayed open, black text on white, the CC line a fixed string of names that would not unwrite. The two clocks kept arguing gently with each other and would continue to. Outside, somewhere below the window’s melt of glass, a motor coughed, settled, and held.


==================== SCENE 33 ====================
Mid-morning slack made the cove look paused—no pull on the pilings, just a skin of green glass stippled with rain that had fallen and then given up. The dock’s planks were slick and dark. Kelp hung from the ladder like old ribbons. The funicular track on the bluff stood empty, a thin iron line ending in air.

The skiff came in low and clean, a municipal gray with the county crest half-scuffed off the bow. The outboard coughed once to let the dock know and then idled in with that modest authority engines have when they’re sure they’ll be obeyed. The Breakwater deputy stood at the bow post, gloved hand on the painter, brim of his cap wet along the edge. He had a face like a good knife—simple, useful, unornamented. Behind him, a second deputy stayed at the tiller and watched the pilings with a mechanic’s patience.

Lena had them staged already the way she knew he would want it: crates chalked and numbered; the crowbar that crushed her camera sealed in a polypropylene sleeve; the stack of ledgers wrapped in kitchen twine and sand grit; the transfer printouts in a binder protected under a plastic cutting board because the air never really dried anything here. She stood with her clipboard and the flare scar still visible on the boathouse planks. Jonah was on a crate with his hands visible, jacket off, sea salt dried into the quilted seams. Caleb sat with his back to a piling, wrists zip-tied in front, rain puckering his shirt where he’d sweated through and then cooled. Ivy was near the ladder with a garment bag like a flag folded wrong, heels in her hand, stockings rolled down to protect them from splinters, mouth in a legal line that said control, control, control.

The deputy stepped across when the skiff’s bow kissed the rubber. He made no show of jumping—he stepped, as if the dock were a hallway floor and the ocean just wall-to-wall rug. He took in the scene the way he always did, left to right, two beats for each person, one for the pile of things that might later be exhibits. The brim of his cap shed a silver curve of water.

“Morning,” he said. “I’m Deputy Salazar, Breakwater detail. Which one of you is Lena Markham?”

Lena raised her clipboard slightly. “Here.”

“You’re the complainant, and you have chain-of-custody on those?” He indicated the crates with a tilt of the chin.

“Yes,” she said. “Initialed. Photographed. Time-stamped. Recorded on the body cam you lent me, which is here.” She touched the camera, its lens fogged from the warm room inside and the cold air here. “Spare SD cards sealed. One’s a clone.”

“You have a list?”

“Two copies, one for you, one for evidence.”

He nodded. He didn’t write anything yet. He let his gaze find the people again. “And these are the parties involved?”

“Caleb Dunn,” Lena said, indicating the man by the piling. “Jonah Pike.” A hand to the man on the crate. “And Ivy Keene.”

Ivy fixed a smile on him crisp enough to cut. “Counsel for Eleanor Hart,” she said. “Which is to say, you and I need to slow down.”

“We’re already at slack, Ms. Keene,” Salazar said. He didn’t ask how she spelled it. He already knew how to spell things. “You can slow down in a minute. First I’m going to separate everybody, make sure nobody needs medical, and read some forms.”

“I’m fine,” Jonah said quietly. “No medical.”

Caleb’s lip had a line where he’d bitten it. “These are unlawful restraints,” he said, lifting his tied hands a fraction. “You’re looking right at it.”

Salazar looked. “You resisting right now?”

“I’m stating what you should already know.”

“Okay. Thank you for stating.” He took a folded microfiber towel from his pocket, wiped the rail, and set his notebook there like a clean plate. He glanced toward the boathouse where the flare had scorched the planking in a warped crescent. “Who applied the restraints?”

“I did,” Lena said. “After he attempted to move the crate labeled N-5 into the skiff without a bill of lading and after he kicked my evidence bag into the water.” A clear bag sat dripping on the dock with sand and two dead flies clinging to the inside; Lena lifted it with two fingers. “I retrieved it. He knows I retrieved it because he watched.”

“Don’t talk for me,” Caleb said.

“Noted,” Salazar said. He nudged the evidence bag with the butt of his pen. “You still want to keep using your mouth, Mr. Dunn, you can do that after I read you. The way this works is we do things in the order that keeps it from unraveling when it counts.” He glanced at Jonah. “Mr. Pike, you staying put.”

“I’ll go where you put me,” Jonah said. He lifted his hands, palms empty. “I’m not trying to go anywhere.”

Ivy stepped in, the garment bag falling against her shin like a fish against a boat. “Deputy, a hearing this morning upheld my client’s competency, which in turn affects authority over these items. There is no emergency removal, no statutory basis to seize—”

“There’s a basis to not put a forklift on a funicular rated for freight under two hundred pounds,” Salazar said. “There’s a basis to not spoof an emergency line during a known blackout and there’s a basis to not retaliate against a person making a report.” He pointed with his pen, not at her, but at the chalked numbers. “You can assert ownership in a minute. Right now I’m going to control the scene.”

He looked at Lena again. “What’s the status of the judge’s order.”

“Confirmed by email. I printed and attached. Page two.”

He flipped the binder with one finger, used the clean hand. He skimmed. He did not nod; he didn’t like to give away agreement the way other people gave away gum. “Okay,” he said. He looked at the skiff. “Berto, go ahead and set us a drift line on the stern. We’re going to be a while.”

The second deputy eased the line out and snagged the cleat. The skiff settled into its own small patience.

Salazar turned to Caleb. “Mr. Dunn. I’m going to read you your rights while those are still on you. Then I’m going to replace those with mine. You understand?”

Caleb’s jaw worked under a wet lock of hair. “No probable cause.”

“We’ll find out. Stand slow.”

Caleb stood. The zip tie chirped where it rubbed his jacket. Salazar read—clear, mid-Atlantic, the words built into him by repetition. Caleb didn’t answer yes or no to the questions meant to elicit those words. Salazar didn’t push. When he was done, he clipped away the tie in one clean snip and took Caleb’s wrists, turning them with a nurse’s habit around fragile things. He cuffed him low and to the front. He checked circulation by pressing a finger and watching the skin pink back up. He double-locked the ratchets.

“You’re under arrest for attempted removal of property under disputed guardianship, interference with a communications system, and tampering with evidence,” he said. “You’ll get a paper. It’ll read cleaner.”

“Charge the tide while you’re at it,” Caleb said.

“Charges aren’t weather,” Salazar said. “They’re words. Words have rules.” He turned to the second deputy. “Berto, you want Mr. Dunn at bow. Keep him dry if you can.”

Berto guided Caleb toward the skiff without having to touch him much. He didn’t take the man’s elbow in a way that would make it a fight; he stepped in a way that made the boat the only place to go.

Ivy’s smile had vanished. “You will not take custody of those crates,” she said, pointing at N-3 as if the chalk mark could move. “They contain heirloom nursery pieces that—”

“That currently figure into an investigation,” Salazar said. “I’m seizing them lawfully with a receipt issued to the custodian of the premises, copied to counsel of record. You’re on the CC line already; I read it.” He glanced over his shoulder to the hill. “The custodian is?”

“Mara Hart,” Lena said. “She’s at the top of the stairs with the keys.”

“Great. Go get me her signature.”

“I’m not participating in a seizure,” Ivy said.

“You don’t have to,” Salazar said. “You can stand there and say no at me all day. It will make no difference to the paper, which is the part that matters.”

Jonah slid off the crate as if to help, then thought better of it, hands still visible. “You want me where.”

“Where you are is fine,” Salazar said, moving past him to kneel by the nearest crate. He ran his glove along the edge where the lid met the side, feeling for telltales. He found a hair—a human hair—stuck in caught varnish and paused just long enough to let his brain take a picture. Then he took an actual picture with his phone, angling the lens to avoid glare.

“What’s in N-1,” he said.

“Cradle pieces from the nursery,” Lena said. “Paint samples matched to the original receipts.”

“Who packed it.”

“Jonah under Caleb’s instruction.”

“Mr. Pike?” Salazar asked without looking up.

Jonah looked at the water once and back. “Yes,” he said. “That’s right.”

“You have a bill of lading.”

“Caleb has a list,” Jonah said. “Not a bill.”

“Did you sign a 1099 for removal.”

“I don’t know what that is,” Jonah said. It was not bravado. It was a fact that lived in his hands and not in his head.

Salazar stood up. He took a roll of red evidence tape from his coat, snapped a strip free, and sealed the crate across the seam. He wrote with a fine Sharpie, neat as an architect. Date. Time. Initials. Location. N-1. He did N-2 and N-3 the same way, in a rhythm that soothed something in the morning. Rain stitched across his cap brim and off the dock in little hard-luck coins.

“You’re not going to cut into them here,” Ivy said, watching his pen like it might be a blade. “They’re antiques.”

“I’m not going to cut them anywhere,” Salazar said. “I’m going to hold them. Later, in a dry room with a camera running, a table, gloves you can approve of, and everyone invited, somebody else will open them with a screwdriver.” He held up the roll, the end of the tape sticking to his glove. “This is just to keep the future honest.”

He pointed to the crowbar in the bag. “That’s the tool used on a camera in the generator shed.”

“Yes,” Lena said.

“You watch that happen,” Salazar asked.

“I watched the result,” Lena said. “My camera went black after a person walked in with that. The shed cam recorded the angle and a sleeve seam. Then it died. The sleeve seam matches the jacket in the boathouse now.” She pointed to a dark nylon jacket hung over a cleat to drain, its sleeve patched with gray tape. “Jonah will tell you who wore it.”

“Jonah?” Salazar asked, turning his face not unkindly.

Jonah swallowed. “Caleb.”

“Okay,” Salazar said. He made a note as if writing Caleb in that notebook changed nothing and everything. He turned to Jonah, let the quiet between them settle. “Mr. Pike, you’re going to come with me also. You’re not under arrest. You’re being detained for questioning. I’m going to pat you down for weapons. You have anything on you I should know will stab me.”

“No,” Jonah said. He raised his arms away from his sides. “No knives.”

Salazar patted him down, clean. He found a brass fishing weight, a pocket screw, a paper twist of licorice. He set the licorice back in the pocket like a dad would and moved on. He put a light hand on Jonah’s upper arm and left it there only long enough for Jonah to understand it was a guide and not a hook.

Ivy said, “Deputy, while you perform your ritual, understand that any statements my client—”

“Ms. Keene,” Salazar said. “If you’re counsel for Eleanor Hart, you’re not counsel for Mr. Dunn unless he says, and even then I’m not holding my breath. You want to represent Mr. Pike too, I’m going to need to see a letter you wrote yourself, and I’m going to watch you not write it on that dock.”

“You’re discourteous.”

“I’m busy,” he said, without heat. He looked at Lena. “Where’s your phone.”

“In my pocket.”

“You recorded the judge’s assistant’s acknowledgment?”

“Email and call log,” she said. “Screenshots printed. Thumb drive.”

“Good.” He took her copies, slid them into an envelope that said E-1. He printed her name on it. He looked up the stairs. Mara had appeared at the break in the guardrail, key ring on a carabiner the size of a fist. She was breathing like she’d come down the slope at a run.

“Ms. Hart,” he said. “We’re going to do a property receipt. You’ll sign as custodian. These crates will go to the evidence locker in town. I’ll send you the number. You’ll call me if anyone calls you. You’ll take no further action until I tell you where to sit.”

Mara nodded without arguing. “I’ll sign,” she said. She had grease on one thumb that never seemed to fully lift; it left a ghost on the paper next to her name.

Caleb watched the pen shape her signature and made a sound that wasn’t a word. He tested the cuffs and stopped when they reminded him what they were.

“You want to keep glaring, Mr. Dunn,” Salazar said mildly, “keep it up. It won’t go on paper.”

“I’ll put it on mine,” Caleb said. “I’ll file for suppression on half of this and sanctions on the other half.”

“I hope your coffee’s good,” Salazar said. “It might take a while.”

He gestured for Berto, and together they slid the first crate toward the skiff, balancing weight, keeping fingers out of the pinch points. They worked like men who had moved too many heavy things badly once and decided to move them well forever afterward. The boat dipped and then held. The deck took the second crate as neatly. Salazar sealed the bedline with more tape he’d initialed and crossed the strip so the crate and the skiff married under the law.

He turned to Ivy again. “Do you intend to accompany Mr. Dunn.”

“I intend to make sure you don’t hide him,” she said.

“We don’t hide things,” Salazar said. “We log them. And we sit under fluorescent lights that hum like cheap bees. You can follow us in your heels.” He looked at her bare feet. “Or not.”

She slid the heels into the garment bag, zipped its mouth like a wound sutured by a distracted intern. “I’ll be right behind you.”

“Fine,” he said. “No lights, no siren. It’s not that kind of day.”

He took one last slow survey. The burnt scallop on the boards. The ladder slick with kelp. The way Lena’s knuckles had faded from white back to their normal color in the last five minutes. He wrote something else down. He used block capitals this time.

“You have any more footage of the subpanel,” he asked Lena, as if this were just another item you check off before lunch.

“Cass has the transfer files. I mirrored them to you last night. If your inbox does what mine did, check spam.”

“I already checked,” he said, and something about that “already” made the dock feel held.

He looked at Jonah. “You ready.”

Jonah nodded once. “Yes.”

He looked at Caleb. “You ready.”

Caleb didn’t answer.

“That’s fine,” Salazar said. He tilted his head at Berto. “Bow.”

They moved. The skiff took them. The crates settled against the ribs with a domestic sound, as if they were just boxes that had always belonged to boats. Salazar stepped back onto the dock to take the last signatures, to hand Mara the yellow copy and Lena the pink, to give Ivy nothing because she had refused paper.

“You’ll email me,” Ivy said tightly.

“I already did,” he said. “Check spam.”

A gull landed, set two deliberate feet on the tape edge like it understood boundaries. The tide didn’t pull. The pilings didn’t complain. Procedure filled the air the way weather had earlier, and it was better. Salazar nodded to Lena once. It wasn’t a handshake, but it was the dock’s version of one.

“Scene is secure,” he said, into his shoulder mic. “Three detainees: one under arrest, one detained, one counsel. Evidence in transit. Copy.”

“Copy,” came back, small and tinny and sufficient.

He stepped into the skiff. Berto cast off. The line went slack and came taut and then slack again, the simplest conversation in the world. The motor cleared its throat like an old teacher and then spoke. The skiff turned her face toward town. On the dock, Lena watched until the gray of the county seal and the red stripes of tape made a single moving shape and then smaller than that. She looked down. The burn ring held its place on the board, a dark smile that meant exactly what it looked like: heat, and then a line, and then someone in charge of the next thing. She put her palm flat against the wood, felt nothing move beneath it, and began to pick up the wet twine.


==================== SCENE 34 ====================
Late morning put a flat shine on the conference table, the kind that makes fingerprints look like weather maps. Someone had set out two paper cups of coffee and then forgotten them; a skin had formed on one, moonsilver and edged brown. The laptop sat in the middle like a fourth object in a still life—open, camera eye up, little green LED lit. The fan hummed with the anxious steadiness of a bee trapped in a jar.

“Volume’s good,” said Ms. Agnew, the grey-sleeved attorney who had materialized an hour ago with a portfolio and an efficient nod. She didn’t take off her raincoat, just pushed it back at the shoulders. “We’ll keep the speaker on the table. No one leans over the microphone unless asked. Lena, you’re fine where you are.”

Lena had a coil of wet twine in her coat pocket and the smell of tide salt still on her sleeves. Her hair was damp along the neck. She pulled her chair in, adjusted, watched the laptop screen show the empty thumbnail squares reorganize into a single window with a seal and a name.

“Department Twenty,” said the bailiff’s flat voice from somewhere off-screen. “On calendar: In re Matlock, ex parte petition for temporary guardianship—competency disputed. Appearances?”

A square in the upper right flickered into the form of the Judge, shoulders and face lit by some disciplined light. His bench was a set of books and an undecorated wall. No flags. No plants. The piece of him in the frame had the same quality the dock had when Salazar had said, “scene is secure”—a plane that other things hung off of.

“Good morning,” he said. “Counsel, your names for the record.”

“Kersey W. Holland,” Kersey said immediately. He had chosen a navy tie, and the blue on screen made his cheeks look splotched. He sat at the far end of the table like a man putting distance between himself and a campfire he had started. “Petitioner’s counsel.” He glanced at the second chair at his side, empty. The second paper cup of coffee looked like he’d put it there for a client who hadn’t arrived. “Petition seeks a finding of incapacity and appointment of a temporary guardian for Ms. Eleanor Matlock due to immediate risk.”

“Wynne Agnew for Respondent, Ms. Matlock, with Ms. Matlock present by audio,” said the other attorney. She turned the laptop slightly and tapped a key. “Eleanor, can you hear us.”

There was a small pop, and then a clean voice. “I can hear you.” Eleanor’s voice sounded like it came from a warm room with books. There was the slightest click of a distant grandfather clock. “You can hear me.”

“Yes,” the Judge said. He leaned in just enough that the camera interpreted it as interest, not sudden movement. “Ms. Matlock, we’ll get to you. Counsel, I see filings overnight. There’s also a notice of withdrawal from Dr. Vale.”

“Correct,” Agnew said. She slid a sheet of paper from her portfolio and laid it like a tile in front of the laptop. “He emailed at 6:17 a.m. Withdrawing his evaluation. Declines to opine due to compromised conditions at the site yesterday. We’ve marked that as Exhibit R.”

Kersey’s mouth ticked. “Your honor, if I may, Dr. Vale’s withdrawal is preliminary. He’s willing to reexamine. The conditions were the Respondent’s making—”

“No,” Lena said before she could think not to, and then looked to Agnew.

Agnew didn’t look at her; she lifted a palm, not to silence but to steady. “We’ll address that as we establish facts,” she said. “Your honor, Respondent moves to admit Exhibits A through Q and R through U. They include chain-of-custody records from Breakwater County Sheriff for physical evidence seized this morning; financial records demonstrating improper transfers related to the petitioner’s escrow accounts; audio recordings and transcripts of statements made by Ms. Ivy Shaw and Mr. Caleb Dorn; and photographic documentation.”

“Objection,” Kersey said, too fast. “Relevance and foundation as to the financials. This is a competency hearing, not a civil forfeiture raid.”

“It goes to motive and pressure,” Agnew said. “And to whether the petitioner’s claims of ‘immediate risk’ were manufactured to effect removal.”

The Judge’s eyes went to a second screen outside their view. The reflection of a list moved in his lenses; he scrolled. “Breakwater Sheriff’s report timed 09:43. Deputy Salazar. Three detainees in custody. Ms. Shaw, Mr. Dorn, Mr.—Jonah Pike. Chain-of-custody references crates of nursery fixtures, subpanel tampering, and a crowbar.” He looked back at them. “Mr. Holland, you filed in your petition that Respondent’s home presented hazards she is unable to properly address, including faulty electrics and unsecured heavy furniture.”

“That’s right,” Kersey said. “We’re not talking about finances, we’re talking about a woman alone in a deteriorating coastal home with unpredictable blackouts and—”

“It’s not deteriorating,” Eleanor said crisply. “It’s old. There’s a difference.”

“Ms. Matlock,” the Judge said, a hand raised slightly. “I’ll invite your statement shortly.”

The laptop speaker crackled when someone laughed in the next room, and then went silent. Agnew tapped the screen to keep it bright. The gleam of the Judge’s bench held.

“Foundation,” he said. “Ms. Agnew.”

Agnew nodded to Lena. “Ms. Breton, speak to Exhibit H.”

Lena reached for the memory stick sitting on the manila folder like a bright fish. She didn’t plug anything in; the uploads had gone in at dawn, after the skiff’s engine had cut and the deputy had said, “Copy.” But she put her fingers on it to anchor herself. “Exhibit H is a series of photographs and short video clips captured last night from pinhole cameras placed at the generator shed and the bell board. They show Mr. Dorn tripping the subpanel manually and severing the bell wire to produce a false signal, corresponding with the time Ms. Shaw attempted to secure Ms. Matlock’s signature.” She glanced at Kersey. He looked past her, jaw tight. “The time stamps line up with the auto-start of the generator and with the drafted text of the second ‘Oliver’ note, which was in the print buffer.”

“Objection,” Kersey said again. “Hearsay as to the ‘note.’”

“It’s an object,” Agnew said. “Not a statement. Dr. Cassarth’s report is Exhibit F. It shows the note is a transfer copy traced from earlier handwriting. Not original.”

The Judge’s hand lifted. “I have Dr. Cassarth’s CV here. She’s a forensic document examiner, not a hobbyist. Mr. Holland, objection overruled. Exhibit H is admitted. F is admitted.”

Kersey’s throat worked. He glanced at the empty chair again like it might think of sitting. “Your honor, even if there were improprieties by third parties, that doesn’t convert an aged person into a competent one. Ms. Matlock has refused standard evaluations. She isolates, she sees patterns where none exist, she’s living surrounded by—” He caught himself. “By memorabilia. Her refusal to consider care is itself evidence.”

“That is where Dr. Vale would have weighed in,” the Judge said evenly. “But I don’t have Dr. Vale. I have his withdrawal. And I have Ms. Matlock.”

The clock tick in Eleanor’s audio room made a neat notch in the silence.

“Ms. Matlock,” the Judge said. “Would you tell me in your own words your understanding of what is happening and what you want.”

There was the smallest sound, like a chair being pulled closer to a desk, and then Eleanor spoke.

“Ivy is frightened of the house,” she said. “And maybe she’s frightened of herself in it. I’m not. It is, as I said, old. It’s run the same way for a long time. The generator is manual because I chose it to be. The bells were put in when Oliver still believed in them. They are not ghosts. They are wires. Someone cut those wires last night to make me scared.” She breathed like a person who had organized her breath into drawers. “I do not want to leave. I have a will. It names executors. It does not name guardians because I do not require one. I can tell you the date. It is the twelfth. I can tell you what the tide does at noon. I can tell you the names of everyone at that table. Lena. Mr. Holland. Ms. Agnew. You, Judge. I can tell you that Ivy has some cash trouble that she thinks can be hidden if she wraps it in the swaddling of concern. I do not require swaddling.”

Agnew slid another page into view, almost as if she were providing an invisible subtitle for Eleanor’s words. “Exhibit M: ledger pages recovered this morning from the mat in Ms. Shaw’s car, documented with time and date stamps. Notarized by Deputy Salazar. They reflect disbursements from Mr. Holland’s escrow in increments coordinated with auctions of Ms. Matlock’s property. There is also a draft guardianship order in Mr. Holland’s files—Exhibit U—with the respondent’s signature block pre-filled.”

Kersey shook his head. “Those are confidential work product. Your honor, counsel is smearing—”

“Confidentiality doesn’t shield fraud,” Agnew said, still calm.

“I’m not going to litigate ethics in open court,” the Judge said. He didn’t raise his voice; the light did the raising for him. “But I will look at what is in front of me. Mr. Holland, Dr. Vale’s withdrawal speaks to the condition of the evaluation site. Ms. Agnew’s Exhibits H and F, and the Sheriff’s report, suggest those conditions were engineered.” He moved his mouse. “The recordings at 20:12 show a bell ring followed by a man’s hand severing a wire. The petition cites those bell rings as decompensation. It cites a blackout as hazardous neglect. If the blackout was staged, that undermines your claim of neglect.”

“Even if,” Kersey began, and then had to swallow and try again. “Even if Mr. Dorn acted inadvisably, the core remains: Ms. Matlock refuses care and resists evaluation.”

“Last year I broke my wrist cutting apples,” Eleanor said. “I went to the doctor. I let him tie me up like a roast to set the bone. I used the pulleys and the little rubber ball like he told me. I did not accuse him of conspiracy. I went home. I paid the bill. I do not confuse help with taking.”

“Ms. Matlock,” the Judge said, and there was the barest crease by his eye, a human thing that was not a smile exactly. “Do you know the nature and extent of your property.”

“Yes,” she said, like she was being asked if she knew her own name. “The house. The tide rights. The south field. The contents of the nursery, which I have not sold. The cash accounts, which I have not touched. The journals, which remain my property though copies were made by my consent.”

“Do you understand the effect of a guardianship.”

“It would place my decisions in someone else’s hands,” Eleanor said. “It would mean I have been made into a figure I do not recognize. A child, a doll. I do not consent.”

The Judge sat back. Somewhere, the bailiff coughed off-screen. The laptop fan shifted a note and went back to its hum.

“Ms. Agnew,” he said. “Anything further.”

“Yes,” Agnew said. “Ms. Breton came into possession of audio in which Ms. Shaw pressures Ms. Matlock to sign a letter of relinquishment. You can hear the bell ring because Mr. Dorn is outside cutting the line at that moment. Exhibit J. We would ask the court to consider that pressure as the ‘immediate risk’ in this case, not Ms. Matlock’s independent choices. We ask the court to affirm Ms. Matlock’s competency for the limited purposes of resisting guardianship and to invalidate all instruments executed under duress in the last forty-eight hours.”

“I object to the characterization,” Kersey said, but the fight had moved down into his shoulders.

“Objection is noted,” the Judge said. “Deputy Salazar’s presence isn’t required by this hearing, but his report is before me. I see Dr. Vale’s withdrawal. I see the exhibits. And I have heard from Ms. Matlock.”

He didn’t look at any of them when he said the next part. He looked at the space just left of camera, where judges look when they are making sentences into law.

“Findings,” he said. “Number one: The petitioner has not met his burden to show by clear and convincing evidence that the respondent lacks capacity to make decisions regarding her person or estate. Number two: Evidence shows attempts by third parties to manufacture hazardous conditions to justify removal; those attempts undermine the petition’s factual predicates. Number three: Respondent demonstrates understanding of the nature and extent of her property, the consequences of guardianship, and the significance of legal proceedings. Therefore: The petition for temporary guardianship is denied. Any removal executed without consent is invalidated. Any documents signed by the respondent in the last forty-eight hours under pressure from Ms. Shaw or Mr. Dorn are void, pending separate review in the civil calendar. Standing remains with Ms. Matlock. Mr. Holland, if you intend to pursue further, you will do so without ex parte maneuvers and without the services of Dr. Vale unless and until he elects to reengage under proper conditions.”

Eleanor’s breath came across the speaker like a light fabric moving. “Thank you,” she said simply.

“Ms. Matlock,” the Judge said, “in a moment my clerk will send written orders. If you choose a voluntary evaluation for your own records or to forestall future petitions, you may select a practitioner, not appointed by any party here, and schedule it on your terms. That is not a court order; that is practical advice.”

“I understand,” she said. “I might prefer someone who likes bell wires.”

The Judge’s mouth did something that might admit the concept of humor. “We are adjourned.”

The court seal went to a blank blue. The hum of the fan suddenly felt like a small animal released back to itself. For a beat, no one moved.

Kersey pushed his chair back an inch. The plastic on the floor made a sound like cold. He put his hands on the table, then took them off, then reached for the forgotten coffee, thought better, and stood. “This isn’t over,” he said to the air, to the empty blue, to the lines between the wood grain.

“It is for today,” Agnew said. She stood too, smoothing her sleeve with a motion that collected everything and put it into order. “The deputy will email you the property release schedule for items seized as evidence that are not contraband. Don’t call Ms. Shaw.”

Lena exhaled. The twine in her pocket had warmed to her. She looked at the laptop. “Eleanor,” she said. “You’re still on.”

“I’m here,” Eleanor said. The clock ticked. “The house is very quiet.”

“I’ll be back as soon as the deputy finishes with the dock,” Lena said. “We’ll get the crate list reconciled. We’ll bring in the south shutters before the afternoon wind.”

“You’ll eat,” Eleanor said. “There’s soup in the blue pot.”

Lena smiled before remembering anyone could see her through the camera. “Yes.”

“Mr. Holland,” Eleanor said then, and his head came up as if someone had picked his name out of a hat. “I am sorry if Ivy involved you in something you have to climb out of. Please do not come to my house again.”

Kersey opened his mouth. Closed it. He took the navy tie and tugged it a quarter inch looser, a concession that didn’t amount to surrender. “I’ll be in touch through counsel,” he said, and he left without looking at anyone.

When the door closed, the table got bigger. Agnew saved the recording to a drive with a methodical sequence—click, click, name, date. She labeled the manila folders. She clicked an email back to the clerk countersigning the orders. She did everything like a person who understood that each small act is a bolt that keeps a structure from shaking itself apart.

“You did well,” she said to Lena without looking up.

Lena rolled the twine in her palm. “She did well,” she said.

“She did,” Agnew agreed. “But so did you.”

On the laptop, the blue gave way to a soft screensaver of default clouds. Eleanor’s audio still lived in the small corner box, the clock a calm metronome. “Is there anything you need from me before you come,” Eleanor asked.

“Just keep the line open,” Lena said. She picked up one of the paper cups and threw it away without even peeking under the skin. “We’re on our way.”
